The Three Essential Properties of Quantity
Author enumerates the three essential properties of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential properties of quantity and explain how equality is defined using the concept of limit correspondence.

Core Idea
Quantity inherently has parts, is estimable, and can be equal or unequal across limits.

The category of quantity is foundational to many Islamic sciences, from inheritance calculations to theological proofs. In this passage, the author enumerates the essential properties that define quantity: having parts, admitting estimation, and admitting equality. Understanding these properties helps distinguish quantity from other categories like quality, which has opposite and intensity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three essential properties
Toward the end of the passage, the author lists three genuine properties of quantity. Scan the Arabic text for the phrase 'ثلاث خواص حقيقية' and extract each one.
The properties are: having parts (لها جزء), admitting estimation (تحتمل التقدير), and admitting equality (تقبل المساواة).
2
Understand the definition of equality
The passage defines equality as the state when applying continuous or discrete units upon one another and finding no difference in limits. This applies only to quantities.
Motion and change do not have this property because they lack such application.
3
Contrast with motion
The author specifically denies that motion has equality or inequality because it does not have the requisite application of units.
This reinforces that motion is not a quantity, despite sometimes being considered in terms of time.
4
Apply the tool of equality to a fiqh example
Consider inheritance shares: they must be equal as quantities. Use the definition to check if the shares are truly equal by applying the units (e.g., dinars) and seeing if limits match.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون كبيرا وصغيرا بالقياس إلى شيئين. والكبير عند من يجعله ضدا ليس ضد الكل ما يعرض صغيرًا بل لما هو بالقياس إلى هذا الكبير مع كبره الذي هو بالقياس إليه كبير . ومن زعم أن المكان من الكم والأسفل منه ضد الأعلى فزعمه باطل؛ إذ المكان لا يضاد المكان من حيث ذاته الذي هو السطح. وكيف وإنهما لا يتعاقبان على موضوع واحد .البتة والمكان من حيث هو المكان ليس بأعلى ولا بأسفل بل من حيث هو نهاية حركة وطرف مسافة أو من حيث هو طرف في جسم حاو؛ وهذه عوارض الكمية فلا يجعله بحيث يشترك الأعلى والأسفل منه في موضوع واحد ولا يلزم من غاية البعد بين الشيئين أن يكونا متضادين وإن كان التضاد يتضمن هذا الشرط ولأن المفهوم من لفظ التضاد في اصطلاح أهل المنطق هو الحالة التي بين ذاتين مشتركتين في موضوع شركة التعاقب ولا وجود لهذا المجموع في المكانين. ثم العلو والسفل قد يعتبر من طريق الإضافة وذلك من جملة ما يمنع التضاد كما في الصغير والكبير. وأما الشدة والضعف فلا يعني بهذا أن كمية لا تكون أزيد من كمية وأنقص بل يعنى أن كمية لا يكون كمية أشد وأزيد في أنها كمية من أخرى نوعًا كان كل واحد منهما أو شخصا أو لا نوعا ولا شخصا. فلا ثلاثة أشد ثلاثية من ثلاثة وأربعة أشد رباعية من أربعة. ولا خط أشد خطية من خط آخر أيضًا وإلا لا يجمع الخطين المختلفين حد واحد وإن كان من حيث المعنى الإضافي أزيد منه بل يجوز أن تكون كمية أزيد / وأشد في طبيعتها من كمية أخرى متصلة كانت أو منفصلة، فالثلاثة في كونها عددًا ليست بأكبر من أربع. والحد الذي مرَّ ذكره للعدد يشملهما بالضرورة: نعم قد يصير أزيد مما يعرض لها من الإضافات المختلفة بينهما. والفرق بين هذا الأزيد وبين الأزيد الذي يمتنع كونها في الكمية أن هذا الأزيد يمكن أن يشار فيه إلى مثل حاصل وزيادة؛ والأزيد الذي ممتنع لا يمكن فيه ذلك. وتفاوت الأشد والأضعف ينحصر بين طرفين ضدين، وتفاوت الأزيد والأنقص لا ينحصر البتة بين طرفين. ومن خواص الكمية أنها بذاتها مساوية أو غير مساوية، والمساواة هي الحالة التي تكون عند توهمك بتطبيق المتصل وآحاد المنفصل بعضها على بعض. فلا يوجد أحدهما يحصل عند حد لم يحصل الآخر عند ذلك الحد على خلاف غير المساواة؛ فالمطابقة التي لا توجد فيها اختلاف للحدود يسمى مساواة. وأنت تعلم أن النقل والحركة إذا اعتبرا بذاتهما من غير التفاوت إلى مقادير خارجة عنهما لا يوجد فيهما هذا التطبيق فليست فيهما المساواة واللا مساواة إذن؛ فالكمية لها ثلاث خواص حقيقية وهي أنها لذاتها لها جزء ولذاتها تحتمل التقدير ولذاتها تقبل المساواة وإتيان إضافتين، وهما: أنها لا تقبل بذاتها مضادة، وأنها لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف.
It is big and small in relation to two things. The big according to one who makes it an opposite is not opposite to the whole of what appears small, but rather to what is in relation to this big with its bigness that is big in relation to it. And whoever claims that place is of quantity and the lower part is opposite to the upper, his claim is false; because place does not oppose place in its essence which is surface. Further, they do not alternate on one subject at all. And place inasmuch as it is place is neither upper nor lower, but rather inasmuch as it is a limit of motion and extremity of distance, or inasmuch as it is a limit in a containing body; these are accidents of quantity. So he does not make it such that the upper and lower share in one subject, nor does extreme distance between two things necessitate that they be opposites, even though opposition includes this condition. And because the concept of the term 'opposition' in the terminology of the logicians is the state between two essences sharing a subject by way of alternation, and this totality does not exist in the two places. Then upper and lower may be considered by way of relation, and that is among the things that prevent opposition, as in small and large. As for intensity and weakness, it is not meant by this that a quantity is not more than a quantity and less, but rather that a quantity is not more intense and more as a quantity than another, whether each is a species or an individual or neither a species nor an individual. So three is not more threeness than three, and four is not more fourness than four. Nor is a line more line-ness than another line; otherwise, two different lines would not share one limit, even if from a relative sense it is more. Rather, it is possible that a quantity is more and more intense in its nature than another quantity, whether continuous or discrete. So three, as a number, is not greater than four. And the definition mentioned for number necessarily includes both. Yes, it may become more due to the different relations that occur to them. The difference between this 'more' and the 'more' that is impossible in quantity is that this 'more' can be pointed to as having a resultant and increase; the impossible 'more' cannot have that. The variation of intensity and weakness is confined between two opposite extremes; the variation of more and less is not at all confined between two extremes. Among the properties of quantity is that it is in itself equal or unequal. Equality is the state when you imagine applying the continuous and the units of the discrete upon one another. So neither of them is attained at a limit which the other is not attained at that limit, contrary to inequality. So the correspondence in which there is no difference of limits is called equality. You know that motion and change, if considered in themselves without variation to external measures, do not have this application; thus there is no equality or inequality in them. So quantity has three real properties: that in itself it has parts, in itself it admits estimation, and in itself it admits equality, and two additional [properties]: that in itself it does not admit opposition, and that it does not admit intensity and weakness.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the category of quantity (al-kammiyya). It begins by addressing the notion of big and small as relational, not inherent opposites. The author refutes the idea that place as quantity has opposite directions, since place as a surface does not have opposites. He explains that opposition requires two essences sharing a subject by alternation, which does not apply to places. Then he discusses intensity and weakness in quantity: a quantity is not more intense in its quiddity than another of the same species; e.g., three is not more three-ness than three, and lines are not more line-ness. However, quantity can be more in a relational sense, but that is external. The variation of more and less is unlimited, unlike intensity which has extremes. Then the author lists the properties peculiar to quantity: it is inherently equal or unequal, equality defined by correspondence of limits. Motion and change lack this, so they are not quantities. Finally, the three essential properties: having parts, admitting estimation, and admitting equality; plus two negatives: no opposition and no intensity. This is an expansion of the doctrine of the categories.

Key Terms
الكمية
المساواة
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three real properties of quantity?
A) Having parts
B) Admitting estimation
C) Admitting opposition
D) Admitting equality

Key Takeaway
Quantity is defined by its capacity to have parts, be measured, and be compared as equal or unequal across limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept of 'time' in Islamic jurisprudence, e.g., the duration of 'iddah (waiting period). Determine whether time has the three essential properties of quantity (parts, estimation, equality). Justify your answer by referring to the definition of equality given in the lesson.
?
In a kalam argument, the mutakallimun often use the property that quantity admits estimation to prove that the world is temporally originated. Explain how this property supports their argument.

Why Quantity Does Not Admit Opposition or Intensity
Author refutes false views about quantity having opposition and intensity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why quantity lacks opposition and intensity, using the refutation of place and the example of numbers.

Core Idea
Quantity has no opposite and no degrees of intensity—three is not more three than three.

In this passage, the author defends the unique status of quantity against misconceptions. Some think that place has opposite directions, or that numbers can be more or less intense. The author refutes these views by defining opposition and intensity carefully. Understanding these negative properties prevents category errors and clarifies the boundaries of the category of quantity in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of opposition
The passage defines opposition as a state between two essences that share a subject by way of alternation. This is key to evaluating claims of opposition.
Alternation means one replaces the other in the same subject.
2
Analyse the refutation of place having opposite directions
The author argues that place as a surface does not alternate on a subject, so it cannot have opposites. Upper and lower are relational, not essential.
Even if two places are far apart, that does not make them opposites.
3
Understand why quantity lacks intensity
Intensity requires extremes; quantity's more/less is unlimited. Also, a specific quantity is not more intense in its quiddity: three is not more three-ness than three.
Relational increase (e.g., three is more than two) is different from intrinsic intensity.
4
Apply the distinction to avoid category mistakes
In theology, when discussing divine attributes, one must not treat quantities as if they have opposites (e.g., saying God's power is opposite to something). This lesson provides the logical tools to avoid such errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون كبيرا وصغيرا بالقياس إلى شيئين. والكبير عند من يجعله ضدا ليس ضد الكل ما يعرض صغيرًا بل لما هو بالقياس إلى هذا الكبير مع كبره الذي هو بالقياس إليه كبير . ومن زعم أن المكان من الكم والأسفل منه ضد الأعلى فزعمه باطل؛ إذ المكان لا يضاد المكان من حيث ذاته الذي هو السطح. وكيف وإنهما لا يتعاقبان على موضوع واحد .البتة والمكان من حيث هو المكان ليس بأعلى ولا بأسفل بل من حيث هو نهاية حركة وطرف مسافة أو من حيث هو طرف في جسم حاو؛ وهذه عوارض الكمية فلا يجعله بحيث يشترك الأعلى والأسفل منه في موضوع واحد ولا يلزم من غاية البعد بين الشيئين أن يكونا متضادين وإن كان التضاد يتضمن هذا الشرط ولأن المفهوم من لفظ الصغير والكبير. وأما الشدة والضعف فلا يعني بهذا أن كمية لا تكون أزيد من كمية وأنقص بل يعنى أن كمية لا يكون كمية أشد وأزيد في أنها كمية من أخرى نوعًا كان كل واحد منهما أو شخصا أو لا نوعا ولا شخصا. فلا طبيعتها من كمية أخرى متصلة كانت أو منفصلة، فالثلاثة في كونها عددًا ليست بأكبر من أربع. والحد الذي مرَّ ذكره للعدد يشملهما بالضرورة: نعم قد يصير أزيد مما يعرض لها من الإضافات المختلفة بينهما. والفرق بين هذا الأزيد وبين الأزيد الذي يمتنع كونها في الكمية أن هذا الأزيد يمكن أن يشار فيه إلى مثل حاصل وزيادة؛ والأزيد الذي ممتنع لا يمكن فيه ذلك. وتفاوت الأشد والأضعف ينحصر بين طرفين ضدين، وتفاوت الأزيد والأنقص لا ينحصر البتة بين طرفين. ومن خواص الكمية أنها بذاتها مساوية أو غير مساوية، والمساواة هي الحالة التي تكون عند توهمك بتطبيق المتصل وآحاد المنفصل بعضها على بعض. فلا يوجد أحدهما يحصل عند حد لم يحصل الآخر عند ذلك الحد على خلاف غير المساواة؛ فالمطابقة التي لا توجد فيها اختلاف للحدود يسمى مساواة. وأنت تعلم أن النقل والحركة إذا اعتبرا بذاتهما من غير التفاوت إلى مقادير خارجة عنهما لا يوجد فيهما وأنها لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف.
It is big and small in relation to two things. The big according to one who makes it an opposite is not opposite to the whole of what appears small, but rather to what is in relation to this big with its bigness that is big in relation to it. And whoever claims that place is of quantity and the lower part is opposite to the upper, his claim is false; because place does not oppose place in its essence which is surface. Further, they do not alternate on one subject at all. And place inasmuch as it is place is neither upper nor lower, but rather inasmuch as it is a limit of motion and extremity of distance, or inasmuch as it is a limit in a containing body; these are accidents of quantity. So he does not make it such that the upper and lower share in one subject, nor does extreme distance between two things necessitate that they be opposites, even though opposition includes this condition. And because the concept of the term 'opposition' in the terminology of the logicians is the state between two essences sharing a subject by way of alternation, and this totality does not exist in the two places. Then upper and lower may be considered by way of relation, and that is among the things that prevent opposition, as in small and large. As for intensity and weakness, it is not meant by this that a quantity is not more than a quantity and less, but rather that a quantity is not more intense and more as a quantity than another, whether each is a species or an individual or neither a species nor an individual. So three is not more threeness than three, and four is not more fourness than four. Nor is a line more line-ness than another line; otherwise, two different lines would not share one limit, even if from a relative sense it is more. Rather, it is possible that a quantity is more and more intense in its nature than another quantity, whether continuous or discrete. So three, as a number, is not greater than four. And the definition mentioned for number necessarily includes both. Yes, it may become more due to the different relations that occur to them. The difference between this 'more' and the 'more' that is impossible in quantity is that this 'more' can be pointed to as having a resultant and increase; the impossible 'more' cannot have that. The variation of intensity and weakness is confined between two opposite extremes; the variation of more and less is not at all confined between two extremes. Among the properties of quantity is that it is in itself equal or unequal. Equality is the state when you imagine applying the continuous and the units of the discrete upon one another. So neither of them is attained at a limit which the other is not attained at that limit, contrary to inequality. So the correspondence in which there is no difference of limits is called equality. You know that motion and change, if considered in themselves without variation to external measures, do not have this application; thus there is no equality or inequality in them. So quantity has three real properties: that in itself it has parts, in itself it admits estimation, and in itself it admits equality, and two additional [properties]: that in itself it does not admit opposition, and that it does not admit intensity and weakness.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the category of quantity (al-kammiyya). It begins by addressing the notion of big and small as relational, not inherent opposites. The author refutes the idea that place as quantity has opposite directions, since place as a surface does not have opposites. He explains that opposition requires two essences sharing a subject by alternation, which does not apply to places. Then he discusses intensity and weakness in quantity: a quantity is not more intense in its quiddity than another of the same species; e.g., three is not more three-ness than three, and lines are not more line-ness. However, quantity can be more in a relational sense, but that is external. The variation of more and less is unlimited, unlike intensity which has extremes. Then the author lists the properties peculiar to quantity: it is inherently equal or unequal, equality defined by correspondence of limits. Motion and change lack this, so they are not quantities. Finally, the three essential properties: having parts, admitting estimation, and admitting equality; plus two negatives: no opposition and no intensity. This is an expansion of the doctrine of the categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defining Quality: Why a Common Definition Fails
Author presents a definition of quality and critiques its inadequacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a common definition of quality (al-kayfiyya) is too broad and apply the criterion that a definition must uniquely capture its definiendum.

Core Idea
A definition of quality that also fits other categories is no definition at all.

In this passage, the author examines a widely-used definition of quality: 'that by which one answers how a person is.' At first glance, this seems plausible. However, the author reveals its fault: the same question can be answered with categories like position or place. Thus, the definition fails to distinguish quality from other categories. Let's dissect why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the proposed definition
Find the term being defined (al-kayfiyya) and the definition presented: 'quality is that by which it is valid to say of a person how he is.'
2
Analyze the definition's scope
Consider the question 'how is he?' What kinds of answers can this question receive? The author notes it can be answered by sitting, standing, being in a place, etc.
3
Test the definition against other categories
Check if the definition applies to categories other than quality. The author points out that answers like 'sitting' belong to the category of position (wadʿ), and 'in a place' belongs to the category of where (ayn). Thus the definition includes these categories.
Also includes action and passion categories.
4
Conclude the definition's failure
Since the definition applies to multiple categories, it is too broad. The author calls it corrupt (fasad) because it does not uniquely identify quality.
A proper definition must be coextensive with the definiendum.
5
Extract the lesson for definitions
This case teaches that a definition must be neither too broad nor too narrow. In Islamic logic, this is a key criterion for evaluating definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكيفية فإنها من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة أيضًا : منها ما يقال إن الكيفية ما به يصح أن يقال على الشخص أنه كيف هو ؛ وفيه من الفساد ما فيه فإنه لا يتصور منه الكيفية الداخلة في المقولة لما أن السؤال به والجواب عنه أمر غير محصل. فإن من الجمهور أن يتوقفوا في أن يجاب بأنه قاعد أو قائم؛ وهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الواقع في مقولة الوضع أو يجاب بأنه في مكان كذا أو فوق كذا؛ وهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الواقع في مقولة الأين. وعلى هذا يقال في الجواب إنه فاعل أو منفعل وكذا وكذا، فلولا الكيفية أمر عام يعم غيرها من المقولات ما عدا الجوهر كلا أو بعضًا عموم الجنس لكل
As for quality, it is among the things that are known by various definitions as well. Among them is what is said: that quality is that by which it is valid to say of a person 'how he is'. And in it is corruption enough, for the quality included in the category cannot be conceived from it, because the question and answer about it are not precise. For the common people may hesitate to answer that he is sitting or standing; this is the answer to the question in the category of position (wadʿ), or to answer that he is in such-and-such a place or above such-and-such; this is the answer to the question in the category of where (ayn). And similarly, one answers that he is acting or being acted upon, etc. Thus, if quality were not a general thing encompassing other categories besides substance, in whole or in part, with the generality of a genus for each...
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage introduces the category of quality (al-kayfiyya). The author notes that there are various definitions, and presents one common definition: quality is that by which one can answer the question 'how is it?' regarding a person. The author immediately critiques this definition, stating it is corrupt because it does not properly capture the quality that is within the category. The reason is that the question 'how is it?' can be answered by referring to other categories, such as position (wadʿ) like sitting or standing, or place (ayn) like being in a certain location, or even action and passion. Thus, the definition is too broad; it would include other accidental categories besides quality. The author implies that a proper definition should be specific to quality alone.

Key Terms
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya): The category of quality, but the author criticizes a common definition as too broad.
المقولة (al-maqūla): A category, one of the ten Aristotelian categories.
الوضع (al-wadʿ): The category of position (e.g., sitting, standing).
الأين (al-ayn): The category of where (place).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition 'quality is that by which one answers how is it?'
A) It is too narrow
B) It applies to other categories
C) It is too vague
D) It is circular

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be coextensive with the definiendum; if it applies to other categories, it is corrupted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Water is the liquid that quenches thirst.' Test this definition against other liquids that quench thirst (e.g., milk). Is it a valid definition? Explain using the author's critique that a definition should be unique to the thing defined.

Properties of Quantity: Part, Divisibility, Equality, and Non-Opposition
Author lists the three essential properties of quantity and two additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential properties of quantity and the two additional attributes that distinguish it from other categories.

Core Idea
Quantity has parts, admits division, and can be equal—but never opposite or intense.

In this lesson, we examine the category of quantity (al-kammiyya) in Islamic logic. The author outlines three real properties that are intrinsic to quantity and two additional attributes that further characterize it. Understanding these properties helps us reason about measurements in Islamic law and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the category
Identify quantity (al-kammiyya) as one of Aristotle's categories. The passage discusses its properties.
2
Extract the three real properties
Find where the author states that quantity has a part (juz'), admits division (taqdīr), and accepts equality (musāwāh) by itself.
These are the essential, inseparable characteristics.
3
Identify the two additional attributes
Note the extra attributes: quantity does not admit opposition (mudāddah) nor intensity/weakness (ashadd wa ad'af).
Contrast with quality, which can be more or less intense.
4
Understand the definition of equality
The author defines equality as the correspondence of limits when mentally applying continuous to discrete units. This is a key logical tool.
Motion and change lack equality by themselves.
5
Apply the properties to Islamic examples
For instance, in fiqh, the quantity of land for zakah is measured in wasqs; equality of measures is crucial. Also, quantity cannot be opposite like 'hot' and 'cold'.
This distinguishes quantity from other categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه إلى مثل حاصل وزيادة؛ والأزيد الذي ممتنع لا يمكن فيه ذلك. وتفاوت الأشد والأضعف ينحصر بين طرفين ضدين، وتفاوت الأزيد والأنقص لا ينحصر البتة بين طرفين. ومن خواص الكمية أنها بذاتها مساوية أو غير مساوية، والمساواة هي الحالة التي تكون عند توهمك بتطبيق المتصل وآحاد المنفصل بعضها على بعض. فلا يوجد أحدهما يحصل عند حد لم يحصل الآخر عند ذلك الحد على خلاف غير المساواة؛ فالمطابقة التي لا توجد فيها اختلاف للحدود يسمى مساواة. وأنت تعلم أن النقل والحركة إذا اعتبرا بذاتهما من غير التفاوت إلى مقادير خارجة عنهما لا يوجد فيهما هذا التطبيق فليست فيهما المساواة واللا مساواة إذن؛ فالكمية لها ثلاث خواص حقيقية وهي أنها لذاتها لها جزء ولذاتها تحتمل التقدير ولذاتها تقبل المساواة وإتيان إضافتين، وهما: أنها لا تقبل بذاتها مضادة، وأنها لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف.
In it to like obtained and increase; the increased which is impossible cannot have that. The disparity of stronger and weaker is confined between two opposite extremes, but the disparity of more and less is not at all confined between two extremes. Among the properties of quantity is that it is by itself equal or unequal. Equality is the state that occurs when you imagine applying the continuous [quantity] and the units of the discrete [quantity] to each other. So one of them does not exist when a limit is reached without the other reaching that limit—contrary to inequality. The correspondence that has no difference of limits is called equality. You know that motion and change, if considered by themselves without reference to external measures, do not involve this application, so equality and inequality do not exist in them. Thus quantity has three real properties: it has a part by itself, it admits division by itself, and it accepts equality by itself; and it brings two additional [properties]: that it does not by itself admit opposition, and that it does not admit intensity and weakness.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the properties of quantity (al-kammiyya). The author begins by contrasting two types of variation: the variation of stronger and weaker (al-ashadd wal-ad'af) which is bounded between two opposite extremes, and the variation of more and less (al-azid wal-anqas) which is not bounded. Then he states a specific property of quantity: it is by itself either equal or unequal. He defines equality as the state where when you mentally apply the continuous quantity to the discrete units, the limits correspond without difference. He then notes that motion and change, considered intrinsically without external measures, do not have such application, so equality and inequality do not apply to them. The author then summarizes that quantity has three real essential properties: (1) it has a part by itself, (2) it admits division by itself, (3) it accepts equality by itself. Additionally, it has two extra properties: it does not by itself admit opposition (i.e., it cannot be directly opposite like hot and cold), and it does not admit intensity or weakness (e.g., one quantity cannot be 'more quantity' than another in the sense of stronger/weaker, only more or less in magnitude). These properties distinguish quantity from other categories. The commentary should explain each point, using examples from Islamic sciences: e.g., quantity of length in a prayer rug (masaha) can be equal or unequal, but the rug itself is not 'more' in a qualitative sense.

Key Terms
الكمية
المساواة
الأشد والأضعف
الأزيد والأنقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT a real property of quantity?
A) Having a part by itself
B) Admitting division by itself
C) Accepting equality by itself
D) Admitting opposition by itself

Key Takeaway
Quantity has three essential properties (part, division, equality) and two additional attributes (no opposition, no intensity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition of equality from the passage, determine whether two quantities of water in two different containers can be considered equal if their surfaces align when the containers are of different shapes.
?
Identify which of the following is a property of quantity: having an opposite, having more and less, having stronger and weaker.

Evaluating Definitions of Quality: From Flawed Attempts to a Sound Definition
Author critiques four definitions of quality and affirms one correct definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique flawed definitions of a logical category and identify the key conditions for a sound definition using genus and differentia.

Core Idea
A good definition excludes everything it should and includes everything it should — quality's sound definition does both.

Quality (al-kayfiyya) is one of the ten categories, and logicians have offered various definitions. This passage examines four flawed definitions, revealing common errors like circularity and metaphor, and then presents a precise definition that uses genus (accident) and differentiae to distinguish quality from all other categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four definitions
List the four definitions of quality that the author examines: (1) 'that by which one says how something is', (2) 'that by which likeness and unlikeness occur', (3) 'that which creates a mark in substance', (4) 'an accident whose conception does not depend on another and does not primarily require division'.
The fourth is the accepted one; the first three are rejected.
2
Pinpoint the logical error in each rejected definition
For each rejected definition, identify the specific flaw: (1) leads to confusion with other categories; (2) circular or ambiguous; (3) metaphorical and unclear.
These errors violate the requirement of clarity and non-circularity.
3
Explain why the accepted definition is sound
Break down the accepted definition into its components: 'accident' distinguishes from substance; 'conception does not depend on another' distinguishes from relation; 'does not primarily require division' distinguishes from quantity, unity, point; 'primarily' distinguishes from knowledge of indivisible things.
Each component acts as a differentia to exclude everything that is not quality.
4
Verify the definition's completeness
Check that the definition includes all instances of quality (e.g., colors, shapes, states) and excludes non-qualities (substance, relation, quantity, etc.). Confirm with examples.
The definition covers all four species of quality (disposition, capacity, affective qualities, shapes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكيفية وأما الكيفية فإنها من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة أيضًا : منها ما يقال إن الكيفية ما به يصح أن يقال على الشخص أنه كيف هو ؛ وفيه من الفساد ما فيه فإنه لا يتصور منه الكيفية الداخلة في المقولة لما أن السؤال به والجواب عنه أمر غير محصل. فإن من الجمهور أن يتوقفوا في أن يجاب بأنه قاعد أو قائم؛ وهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الواقع في مقولة الوضع أو يجاب بأنه في مكان كذا أو فوق كذا؛ وهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الواقع في مقولة الأين. وعلى هذا يقال في الجواب إنه فاعل أو منفعل وكذا وكذا، فلولا الكيفية أمر عام يعم غيرها من المقولات ما عدا الجوهر كلا أو بعضًا عموم الجنس لكل نوع منه كما ذهب إليه من السلف لما كانت الأجوبة موافقة: وفيه من النظر؛ إذ المقولات عند الجمهور مشابهة ولا عمومية لبعضها بالنسبة إلى البعض ولا خصوصية كذلك. ولأن الكيف قد يذكر ويراد به الكيفية؛ وقد يذكر ويراد به ما له الكيفية. ولو كان كذلك فذلك أمر غير محصل ولا يمكن أن يقال (۱) المراد منه هذا على التعيين أو ذلك، والفساد في الكل ظاهر. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية ما به تقع المشابهة بين الأشياء واللا مشابهة؛ وفيه [٦] من الفساد أيضًا فإن لفظة المشابهة إما أن يراد بها الموافقة في الكيفية / وإنه يفضي إلى الدور، وإما أن يراد بها الغير؛ وذلك لا تدرى حاله بأنه يصح أو لا يصح. وإن صح فإما أن يصح بطريق الحقيقة أو بطريق المجاز؛ والفساد في الكل ظاهر. ولا يقال لا فساد فيه إذا كان المراد منها الغير بطريق الحقيقة؛ فإن الفساد فيه ظاهر وإنه في حيز المنع فلا يمكن أن يراد بطريق الحقيقة عند الجمهور من العلماء؛ خصوصا عند أهل المنطق. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية هي التي تُحدِثُ رسما في الجوهر؛ وفيه من الفساد أيضًا فإن لفظ الرسم يشبه أن يكون استعماله بطريق المجاز وحينئذ لا يتحقق معنى. وإن تحقق فلا يكون بحسب التعارف فيكون هذا اللفظ أشد بعدا عن البيان من لفظة الكيفية. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية عرض لا يتوقف تصوره على تصور غيره ولا يقتضي القسمة واللَّا قسمة في المحل اقتضاء أوليا؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا فساد فيه. فبالعرض يتميز عن الجوهر وبالذي لا يتوقف عن المضاف وبالذي لا يقتضي عن الكم والوحدة والنقطة، ويقوله اقتضاء أوليا عن العلم بالمعلومات التي لا تنقسم. فإن علم الانقسام لا يكون لذاتها بل بواسطة الوحدة والمفهوم منها أن يقال إنها عبارة عن هيئة قارة لا يوجب تصورها تصور شيء خارج
Section on quality. As for quality, it is among things known by various definitions as well. Among them is that it is said: quality is that by which it is correct to say of a person 'how he is.' There is corruption in it, for it does not conceive the quality entering into the category, because the question and answer about it are unattained. For among the commoners they hesitate to answer 'he is sitting' or 'standing'; and this is the answer to the question occurring in the category of position, or they answer 'he is in such a place' or 'above such'; and this is the answer to the question occurring in the category of location. And likewise, it is said in answer 'he is an agent' or 'patient' and so on. So if quality were not a general matter encompassing other categories besides substance, either wholly or partially, with a generality of genus for each of its species, as the early scholars held, then the answers would not be congruent. There is critique in that; for the categories according to the commoners are similar, with no generality of some over others and no particularity likewise. And because 'how' (kayf) is sometimes mentioned meaning quality, and sometimes mentioned meaning that which has quality. If it were like that, then that is unattained, nor can it be said that its intended meaning is specifically this or that; corruption in all is apparent. Among them is what is said: quality is that by which likeness and unlikeness occur between things. There is also corruption in it, for the word 'likeness' either means agreement in quality—and that leads to circularity—or it means something else, whose state is unknown as to whether it is correct or not. And if correct, either it is correct by way of reality or by way of metaphor; corruption in all is apparent. Nor is it said that there is no corruption in it if what is meant is something else by way of reality, for the corruption in it is apparent, and it is in the realm of denial; it cannot be intended by way of reality according to the majority of scholars, especially among the logicians. Among them is what is said: quality is that which creates a mark (rasm) in the substance. There is also corruption in it, for the word 'mark' seems to be used metaphorically; and then no meaning is realized. And if it is realized, it would not be according to customary usage, so this word is further from clarity than the word 'quality'. Among them is what is said: quality is an accident whose conception does not depend on the conception of another, and does not require division and non-division in the subject primarily. This is among those in which there is no corruption. So by 'accident' it is distinguished from substance, and by 'whose conception does not depend' [it is distinguished] from relation, and by 'does not require' [it is distinguished] from quantity, unity, and point; and its statement 'primarily' [distinguishes it] from knowledge of indivisible things. For knowledge of division does not belong to itself but through unity. And the concept from it is that it is said that it is an expression of a stable state (hay'a qārra) whose conception does not entail the conception of anything external.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of various definitions of quality (al-kayfiyya), one of the ten Aristotelian categories. The author, likely the commentator or super-commentator, proceeds by enumerating four definitions, pointing out the flaws in each, and then offering a sound definition. The first definition 'quality is that by which it is correct to say of a person how he is' is rejected because it leads to questions about other categories (e.g., position, location) and does not uniquely identify quality. The second definition 'quality is that by which likeness and unlikeness occur' is criticized for potential circularity or ambiguity. The third definition 'quality is that which creates a mark (rasm) in the substance' is rejected because 'mark' is metaphorical and unclear. Finally, the author presents a definition that he considers free of corruption: 'quality is an accident whose conception does not depend on the conception of another and does not primarily require division or non-division in the subject.' He then explains how each part of this definition distinguishes quality from other categories: 'accident' distinguishes it from substance; 'conception does not depend on another' distinguishes it from relation (al-mudāf); 'does not require division' distinguishes it from quantity, unity, and point; and 'primarily' distinguishes it from knowledge of indivisible things, such as the knowledge of division, which though indivisible itself, its conception involves division indirectly. Thus, the author refines the definition to be precise and non-circular, avoiding the pitfalls of earlier definitions. This is typical of logical works that seek to establish clear definitions for each category.

Key Terms
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya): quality
المشابهة (al-mushābaha): likeness
الرسم (al-rasm): mark
العرض (al-ʿarad): accident
الهيئة القارة (al-hayʾa al-qārra): stable state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition 'quality is that which creates a mark in the substance'?
A) Because it is circular
B) Because 'mark' is metaphorical and unclear
C) Because it confuses quality with quantity
D) Because it applies only to substances

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must use clear, non-circular, and non-metaphorical language, and its components must uniquely identify the definiendum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Critique of Flawed Definitions of Quality
Author critiques definitions of quality and endorses one as sound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate definitions of a logical category by identifying circularity, ambiguity, and obscurity, using the critiques of definitions of quality as a model.

Core Idea
A sound definition must avoid circularity, ambiguity, and obscurity.

In this passage, the author examines several proposed definitions of the category 'quality' (al-kayfiyya) and demonstrates why they fail. By learning to spot these flaws, you will sharpen your ability to critically assess definitions in any Islamic science, from Usul al-Fiqh to Kalam. The skill of evaluating definitions is essential for precise reasoning and avoiding conceptual confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions being critiqued
Locate each definition of 'quality' that the author presents. There are three flawed definitions and one accepted definition. List them in order as they appear in the Arabic passage.
The first definition: quality as a type of some predecessor view; second: quality is that by which similarity and dissimilarity occur; third: quality is that which produces a trace in substance; fourth: quality is an accident independent in conception etc.
2
Extract the specific flaw for each definition
For each flawed definition, identify the exact logical problem the author raises: ambiguity, circularity, metaphorical language, or unintelligibility. Write the flaw in your own words.
For the similarity definition, the flaw is circularity (if similarity means agreement in quality) or unknown reference. For the trace definition, the flaw is metaphorical obscurity.
3
Understand why the accepted definition is sound
The author endorses the definition: 'Quality is an accident whose conception does not depend on the conception of another, and does not necessitate division or non-division in the subject with a primary necessity.' Explain why this avoids the earlier problems: it is clear, non-circular, and uses terms that are better understood.
This definition does not rely on 'quality' itself, it is literal, and it does not introduce ambiguous terms like 'trace'.
4
Apply the criteria to other definitions
Practice by evaluating a definition from another Islamic science, e.g., 'The wajib is that whose omission entails punishment.' Check for circularity: does 'punishment' require knowing 'wajib'? Does it use ambiguous terms?
This is a real definition debated in Usul al-Fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوع منه كما ذهب إليه من السلف لما كانت الأجوبة موافقة: وفيه من النظر؛ إذ المقولات عند الجمهور مشابهة ولا عمومية لبعضها بالنسبة إلى البعض ولا خصوصية كذلك. ولأن الكيف قد يذكر ويراد به الكيفية؛ وقد يذكر ويراد به ما له الكيفية. ولو كان كذلك فذلك أمر غير محصل ولا يمكن أن يقال المراد منه هذا على التعيين أو ذلك، والفساد في الكل ظاهر. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية ما به تقع المشابهة بين الأشياء واللا مشابهة؛ وفيه من الفساد أيضًا فإن لفظة المشابهة إما أن يراد بها الموافقة في الكيفية وإنه يفضي إلى الدور، وإما أن يراد بها الغير؛ وذلك لا تدرى حاله بأنه يصح أو لا يصح. وإن صح فإما أن يصح بطريق الحقيقة أو بطريق المجاز؛ والفساد في الكل ظاهر. ولا يقال لا فساد فيه إذا كان المراد منها الغير بطريق الحقيقة؛ فإن الفساد فيه ظاهر وإنه في حيز المنع فلا يمكن أن يراد بطريق الحقيقة عند الجمهور من العلماء؛ خصوصا عند أهل المنطق. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية هي التي تُحدِثُ رسما في الجوهر؛ وفيه من الفساد أيضًا فإن لفظ الرسم يشبه أن يكون استعماله بطريق المجاز وحينئذ لا يتحقق معنى. وإن تحقق فلا يكون بحسب التعارف فيكون هذا اللفظ أشد بعدا عن البيان من لفظة الكيفية. ومنها ما يقال إن الكيفية عرض لا يتوقف تصوره على تصور غيره ولا يقتضي القسمة واللَّا قسمة في المحل اقتضاء أوليا؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا فساد فيه.
A type of it, as some of the predecessors held, because the answers were in agreement: and there is scrutiny in it; for the Categories according to the majority are similar, and there is no universality of some relative to others, nor particularity likewise. And because 'quality' (kayf) may be mentioned and intended by it the quality (kayfiyya), and may be mentioned and intended by it that which has quality. And if it were so, then that is an unattained matter, and it cannot be said that what is meant by it is this specifically or that, and the corruption in all is apparent. And among them is what is said: 'Quality is that by which similarity and dissimilarity occur between things'; and in it there is corruption as well, for the term 'similarity' either is intended by it agreement in quality, and that leads to circularity, or is intended by it something else; and that is not known as to its state, whether it is valid or not. And if it is valid, then either it is valid by way of literal truth or by way of metaphor; and the corruption in all is apparent. And it is not said that there is no corruption in it if what is meant by it is something else by way of literal truth; for the corruption in it is apparent and it is in the realm of being prevented, so it cannot be intended by way of literal truth according to the majority of scholars, especially according to the logicians. And among them is what is said: 'Quality is that which produces a trace (rasm) in the substance'; and in it there is corruption as well, for the term 'trace' seems to be used metaphorically, and then no meaning is realized. And if it is realized, it is not according to convention, so this term is further from clarity than the term 'quality'. And among them is what is said: 'Quality is an accident whose conception does not depend on the conception of another, and does not necessitate division or non-division in the subject with a primary necessity'; and this is among that which has no corruption in it.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage critiques several definitions of quality (kayfiyya), which is one of the Aristotelian categories. The author first mentions a view that treats quality as a type of something, but then points out that the categories are similar (i.e., not strictly universal or particular relative to each other). Also, the term 'quality' (kayf) can be ambiguous, referring either to the abstract quality (kayfiyya) or to the thing having that quality. This ambiguity makes the definition uninformative. Next, the definition 'quality is that by which similarity and dissimilarity occur' is criticized because 'similarity' either means agreement in quality (circular) or something else, which is unknown. If it means something else, it could be literal or metaphorical; both are problematic. If taken literally, it is denied by most scholars and logicians. Third, the definition 'quality is that which produces a trace (rasm) in substance' is flawed because 'trace' is metaphorical and unclear; even if real, it is unconventional and more obscure than the original term. Finally, the definition 'quality is an accident whose conception does not depend on another, and does not primarily necessitate division or non-division in the subject' is accepted as sound. This definition avoids circularity and ambiguity, and will be expanded in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الكيفية
الجوهر
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the definition is too broad.
D) Because quality is not related to similarity.

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must be clear, non-circular, and literal; the accepted definition of quality meets these criteria.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defining Quality and Distinguishing It from Other Categories
Author presents the correct definition of quality and distinguishes it from other categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the definition of quality (kayfiyya) and identify which categories are excluded by each condition in the definition.

Core Idea
Quality is a fixed state independent of relation, division, or proportion.

In the Aristotelian categories, quality (kayfiyya) is one of the nine accidents. But how do we define it precisely, and how do we ensure it doesn't overlap with other categories like relation, quantity, or position? The author provides a careful definition using negative conditions – each condition carves out what quality is not. By studying this, you learn the logical method of definition by differentiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'quality' (kayfiyya). Locate the term in the Arabic and note that the definition uses a series of exclusions.
2
Extract the genus and differentiae
The genus is 'accident' (عرض), as stated in the first phrase. Then differentiae are listed: 'fixed state' (هيئة قارة), 'whose conception does not necessitate conception of anything external to it and to its bearer', and 'does not necessitate division or proportion in the parts of its bearer'.
Differentiae are negative here – they exclude other categories.
3
Map each condition to the category it excludes
For each phrase in the definition, identify which other category is excluded: 'fixed state' excludes time, action, passion; 'no external conception' excludes relation, place, time, possession; 'no division' excludes quantity, unity, point; 'no proportion in parts' excludes position.
This is a parsing exercise – match conditions to categories.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Check if the definition includes all types of quality (e.g., colors, states, dispositions) and excludes all other categories. Does the definition cover qualities like knowledge? The author notes a special case for 'knowledge of indivisible things' to clarify that knowledge's divisibility is secondary.
Complete definition excludes all non-quality categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبالعرض يتميز عن الجوهر وبالذي لا يتوقف عن المضاف وبالذي لا يقتضي عن الكم والوحدة والنقطة، ويقوله اقتضاء أوليا عن العلم بالمعلومات التي لا تنقسم. فإن علم الانقسام لا يكون لذاتها بل بواسطة الوحدة والمفهوم منها أن يقال إنها عبارة عن هيئة قارة لا يوجب تصورها تصور شيء خارج عنها وعن حاملها ولا قسمة ونسبة في أجزاء حاملها؛ فبهيئة قارة يفارق الزمان ومقولة أن يفعل وأن ينفعل، وبأنها لا يوجب تصورها تصور شيء خارج عنها يفارق المضاف والأين ومتى والملك، وبأنها لا توجب قسمة الكم، وبأنها لا توجب نسبة في أجزاء حاملها الوضع.
So by 'accident' it is distinguished from substance; by 'whose conception does not depend' from relation; by 'does not necessitate' from quantity, unity, and point; and by the phrase 'with a primary necessity' from knowledge of indivisible things. For the knowledge of division is not by their essences but through unity. And the concept from it is that it is said: 'It is a fixed state (hay'a qarra) whose conception does not necessitate the conception of anything external to it and to its bearer, nor division and proportion in the parts of its bearer.' So by 'fixed state' it differs from time and the categories of doing and being affected; by the fact that its conception does not necessitate the conception of anything external to it, it differs from relation, place (ayn), when (mata), and possession (milk); by the fact that it does not necessitate division, it differs from quantity; and by the fact that it does not necessitate proportion in the parts of its bearer, it differs from position (wad').
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage gives the correct definition of quality (kayfiyya) and shows how it distinguishes quality from other categories. The definition uses several negative conditions: quality is an accident (so not substance), its conception does not depend on another (so not relation, place, time, possession), it does not necessitate division (so not quantity, unity, point), and it does not primarily necessitate division (so not knowledge of divisible things). The author also provides a fuller expression: 'a fixed state whose conception does not necessitate the conception of anything external to it and to its bearer, nor division and proportion in the parts of its bearer.' Then each part is explained: 'fixed state' excludes time and the categories of action/passion; 'does not necessitate conception of external' excludes relation, place, time, possession; 'does not necessitate division' excludes quantity; 'does not necessitate proportion in parts' excludes position. This careful differentiation shows that quality is a distinct category that does not imply any external relation or spatial/temporal division.

Key Terms
الهيئة القارة (al-hay'a al-qarra: fixed state)
المضاف (al-mudaf: relation)
الوضع (al-wad': position)

Key Takeaway
The definition of quality uses negative conditions to precisely distinguish it from all other categories, showing that logical definition often requires excluding what the thing is not.

The Division of Quality into Four Types
Author classifies quality into four types and begins describing the first type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of quality based on the given dichotomies and identify the three species of the first type.

Core Idea
Quality is divided by two dichotomies: quantitative vs non-quantitative, sensible vs non-sensible.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic division of quality into four types that covers all qualities. The division is based on two key distinctions: whether the quality is specialized to quantity or not, and whether it is sensible or not. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly categorizing properties in later logical and theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of division
The author splits quality using two dichotomies: (1) specialized to quantities or not, (2) sensible or not. Combine these to yield four types.
The four types are: (i) quantitative qualities, (ii) sensible qualities, (iii) dispositions, (iv) psychic perfections.
2
Identify the first type's range
The first type is qualified as 'specialized to quantities'. It acts as a genus covering all qualities that inherently belong to quantity, like shape, straightness, etc.
3
Examine the three species of the first type
Within the first type, list the three species: (a) shape (e.g., sphere), (b) non-shape modifications (e.g., straightness, evenness), (c) the combination (form/constitution, i.e., shape as sensible).
The third species is shape perceived in a body, like a colored square.
4
Grasp the common meaning of the first type genus
The common meaning is 'a quality that attaches to substances through their primary attachment to quantity insofar as it is quantity' – i.e., it directly modifies quantity, not accidentally.
This excludes strength/weakness because they accompany quantity but not necessarily due to quantity itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكيفية تنقسم إلى أربعة أنواع تحتوي عليها القسمة وهي أن الكيفية إما أن تكون مختصة بالكميات من جهة ما هي منها، وإما أن لا تكون: وإنها إما أن تكون من المحسوسات وإما أن لا تكون. وما لا يكون منها فذلك إما أن يكون الجسم بواسطته مستعدا لقول الكمالات، وإما أن لا يكون وذلك من الأعراض النفسانية التي هي كمالات بأنفسها. أما النوع الأول: فإنه كالجنس بالنسبة إلى ما تحته من الأنواع وهي ثلاثة: منها الشكل وهو ما أحاط به حد مثل الكرة والدائرة، أو حدود مثل المربع والمكعب كما قال به أو قليديس؛ ومنهـا غير الشكل وذلك كالاستقامة والانحناء للخط، والتحديب والتقعير والتسطح للسطح، والزوجية والفردية للعدد؛ ومنها ما هو حاصل من شكل وغير شكل وهو الذي يسمى صورة وخلقة، وهو الشكل من حيث هو محسوس في الجسم فيكون الشكل الملون خلقة. وما هو من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع؛ فذلك على ستة أقسام: الأول بحسب أن يعلم أن المعنى الجامع لهذا الجنس هو أنه هو الكيفية التي تعرض للجواهر لعروضها أولاً للكمية بما هي كمية ليس كالقوة والضعف فإنها وإن قارنت الكمية فليس لأجل أن الكمية بنفسها مستعدة لها استعدادًا أوليًا. وأما الشكل فإنه يعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وهذا الجنس بهذا المعنى يدخل فيه الشكل والاستقامة والانحناء وغيرها كما مر . ثم لقائل
Then quality is divided into four types which the division contains: that quality is either specialized to quantities in terms of being from them, or not; and that it is either from the sensibles or not. And that which is not from them is either that by which the body is disposed to receive perfections, or not; and that is from the psychic accidents which are perfections in themselves. As for the first type: it is like a genus relative to the species under it, which are three: from them is shape, which is what a boundary surrounds, like sphere and circle, or boundaries like square and cube as Euclid said; and from them is other than shape, like straightness and curvature for a line, convexity, concavity, and flatness for a surface, and evenness and oddness for number; and from them is what results from shape and non-shape, which is called form and constitution, and it is shape in terms of being sensible in the body, so the colored shape is constitution. And what is from the necessary concomitants is mentioned in this place; so that is according to six divisions: the first is in terms of knowing that the common meaning for this genus is that it is the quality that attaches to substances due to their first attachment to quantity insofar as it is quantity, not like strength and weakness, for although they accompany quantity, it is not because quantity itself is primarily disposed to them. As for shape, it attaches to magnitude insofar as it is magnitude, and this genus in this meaning includes shape, straightness, curvature, and others as mentioned. Then one may say...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
After defining quality, the author divides it into four types. The division is based on two dichotomies: whether the quality is specialized to quantities or not, and whether it is sensible or not. This yields four types: (1) qualities specialized to quantities (like shape and other quantitative modifications), (2) sensible qualities, (3) dispositions (like strength and weakness) that prepare the body for perfections, and (4) psychic accidents that are perfections themselves. The passage then focuses on the first type, which serves as a genus for three species: shape (e.g., sphere, square), non-shape (e.g., straightness, evenness), and a combination of both (form/constitution, which is shape as sensible). The author mentions that the common meaning of this genus is a quality that attaches to substances through their primary attachment to quantity. For example, shape attaches to magnitude as such, unlike strength and weakness which only accompany quantity incidentally. The passage ends abruptly, discussing that shape and others are subsumed under this genus.

The Three Species of the First Type of Quality
Classification of the first type of quality into three species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the three species of the first type of quality: shape, non-shape, and form (khilqa).

Core Idea
The first type of quality has three species: shape, non-shape, and their combination.

This lesson breaks down the classification of the first type of quality (al-naw' al-awwal min al-kayfiyya). The author treats it as a genus with three species. Understanding these species is essential for correctly categorizing attributes of physical bodies in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
The first type of quality is like a genus (jins) that includes multiple species. Recognize that 'first type' here refers to a specific category of quality that attaches to substances via quantity.
This is a logical classification, not a biological one.
2
List the three species
The text lists: (1) shape (shakl), (2) non-shape (ghayr shakl, e.g., straightness, curvature), and (3) form (khilqa). Note that khilqa combines shape and non-shape elements.
Non-shape includes properties like evenness/oddness for numbers, though they are not physical shapes.
3
Define shape
Shape is defined as what is surrounded by one boundary (e.g., sphere, circle) or multiple boundaries (e.g., square, cube). Quote Euclid for authority.
These are geometric examples; in Islamic thought, they are used for physical bodies.
4
Define non-shape
Non-shape includes qualities like straightness and curvature for lines, convexity for surfaces, etc. These attach to quantity but are not delimiting boundaries.
Evenness/oddness for numbers is included because numbers are quantities.
5
Define form (khilqa)
Khilqa is the colored shape of a body; it results from the combination of shape and color. It is sensible in the body.
The text emphasizes that khilqa is shape made perceptible through color.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النوع الأول: فإنه كالجنس بالنسبة إلى ما تحته من الأنواع وهي ثلاثة: منها الشكل وهو ما أحاط به حد مثل الكرة والدائرة، أو حدود مثل المربع والمكعب كما قال به أو قليديس؛ ومنها غير الشكل وذلك كالاستقامة والانحناء للخط، والتحديب والتقعير والتسطح للسطح، والزوجية والفردية للعدد؛ ومنها ما هو حاصل من شكل وغير شكل وهو الذي يسمى صورة وخلقة، وهو الشكل من حيث هو محسوس في الجسم فيكون الشكل الملون خلقة. وما هو من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع؛ فذلك على ستة أقسام: الأول بحسب أن يعلم أن المعنى الجامع لهذا الجنس هو أنه هو الكيفية التي تعرض للجواهر لعروضها أولاً للكمية بما هي كمية ليس كالقوة [٥٧,] والضعف فإنها وإن قارنت الكمية فليس لأجل أن الكمية بنفسها مستعدة لها استعدادًا أوليًا. وأما الشكل فإنه يعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وهذا الجنس بهذا المعنى يدخل فيه الشكل والاستقامة والانحناء وغيرها كما مر . ثم لقائل أن يقول الخلقة يخالف البواقي؛ إذ البواقي تعرض للكمية عروضًا أوليًا على خلاف الخلقة؛ فإنه ما لم يكن جسم متلون لم يكن خلقة. وهذا كالقوة واللا قوة التي تعرض أيضًا للكميات من حيث إنها تعرض لذوات الكميات. لكنا نقول إن الأمور التي تعرض للكمية منها ما تعرض للكمية نفسها لا بشرط أنها كمية لشيء، وفرق بين أن يقال إن الكمية تعرض لها الأمر عندما تكون في شيء وأن يقال إن الكمية إنما تعرض لها أمر لما أنها في الشيء الذي عرض له الأمر، فاللون أولا يقال على السطح وبتوسطه؛ فيقال على الجسم فيحمل السطح، ثم الجسم حتى يكون الجسم ذو القوة هو الذي مقدار تحته ذو قوة بل القوة يحملها جسم تحملها مادته وصورته، وإنما تحمله مادته وحده. فالخلقة التامة من شيء حامله السطح بذاته أو ما يحيط به السطح؛ وذلك الشيء هو الشكل، وشيء حامله السطح أيضًا ولكن عند كونه نهاية لجسم ما طبيعي وهذا الشيء هو اللون فإذن الخلقة من أمرين حاملهما الأول هو الكم وبتوسطه يقال على الجسم.
As for the first type: it is like a genus in relation to the species under it, which are three: among them is shape, which is what is surrounded by a single boundary like a sphere and a circle, or by boundaries like a square and a cube as Euclid stated; and among them is non-shape, such as straightness and curvature for a line, convexity, concavity, and flatness for a surface, and evenness and oddness for number; and among them is what results from shape and non-shape, which is called form and constitution (khilqa), and it is shape insofar as it is sensible in the body, so the colored shape is constitution. And what belongs to the concomitants is mentioned in this place; that is in six divisions. The first is in order to know that the common meaning of this genus is that it is the quality that attaches to substances by first attaching to quantity insofar as it is quantity, not like potency and impotence, for although they accompany quantity, it is not because quantity itself is primarily disposed for them. As for shape, it attaches to magnitude insofar as it is magnitude, and this genus in this sense includes shape, straightness, curvature, and others as mentioned. Then one may say that constitution (khilqa) differs from the rest, for the rest attach to quantity primarily, unlike constitution; for if the body were not colored, there would be no constitution. This is like potency and impotence, which also attach to quantities insofar as they attach to the essences of quantities. But we say: among the attributes that attach to quantity, some attach to quantity itself unconditionally as quantity of something, and there is a difference between saying that the attribute attaches to quantity when it is in a thing and saying that the attribute attaches to quantity because it is in the thing to which the attribute attaches. For color is first predicated of the surface and through it predicated of the body; so the surface bears it, then the body, until the body endowed with potency is that whose underlying magnitude is endowed with potency. Rather, potency is borne by a body whose matter and form bear it; and it is borne only by its matter alone. So perfect constitution (khilqa) is from something whose bearer is the surface itself or what the surface surrounds; that thing is shape, and something whose bearer is also the surface but inasmuch as it is the extremity of a natural body, and that thing is color. Therefore constitution is from two things; their first bearer is quantity, and through it it is predicated of the body.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of the category of quality (kayfiyya). The author first states that the first type of quality is like a genus for three species: shape (shakl), non-shape (like straightness and curvature), and a combination of shape and non-shape called form or constitution (khilqa). He defines shape as what is surrounded by one or more boundaries, citing examples from geometry. Then he clarifies the common meaning of this genus: it is a quality that attaches to substances by first attaching to quantity as quantity, distinguishing it from attributes like potency that also accompany quantity but not primarily. The author then addresses an objection: khilqa seems different because it requires color, so it does not attach to quantity primarily. He responds by distinguishing between attributes that attach to quantity itself and attributes that attach to quantity because it is in a thing that has another attribute. He explains that color first attaches to the surface, then to the body, while shape attaches to magnitude. Thus khilqa involves both shape and color, but its primary bearer is quantity. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each point without adding external information.

كيفية
شكل
استقامة
خلقة
النوع الأول

The Common Meaning and the Khilqa Objection
Explaining the common meaning of the first type and defending the inclusion of khilqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why khilqa belongs to the first type of quality despite requiring color, using the principle of primary attachment to quantity.

Core Idea
Khilqa attaches to quantity primarily even though it involves color.

This lesson examines the common meaning (ma'na al-jami') of the first type of quality and addresses an objection: since khilqa requires color, does it still attach to quantity primarily? The author argues yes, because quantity is the first bearer of both shape and color.

Guided Path
1
Understand the common meaning
The first type of quality attaches to substances by first attaching to quantity as quantity. This distinguishes it from qualities like potency that accompany quantity but are not primarily attached.
Potency and impotence are given as examples of qualities that are not primarily attached to quantity.
2
Identify the objection
An objector argues that khilqa (colored shape) differs from shape and non-shape because it requires color. Color attaches to the surface, not directly to quantity. Therefore, khilqa seems not to attach to quantity primarily.
The objection is that if color is not primarily attached to quantity, how can khilqa be?
3
Analyze the author's response
The author distinguishes between attributes that attach to quantity itself and those that attach to quantity because it is in a thing. Color attaches to the surface, which is a quantity, but it does so because the surface is the extremity of a body. However, khilqa's primary bearer is still quantity, because both shape (which is quantity itself) and color (which attaches to surface, a quantity) are ultimately borne by quantity.
The response involves a nuanced analysis of the bearer (hamil) of attributes.
4
Apply the distinction
Understand that khilqa is composed of shape and color. Shape attaches to magnitude itself; color attaches to the surface, which is a quantity. Thus, khilqa's first bearer is quantity, satisfying the common meaning.
The author emphasizes that khilqa, though composite, still belongs to the first type.

Source Text

أما النوع الأول: فإنه كالجنس بالنسبة إلى ما تحته من الأنواع وهي ثلاثة: منها الشكل وهو ما أحاط به حد مثل الكرة والدائرة، أو حدود مثل المربع والمكعب كما قال به أو قليديس؛ ومنها غير الشكل وذلك كالاستقامة والانحناء للخط، والتحديب والتقعير والتسطح للسطح، والزوجية والفردية للعدد؛ ومنها ما هو حاصل من شكل وغير شكل وهو الذي يسمى صورة وخلقة، وهو الشكل من حيث هو محسوس في الجسم فيكون الشكل الملون خلقة. وما هو من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع؛ فذلك على ستة أقسام: الأول بحسب أن يعلم أن المعنى الجامع لهذا الجنس هو أنه هو الكيفية التي تعرض للجواهر لعروضها أولاً للكمية بما هي كمية ليس كالقوة [٥٧,] والضعف فإنها وإن قارنت الكمية فليس لأجل أن الكمية بنفسها مستعدة لها استعدادًا أوليًا. وأما الشكل فإنه يعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وهذا الجنس بهذا المعنى يدخل فيه الشكل والاستقامة والانحناء وغيرها كما مر . ثم لقائل أن يقول الخلقة يخالف البواقي؛ إذ البواقي تعرض للكمية عروضًا أوليًا على خلاف الخلقة؛ فإنه ما لم يكن جسم متلون لم يكن خلقة. وهذا كالقوة واللا قوة التي تعرض أيضًا للكميات من حيث إنها تعرض لذوات الكميات. لكنا نقول إن الأمور التي تعرض للكمية منها ما تعرض للكمية نفسها لا بشرط أنها كمية لشيء، وفرق بين أن يقال إن الكمية تعرض لها الأمر عندما تكون في شيء وأن يقال إن الكمية إنما تعرض لها أمر لما أنها في الشيء الذي عرض له الأمر، فاللون أولا يقال على السطح وبتوسطه؛ فيقال على الجسم فيحمل السطح، ثم الجسم حتى يكون الجسم ذو القوة هو الذي مقدار تحته ذو قوة بل القوة يحملها جسم تحملها مادته وصورته، وإنما تحمله مادته وحده. فالخلقة التامة من شيء حامله السطح بذاته أو ما يحيط به السطح؛ وذلك الشيء هو الشكل، وشيء حامله السطح أيضًا ولكن عند كونه نهاية لجسم ما طبيعي وهذا الشيء هو اللون فإذن الخلقة من أمرين حاملهما الأول هو الكم وبتوسطه يقال على الجسم.
As for the first type: it is like a genus in relation to the species under it, which are three: among them is shape, which is what is surrounded by a single boundary like a sphere and a circle, or by boundaries like a square and a cube as Euclid stated; and among them is non-shape, such as straightness and curvature for a line, convexity, concavity, and flatness for a surface, and evenness and oddness for number; and among them is what results from shape and non-shape, which is called form and constitution (khilqa), and it is shape insofar as it is sensible in the body, so the colored shape is constitution. And what belongs to the concomitants is mentioned in this place; that is in six divisions. The first is in order to know that the common meaning of this genus is that it is the quality that attaches to substances by first attaching to quantity insofar as it is quantity, not like potency and impotence, for although they accompany quantity, it is not because quantity itself is primarily disposed for them. As for shape, it attaches to magnitude insofar as it is magnitude, and this genus in this sense includes shape, straightness, curvature, and others as mentioned. Then one may say that constitution (khilqa) differs from the rest, for the rest attach to quantity primarily, unlike constitution; for if the body were not colored, there would be no constitution. This is like potency and impotence, which also attach to quantities insofar as they attach to the essences of quantities. But we say: among the attributes that attach to quantity, some attach to quantity itself unconditionally as quantity of something, and there is a difference between saying that the attribute attaches to quantity when it is in a thing and saying that the attribute attaches to quantity because it is in the thing to which the attribute attaches. For color is first predicated of the surface and through it predicated of the body; so the surface bears it, then the body, until the body endowed with potency is that whose underlying magnitude is endowed with potency. Rather, potency is borne by a body whose matter and form bear it; and it is borne only by its matter alone. So perfect constitution (khilqa) is from something whose bearer is the surface itself or what the surface surrounds; that thing is shape, and something whose bearer is also the surface but inasmuch as it is the extremity of a natural body, and that thing is color. Therefore constitution is from two things; their first bearer is quantity, and through it it is predicated of the body.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of the category of quality (kayfiyya). The author first states that the first type of quality is like a genus for three species: shape (shakl), non-shape (like straightness and curvature), and a combination of shape and non-shape called form or constitution (khilqa). He defines shape as what is surrounded by one or more boundaries, citing examples from geometry. Then he clarifies the common meaning of this genus: it is a quality that attaches to substances by first attaching to quantity as quantity, distinguishing it from attributes like potency that also accompany quantity but not primarily. The author then addresses an objection: khilqa seems different because it requires color, so it does not attach to quantity primarily. He responds by distinguishing between attributes that attach to quantity itself and attributes that attach to quantity because it is in a thing that has another attribute. He explains that color first attaches to the surface, then to the body, while shape attaches to magnitude. Thus khilqa involves both shape and color, but its primary bearer is quantity. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each point without adding external information.

B) Because shape, which is part of khilqa, attaches to quantity primarily

Key Takeaway
Khilqa belongs to the first type of quality because quantity is the first bearer of both its components (shape and color), even though color attaches indirectly.
