Form as Quality: Distinguishing Shakl from Quantity and Position
Author defines shakl as a quality and distinguishes it from quantity and position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify shakl (form) as a quality (kayfiyya) in the ten categories and refute claims that it belongs to quantity or position using the author's arguments.

Core Idea
Form is not the limits-boundary object but the configuration (hay'a) that makes a thing square or round.

The category of form (shakl) is often confused with quantity or position because geometry speaks of 'figures.' But the author argues that the true form is a quality—the configuration arising from magnitude and limits. In this passage, he carefully separates what belongs to quantity (the limited magnitude), what belongs to quality (the configuration), and why position is about parts, not boundaries. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of Aristotle's categories as used in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of form
Find where the author contrasts the engineers' definition ('what is surrounded by limits') with his own definition (the configuration resulting from limit and limited). Note that the first defines a quantity, the second a quality.
The author uses the example of 'square' (murabba') vs 'squaring' (tarbi').
2
Examine the argument against classification as quantity
The author states that 'what is surrounded by limits per se is magnitude, and magnitude per se is quantity.' Therefore, if form were defined that way, it would be quantity, not quality. But the true form is that configuration, which cannot be surrounded by limits.
This is a straightforward 'genus-differentia' move: quantity vs quality.
3
Analyze the refutation of form as position (wad')
The author argues that position concerns parts (ajza') and their adjacency relations, while form concerns limits (hudud). He gives the example of sitting: it involves a relation of parts. Form, by contrast, involves limits surrounding a magnitude. So they are different categories.
He also criticizes those who confuse homonymy of 'wad' (position as category vs adjacency).
4
Synthesize the correct definition of shakl as quality
From the arguments, conclude that shakl is a quality (kayf) that inheres in quantity but is not itself quantity. It is the specific configuration that results from magnitude and limits, e.g., squareness (tarbi'), roundness.
This is the key takeaway for applying the ten categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقول الخلقة يخالف البواقي؛ إذ البواقي تعرض للكمية عروضًا أوليًا على خلاف الخلقة؛ فإنه ما لم يكن جسم متلون لم يكن خلقة. وهذا كالقوة واللا قوة التي تعرض أيضًا للكميات من حيث إنها تعرض لذوات الكميات. لكنا نقول إن الأمور التي تعرض للكمية منها ما تعرض للكمية نفسها لا بشرط أنها كمية لشيء، وفرق بين أن يقال إن الكمية تعرض لها الأمر عندما تكون في شيء وأن يقال إن الكمية إنما تعرض لها أمر لما أنها في الشيء الذي عرض له الأمر، فاللون أولا يقال على السطح وبتوسطه؛ فيقال على الجسم فيحمل السطح، ثم الجسم حتى يكون الجسم ذو القوة هو الذي مقدار تحته ذو قوة بل القوة يحملها جسم تحملها مادته وصورته، وإنما تحمله مادته وحده. فالخلقة التامة من شيء حامله السطح بذاته أو ما يحيط به السطح؛ وذلك الشيء هو الشكل، وشيء حامله السطح أيضًا ولكن عند كونه نهاية لجسم ما طبيعي وهذا الشيء هو اللون فإذن الخلقة من أمرين حاملهما الأول هو الكم وبتوسطه يقال على الجسم.
 والثاني أن الرسم المشهور في الشكل يشبه أن يكون ذلك الرسم غير متحقق للشكل الذي هو الكيفية بل هو رسم للذي يستعمله المهندسون الذين يقولون إنه مساو لشكل آخر وغير مساو، وهو مقدار مشكل. فإن الشيء الذي يحيط به الحدود بالذات هو المحدود، والمحدود بالذات هو المقدار، والمقدار بالذات كم، والشكل كيف. فليس إذن ما يحيط به الحدود بشكل هو الشكل الذي من باب الكيف لكن الهيئة الحاصلة من وجود الحد والمحدود على نسبة ما هو الشكل. ألا ترى أن المربع غير التربيع؟ وقد يقال [المربع]، ويُعنى به التربيع كما يقال أبيض ويُعنى به البياض. ثم التربيع شكل من باب الكيف ولا يقال إنه ما أحاط به حد، أو حدود ولا يحيط به الحد ولا الحدود أيضًا فإن ذلك هو المكيف نحو الكرة والمربع لا الكيف؛ ولا يصح حمل الكيف عليه. والمهندسون إنما يعنون بالمربع والشكل ما يكون فيه التربيع والشكل. فلذلك يصح أن يقال للشكل إنه ما أحاط به حد أو حدود، إذا عرفت هذا فقد عرفت أن الاستقامة والانحناء والتعقيب وغير ذلك ليست بأشكال بل هيئات للكمية لا تعلق لها بالحدود أصلًا.
 والثالث هو أن الشكل من الكيف لا من الوضع؛ إذ الشكل إنما يلتئم من مقدار / ومن حدود على هيئة كالمربع من سطح ومن أربعة حدود ومن هيئة، فلا يبالي في عادة الجمهور أن يطلق اسم المربع على السطح من حيث له أربعة حدود وعلى الحدود الأربعة وعلى الهيئة لكن السطح والحدود من باب الكم، والهيئة من باب الكيف؛ وليس ولا واحد منهما من باب الوضع. ومن زعم أن الشكل مما يحمل معنى الوضع عليه بوجه من الوجوه فذلك بغفلة من اشتراك الاسم في الوضع؛ وذلك لأن الوضع قد يقال على معان: فيقال وضع لحصول الشيء في موضعه؛ وهذا هو نفس مقولة الأين. ويقال وضع لحصول الشيء مجاورًا لشيء من جهة مخصوصة كما إذا وضع خط من يمين خط مثلا، وهذا نوع من المضاف. ومن زعم بأنه لا يكون من باب المضاف فعليه أن ينظر في الوضع الذي هو المقولة فإن الجلوس صفة لحمله الجالس لا لشيء من أجزائه لكن إنما تكون هذه الصفة للجالس إذا كان لأجزائه بعضها إلى بعض إضافة، ولا كل إضافة بل إضافة هيئة المجاورة، ولا كل إضافة هيئة المجاورة بل أن يكون لها نسبة مع ذلك إلى جهات يكتفيها على ما عرف. ومن زعم أن الشكل من الوضع لما أنه يتعلق بحدود بينها تجاور خاص فقد أخطأ من وجوه منها أنه أخذ الحدود مكان الأجزاء؛ وإنما الاعتبار في الوضع بالأجزاء وفي الشكل بالحدود. ومنها أنه ظن أن هذا الوضع من المقولة ولم يعلم أن
That natural form (khilqa) differs from the other accidents; for the other accidents pertain to quantity as primary accidents unlike natural form; for unless a body is colored, there is no natural form. This is like power and lack of power which also pertain to quantities insofar as they pertain to the essences of quantities. But we say that among the things that pertain to quantity, some pertain to quantity itself without condition that it is quantity for something, and there is a difference between saying that quantity has something occur to it when it is in a thing and saying that quantity only has something occur to it because it is in the thing that has the occurrence. Thus color is first said of the surface and intermediately on the body, so the surface is predicated, then the body, so that the body having power is that under which there is a magnitude having power; rather, power is borne by a body whose matter and form bear it, and only its matter bears it. So complete natural form is from something whose bearer is the surface itself or what the surface surrounds; that thing is form (shakl), and something else whose bearer is the surface also but when it is the boundary of a natural body, and that thing is color. So natural form consists of two things whose first bearer is quantity and through it is said of the body.
 Second, the well-known definition of form seems not to be realized for form as quality but is rather a definition used by engineers who say it is equal to another form or not equal, and it is a configured magnitude. For the thing surrounded by limits per se is the limited, and the limited per se is the magnitude, and magnitude per se is quantity, and form is quality. So what is surrounded by limits is not the form that belongs to quality, but rather the configuration (hay'a) resulting from the existence of the limit and the limited in a certain ratio is the form. Do you not see that the square (murabba') is different from squaring (tarbi')? And it may be said 'square' meaning squaring, just as 'white' is said meaning whiteness. Then squaring is a form belonging to quality, and it is not said that it is what a limit or limits surround, nor does the limit or limits surround it, for that is the quantified, like the sphere and the square, not the quality; and it is not correct to predicate quality of it. The engineers mean by 'square' and 'form' that in which squaring and form exist. Therefore it is correct to say of form that it is what a limit or limits surround; if you have understood this, then you have understood that straightness, curvature, twist and so on are not forms but rather configurations of quantity having no relation to limits at all.
 Third, form belongs to quality, not to position (wad'); for form is composed of magnitude and limits in a configuration, like a square composed of a surface, four limits, and a configuration. Common usage does not mind applying the name 'square' to the surface insofar as it has four limits, to the four limits, and to the configuration; but the surface and limits belong to quantity, and the configuration belongs to quality; not one of them belongs to position. Whoever claims that form carries the meaning of position in any way is due to negligence of the homonymy of the term 'position'; for 'position' is said in several senses: it is said for the occurrence of a thing in its place—this is the category of 'where' (ayn). And it is said for the occurrence of a thing adjacent to another in a specific manner, as when a line is placed to the right of a line, for example—this is a type of relation (idafa). And whoever claims that it is not of the type of relation must consider position as a category; for sitting is an attribute of the bearer of the sitter, not of any of its parts, but this attribute belongs to the sitter only if its parts have a relation to each other, not any relation but a relation of adjacency configuration, and not any adjacency configuration but that it has a proportion with respect to directions that suffice, as known. And whoever claims that form belongs to position because it is related to limits with special adjacency has erred in several ways: one is that he took limits in place of parts; consideration in position is regarding parts, while in form it is regarding limits. Another is that he thought this position is a category, and he did not know that...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines the category of form (shakl) as a quality (kayfiyya) and refutes two alternative classifications: that form is a quantity or that it belongs to the category of position (wad'). The author begins by contrasting natural form (khilqa) with other accidents that pertain to quantity. Natural form requires a colored body, while other accidents like power are immediate to quantity. He then clarifies that some accidents occur to quantity itself, not because it is in a subject. The example of color shows how an accident first attaches to the surface and then to the body. Next, the author addresses the standard definition of form used by engineers: 'what is surrounded by limits.' He argues that this defines the quantified object (al-miqdar), not form as a quality. The true form is the configuration (hay'a) resulting from the limits and the limited. For instance, a square (murabba') is not the same as squaring (tarbi'); squaring is a quality. The engineers, however, use 'square' for both the surface and the shape, leading to confusion. Finally, the author argues that form cannot belong to position (wad'). He distinguishes senses of 'position': (1) location in place (the category 'where'), and (2) adjacency relation (a type of relation). Form involves limits, not parts, and position involves parts. The example of sitting shows that position in the category sense requires a specific relation of parts to each other and to directions. Therefore, form is a quality and not position.

Key Takeaway

Shape: Defined by Boundaries, Not by Quality
Author clarifies definition of shape and its distinction from quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a qualified thing (mukayyaf) and its quality (kayf), and apply the definition of shape as that which is enclosed by boundaries.

Core Idea
Shape is defined by boundaries, not by qualitative states.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common confusion: thinking that straightness or curvature themselves are shapes. Instead, he distinguishes between the qualified object (mukayyaf) and the quality (kayf), and provides a precise definition of shape as that which is enclosed by a boundary. Understanding this distinction is crucial for working with definitions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between mukayyaf and kayf
Notice the contrast: al-mukayyaf is the sphere or square; al-kayf is roundness or squareness. The two are not the same.
2
Find the definition of shape
Look for the phrase 'ما أحاط به حد أو حدود' (that which is enclosed by a boundary or boundaries). This is the core definition.
3
Understand what is excluded
The author explicitly excludes straightness, curvature, roundness from being shapes. They are states of quantity.
4
Apply the definition to a concrete example
Consider a cube: it is a shape because it is enclosed by six boundaries (faces). The quality of being 'cubic' is not the shape itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ذلك هو المكيف نحو الكرة والمربع لا الكيف؛ ولا يصح حمل الكيف عليه. والمهندسون إنما يعنون بالمربع والشكل ما يكون فيه التربيع والشكل. فلذلك يصح أن يقال للشكل إنه ما أحاط به حد أو حدود، إذا عرفت هذا فقد عرفت أن الاستقامة والانحناء والتعقيب وغير ذلك ليست بأشكال بل هيئات للكمية لا تعلق لها بالحدود أصلًا.
For that is the qualified (mukayyaf) like the sphere and square, not the quality (kayf); and it is not correct to attribute quality to it. The engineers mean by square and shape that which contains squaring and shape. Therefore it is correct to say of a shape that it is what is enclosed by a boundary or boundaries. If you have understood this, then you have understood that straightness, curvature, roundness, and so on are not shapes but states (hay'at) of quantity, having no relation to boundaries at all.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of shape. The author distinguishes between the thing that is qualified (mukayyaf) and the quality itself (kayf). For example, a sphere or a square is a thing that has a shape; it is not the quality of roundness or squareness itself. He notes that engineers (muhandisun) use the term 'square' or 'shape' to refer to the object that contains the property of being square or shaped. Thus, a shape is correctly defined as 'that which is enclosed by a boundary or boundaries.' From this definition, the author draws an important conclusion: straightness (istiqama), curvature (inhina'), roundness (ta'qib), and similar attributes are not shapes themselves; they are rather states or modes (hay'at) of quantity (kammiyya) that have no connection to boundaries (hudud) in their essence. In other words, shape is about the boundaries, not the qualitative states of the enclosed quantity.

Shape belongs to quality not position
Author argues that shape is from quality, not position, refuting opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categorical classification of shape as quality and refute the mistaken claim that it belongs to position.

Core Idea
Shape is a composite of quantity and configuration; configuration is quality, not position.

In this passage, the author addresses a common confusion in Aristotle's categories: where does shape belong? Some think shape involves position because its boundaries are adjacent. But the author insists that shape's essential aspect—its configuration—is a quality. He distinguishes different meanings of 'position' to show that the adjacency of boundaries is a relation, not the category of position.

Guided Path
1
State the claim being refuted
Identify the opposing view that shape belongs to the category of position (wad'). The author counters this in the passage.
2
Analyze the components of shape
Break down shape into quantity (e.g., surface, boundaries) and configuration (hay'a). Note that the configuration is explicitly said to be from quality.
The square example helps: surface and boundaries are quantity, configuration is quality.
3
Distinguish meanings of 'wad''
Understand that 'position' (wad') has multiple meanings: location (the category of 'where'), adjacency (relation), and the category of position proper (arrangement of parts relative to external directions).
The category of position is not the same as relational adjacency.
4
Examine the three errors
List the three errors of those who say shape is from position: (1) confusing boundaries with parts, (2) mistaking adjacency for the category, (3) assuming involvement implies membership in the category.
Each error is explained in the passage.
5
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that shape's configuration (hay'a) is qualitative, so shape belongs to quality. The adjacency of its boundaries is merely a relation, not the category of position.

والثالث هو أن الشكل من الكيف لا من الوضع؛ إذ الشكل إنما يلتئم من مقدار ومن حدود على هيئة كالمربع من سطح ومن أربعة حدود ومن هيئة، فلا يبالي في عادة الجمهور أن يطلق اسم المربع على السطح من حيث له أربعة حدود وعلى الحدود الأربعة وعلى الهيئة لكن السطح والحدود من باب الكم، والهيئة من باب الكيف؛ وليس ولا واحد منهما من باب الوضع. ومن زعم أن الشكل مما يحمل معنى الوضع عليه بوجه من الوجوه فذلك بغفلة من اشتراك الاسم في الوضع؛ وذلك لأن الوضع قد يقال على معان: فيقال وضع لحصول الشيء في موضعه؛ وهذا هو نفس مقولة الأين. ويقال وضع لحصول الشيء مجاورًا لشيء من جهة مخصوصة كما إذا وضع خط من يمين خط مثلا، وهذا نوع من المضاف. ومن زعم بأنه لا يكون من باب المضاف فعليه أن ينظر في الوضع الذي هو المقولة فإن الجلوس صفة لحمله الجالس لا لشيء من أجزائه لكن إنما تكون هذه الصفة للجالس إذا كان لأجزائه بعضها إلى بعض إضافة، ولا كل إضافة بل إضافة هيئة المجاورة، ولا كل إضافة هيئة المجاورة بل أن يكون لها نسبة مع ذلك إلى جهات يكتفيها على ما عرف. ومن زعم أن الشكل من الوضع لما أنه يتعلق بحدود بينها تجاور خاص فقد أخطأ من وجوه منها أنه أخذ الحدود مكان الأجزاء؛ وإنما الاعتبار في الوضع بالأجزاء وفي الشكل بالحدود. ومنها أنه ظن أن هذا الوضع من المقولة ولم يعلم أن هذا من المضاف؛ وإنما الاعتبار في الوضع الذي هو المقولة بوضع أجزاء الشيء عند شيء من خارج لا بوضع أجزاء الشيء في نفسه قط. ومنها أنه ظن أن الشيء إذا كان متعلقا بمقولة فهو من تلك المقولة؛ فالشكل وإن كان لا يوجد إلا بإضافة بين الحدود أو بوضع فلا يلزم أن يكون وضعًا. والشكل لا يتعلق بالوضع الذي من المقولة بل بالوضع الذي هو من الإضافة. والدائرة لا تتعلق بهذا الوضع بل إنما تتم أن يكون لحدها هيئة مخصوصة في الانحناء فتجعل للمحدود هيئة الشكل كيفية بكيفيته.
The third point is that shape is from quality not from position; for shape is composed of a quantity and boundaries in a configuration (hay'a), like the square which is from a surface and four boundaries and a configuration. The common people do not mind applying the name 'square' to the surface insofar as it has four boundaries, and to the four boundaries, and to the configuration; but the surface and boundaries belong to the category of quantity, and the configuration belongs to the category of quality; and neither of them belongs to the category of position. Whoever claims that shape carries the meaning of position in any way, that is due to ignorance of the homonymy of the term 'position' (wad'). For 'position' may be used in several meanings: it is said for the occurrence of a thing in its place—this is the category of 'where' (ayn); and it is said for the occurrence of a thing adjacent to another in a specific direction, like when a line is placed to the right of another line—this is a type of relation (mudaf). Whoever claims that it is not from the category of relation should consider that position (as a category) is a quality of the sitter's body, not of its parts, but this quality only applies to the sitter when his parts have a certain relation to each other—not every relation, but a relation of adjacency configuration (hay'at al-mujawara), and not every such relation, but specifically one that has a proportion to directions as known. Whoever claims that shape is from position because it involves boundaries with specific adjacency has erred in several ways: first, he took boundaries in place of parts, whereas in position the consideration is of parts, in shape of boundaries; second, he thought that this position is from the category (of position), not knowing that it is from relation; indeed the consideration in the category of position is of the parts of a thing relative to something external, never of the parts of the thing in itself; third, he thought that if a thing pertains to a category, then it is from that category; but shape, even though it does not exist except with a relation between boundaries or a position, does not necessarily become a position. Shape does not relate to position as a category but to position as a relation. The circle does not relate to this position; rather, its boundary has a specific configuration in curvature, which gives the bounded object the configuration of shape as a quality through its quality.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage argues that shape belongs to the category of quality (kayf), not to the category of position (wad'). The author explains that shape is composed of three aspects: quantity (like surface), boundaries (like four lines), and a configuration (hay'a). People commonly call the entire composite a square, but each aspect belongs to different categories: surface and boundaries are quantity, configuration is quality. Neither is position. The author then refutes those who think shape involves position. He distinguishes different meanings of wad': one meaning is the category of 'where' (ayn) – location; another is a relation (mudaf) like adjacency. The category of position in Aristotle's sense is a specific quality of a body whose parts are adjacent in a certain way relative to external directions. Those who confuse shape with position make three errors: (1) they mistake boundaries for parts, but position concerns parts of a body, not its boundaries; (2) they think the adjacency of boundaries is the category of position, but it is actually a relation; (3) they assume if something involves a category then it is that category, but shape involves relation or position only accidentally. In reality, shape is a quality – the specific configuration of the boundary that gives the object its form, like the circle's curvature being a quality.

Angle: Distinguishing Angle from Shape and Its Types
Author examines the angle, distinguishes it from shape, and classifies its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify angles into planar and solid and identify the subtypes of planar angles based on line types and relative magnitude.

Core Idea
An angle is not a shape—it is a quantity bounded at a meeting point or line.

The author turns from general quantities to a specific quantity: the angle. He first distinguishes it from shape, then systematically classifies it. Understanding this classification helps you see how logicians and geometers define and divide concepts with precision. This parsing exercise sharpens your ability to analyze definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the contrast
The author contrasts angle (zawiya) with shape (shakl). Note how he defines angle: a quantity bounded between boundaries meeting at a single boundary (خط أو نقطة مشتركة).
This distinction prevents confusion between the geometric figure (shape) and its constituent angle.
2
Distinguish planar vs. solid angles
Planar (musattaha) angle is formed by two lines on a surface meeting at a point. Solid (mujassama) angle is formed by two surfaces meeting at a line. This is the primary division.
In Islamic astronomy, planar angles appear on celestial spheres, solid angles in conical projections.
3
Classify planar angles by line type
Planar angles are divided based on the lines: between two straight lines, two curved lines, or one straight and one curved. Each type has further subcategories.
This classification is used in geometric optics (Ibn al-Haytham) and astronomical instruments.
4
Classify straight-line planar angles by magnitude
If the lines are equally inclined on both sides, it is right (qa'ima). If smaller, acute (hadda); if larger, obtuse (munfarija). These are the three standard categories.
Right angles are foundational in Islamic architecture (e.g., prayer halls) and trigonometry.
5
Classify curved and mixed planar angles by convex/concave
For two curved lines, the enclosing boundary can be convex of both arcs, concave of both, or one convex and one concave. For a straight and a curved line, the convex or concave side of the circle matters.
These appear in lunar crescent calculations (hilal visibility).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع هو النظر في حال الزاوية وكيفية وقوعها في الكمية أو الكيفية، أما الفرق بين الزاوية وبين الشكل فهو أن الزاوية إنما هي زاوية من حيث يعتبر المقدار متحددًا بين حدين أو حدود يلتقيان بحد واحد. وإنما قد تكون مسطحة وهي السطح المتحدد بخطين يلتقيان بنقطة؛ وقد تكون مجسمة وهي الجسم المتحدد بسطحين بخط. أما المسطحة فإما أن تكون بين خطين مستقيمين أو مستديرين أو بين مستقيم ومستدير والأول إما أن يكون مثل الخط المتصل بالثاني إلى الجانبين على السواء والزاوية تسمى قائمة حينئذ، وإما أن لا تكون والأصغر من القائمة تسمى حادة والأكبر منفرجة؛ والثاني إما [0] أن يكون المحيط بها حديتا القوسين أو تقعرهما أو حدية أحدهما وتقعر الأخرى؛ والثالث إما أن يكون المحيط بها مع الخط المستقيم حدية الدائرة أو تقعرها. وأما المجسمة فإما أن يكون المحيط بها بسيطا واحدا كما في رأس المخروط أو بسطاً وسطحًا وهي التي على رأس نصف المخروط، أو سطوحًا وهي ظاهرة. وبالحري أن يكون هنا معنى جامع للزاوية التي عن خطين والتي عن سطوح عند نقط، والتي عن سطحين عند خط؛ وذلك هو كون المقدار ذا حدود تنتهي عند خط واحد مشترك لها من حيث هو كذلك. فإن جعل اسم
The fourth point is the examination of the angle and how it falls under quantity or quality. As for the difference between angle and shape: an angle is only an angle insofar as the quantity is considered as bounded between two boundaries or boundaries that meet at a single boundary. It may be planar: a surface bounded by two lines meeting at a point; or solid: a body bounded by two surfaces at a line. As for the planar: it is either between two straight lines, or two curved lines, or between a straight and a curved. The first: either the line is continuous with the second on both sides equally—the angle is then called right; or not—the smaller than right is called acute, the larger obtuse. The second: either the enclosing (boundary) is the convex sides of the two arcs or their concave sides, or the convex of one and the concave of the other. The third: either the enclosing with the straight line is the convex of the circle or its concave. As for the solid: either the enclosing is one simple (surface) as in the vertex of a cone, or a plane and a surface as at the vertex of a half-cone, or surfaces—this is obvious. It is fitting that there be here a comprehensive meaning for the angle formed by two lines, that by surfaces at a point, and that by two surfaces at a line; that is, that the quantity has boundaries ending at a single line common to them insofar as it is so. Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage examines the angle (zawiya) and its categorization. First, the author distinguishes angle from shape: an angle is considered as a quantity bounded between boundaries that meet at a single boundary (like lines meeting at a point or surfaces meeting at a line). Angles are divided into planar (musattaha) and solid (mujassama). Planar angles are formed by two lines on a surface meeting at a point. Solid angles are formed by two surfaces meeting at a line. The planar angle is further divided based on the types of lines: between two straight lines, two curved lines, or one straight and one curved. For two straight lines, if the lines are perpendicular and equal on both sides, it is a right angle (qa'ima); if less, acute (hadda); if more, obtuse (munfarija). For two curved lines, the enclosing boundaries can be the convex sides of both arcs, the concave sides, or one convex and one concave. For a straight and a curved line, the enclosing can be the convex side of the circle or its concave side. Solid angles are categorized based on the surfaces enclosing them: one simple surface (like the vertex of a cone), one plane and one surface (half-cone vertex), or multiple surfaces. The author suggests a comprehensive definition: an angle is a quantity whose boundaries end at a common line (or point) that they share. This unifies all types of angles.

Defining Angle: Genus, Differentia, and Classification
Author defines angle, classifies its types, and debates its category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the author's definition of angle and classify plane and solid angles according to their bounding lines or surfaces.

Core Idea
Angle is a quantity determinate between boundaries meeting at a common boundary.

The author begins by distinguishing angle from shape and then provides a precise definition. He also classifies angles into types based on the nature of the bounding lines or surfaces. Understanding this definition and classification is essential for correctly placing angle in the Aristotelian categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic term الزاوية in the passage and note the two definitions proposed: (a) a quantity determinate between boundaries meeting at a common boundary, and (b) a quantity ending at a point.
The author favours the first as more comprehensive.
2
Extract the genus
The author explicitly states that angle is a quantity (مقدار). So the genus is quantity (الكم).
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is that the quantity has boundaries that meet at a single common boundary (بحد واحد مشترك). This distinguishes angle from other quantities.
For plane angles, the common boundary is a point; for solid angles, it is a line.
4
Classify plane and solid angles
Plane angles are bounded by two lines meeting at a point; solid angles are bounded by two surfaces meeting along a line. Within plane, further classify by line type (straight/curved).
The author lists subtypes: between two straight lines, two curves, or one straight and one curve.
5
Evaluate the definition's completeness
Check if the definition includes all instances (both plane and solid) and excludes non-angles. The author's comprehensive meaning covers all cases.
He suggests that the name 'angle' can be applied to the comprehensive meaning without error.

This belongs to relation; rather consideration in position which is the category is by the placing of parts of a thing relative to something external, not by the placing of parts of a thing in itself at all. Among them is that one thought that if a thing is related to a category then it is from that category; so shape, although it does not exist except with a relation between boundaries or position, it does not necessarily follow that it is position. Shape does not relate to position which is from the category but to position which is from relation. The circle does not relate to this position but rather its boundary has a specific configuration in curvature, making for the bounded the configuration of shape as a quality by its quality.
The fourth is the examination of the state of angle and how it falls into quantity or quality. As for the difference between angle and shape, angle is an angle inasmuch as the quantity is considered determinate between two boundaries or boundaries that meet at a single boundary. It may be plane, which is the surface bounded by two lines meeting at a point; and it may be solid, which is the body bounded by two surfaces along a line. As for the plane, it is either between two straight lines, or two curved lines, or a straight and a curved. The first is either like the line attached to the second equally to both sides, and the angle is then called right; or not, and the smaller than right is called acute and the larger obtuse. The second is either bounded by the two arcs or their concavities, or by one arc and the concavity of the other. The third is either bounded by the straight line and the arc of the circle or its concavity. As for the solid, it is either bounded by a single simple [surface] as in the apex of a cone, or by a plane and a surface as at the apex of a half-cone, or by surfaces which are apparent. It is fitting that there be a comprehensive meaning for the angle from two lines and that from surfaces at a point, and that from two surfaces along a line; and that is that the quantity has boundaries ending at one common line for them inasmuch as it is so. For if the name 'angle' is given to this meaning, it is not far from correctness, and the ending of the plane angle at the point is not because it is an angle. If the name 'angle' is given to the quantity inasmuch as it ends at a point, there is no debate in it, and the meaning of angle becomes more specific than what we mentioned. No attention is paid to the statement of one who says that angle is another genus of quantity between line and surface, on the assumption that line has length only, and surface has length and width which are two perpendicular dimensions. Likewise between surface and body; for he erred in the knowledge of length and width, and the error extends to this, but the plane angle is surface and the solid is body if by angle is meant the quantity having this type of determination.
But if we look at configuration, angle is a quality. The geometricians have applied the name 'angle' to the quantity having the angle; therefore the angle is bisected, equal, small and large, and the completeness of the square's boundaries relative to each other is a state of position or relation likewise the state of the two boundaries of the angle. It has been said that angle is a quantity ending at a point, and this is close to what we mentioned. It has been said that it belongs to quantity, otherwise it would not be susceptible to equality and inequality, but this is subject to rejection because quantity is not annihilated by multiplication at all, but the angle is annihilated by multiplication if repeated, although this rejection is itself subject to rejection, known by reflection. As for the statement of equality and inequality, that is because its subject is a quantity. It has been said that it belongs to quality, otherwise it would not be susceptible to similarity and dissimilarity; and that is not due to the subject because the subject is a quantity and it is not essentially receptive, but this too is subject to rejection because it could be accidentally even if not by the subject. It has been said that it belongs to relation, otherwise it would not be correct to say that it is the contact of two lines, and this too is subject to rejection because the angle is described as being smaller, (1) supposed. And its form: 'Allah'.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological examination of the category (maqula) to which the angle belongs, following a critique of misclassifications. The author first corrects a previous error: someone thought that if a thing is related to a category, it must be in that category. For example, shape (shakl) involves position (wad'), but that does not make shape belong to the category of position; the position involved is relational, not categorical. Similarly, a circle's shape is a quality of curvature, not a position.

Then the fourth inquiry (al-rabi') is introduced: the angle and how it falls into quantity (kamm) or quality (kayf). The author distinguishes angle from shape: angle is a quantity determinate between boundaries meeting at a common boundary. He classifies angles into plane (musattaha) and solid (mujassama). Plane angles are further divided by the types of lines: both straight, both curved, or one of each. He defines right, acute, and obtuse for straight lines; for curved, he mentions the arc and concavity combinations. Solid angles are classified by the number of bounding surfaces: single (like cone apex), plane+surface (half-cone), or multiple surfaces.

He proposes a comprehensive definition: angle is a quantity whose boundaries end at a common line (for solid angles) or point (for plane angles) in that respect. This definition, he says, is close to correctness. Then he refutes a false claim that angle is a genus between line and surface, showing the misunderstanding of dimensions.

Next, he discusses the category dispute. From the perspective of configuration (hay'a), angle is quality. Geometricians apply the name to the quantity itself, so angle can be bisected, etc. He presents arguments for quantity, quality, and relation, and refutes each with counter-arguments. For quantity: if angle were quantity, it would not be annihilated by multiplication, but a right angle doubled becomes a straight line, so it ceases to be an angle. For quality: similarity/dissimilarity could be accidental. For relation: the claim that angle is contact of two lines is rejected because angle itself is described as smaller, not the contact. The passage ends with an apparently marginal note.

The author's method is to test each categorical assignment and show its vulnerability, concluding that the angle's category is not straightforward.

Testing the Categories of Angle: Quantity, Quality, or Relation?
Author debates angle's category among quantity, quality, and relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's arguments against classifying angle as pure quantity, quality, or relation, and evaluate their strength.

Core Idea
Angle cannot be pure quantity because doubling destroys it; but quality and relation also fail.

After defining angle, the author examines three candidate categories. He presents objections to each, showing the difficulty of placing angle in a single category. This lesson explores those objections and the author's method of testing categorical boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the candidate categories
The author tests three categories: quantity (الكم), quality (الكيف), and relation (المضاف). He lists arguments for each and then refutes them.
This is a dialectical method: each candidate is proposed and then critiqued.
2
Examine the test for quantity
The argument for quantity: angle admits equality/inequality. The author's objection: quantity is not annihilated by multiplication, but doubling a right angle produces a straight line (no longer an angle). So angle cannot be pure quantity.
The author says: 'الكم لا يبطل بالتضعيف أصلا، والزاوية تبطل بالتضعيف' (quantity is not annihilated by multiplication, but angle is annihilated when repeated).
3
Examine the test for quality
The argument for quality: angle admits similarity/dissimilarity. The author's objection: this similarity may be accidental, not essential, because the subject (quantity) is not essentially receptive to quality.
The author says: 'ليس ذلك بسبب المحل لما كان المحل كما وهو غير قابل بالذات' (that is not due to the subject because the subject is quantity and it is not essentially receptive).
4
Examine the test for relation
The argument for relation: angle is the contact of two lines. The author's objection: angle itself is described as being smaller or larger, not the contact. So it is not essentially relational.
The author says: 'الزاوية توصف بكونها أصغر' (the angle is described as being smaller), which would not apply to a relation.
5
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author does not definitively place angle in one category. Instead, he shows the limitations of each categorization, suggesting that angle is a complex entity that straddles categories. This reflects an epistemological caution.
The passage ends without a final verdict, leaving the question open.

As for the difference between angle and shape, angle is an angle inasmuch as the quantity is considered determinate between two boundaries or boundaries that meet at a single boundary. It may be plane, which is the surface bounded by two lines meeting at a point; and it may be solid, which is the body bounded by two surfaces along a line. As for the plane, it is either between two straight lines, or two curved lines, or a straight and a curved. The first is either like the line attached to the second equally to both sides, and the angle is then called right; or not, and the smaller than right is called acute and the larger obtuse. The second is either bounded by the two arcs or their concavities, or by one arc and the concavity of the other. The third is either bounded by the straight line and the arc of the circle or its concavity. As for the solid, it is either bounded by a single simple [surface] as in the apex of a cone, or by a plane and a surface as at the apex of a half-cone, or by surfaces which are apparent. It is fitting that there be a comprehensive meaning for the angle from two lines and that from surfaces at a point, and that from two surfaces along a line; and that is that the quantity has boundaries ending at one common line for them inasmuch as it is so. And its form: 'Allah'.

Determining the Category of an Angle
Defines angle, refutes objections on its category, concludes it is quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a definition of an entity by testing its category membership using the criteria of the Aristotelian categories.

Core Idea
An angle's essence is quality (form), not quantity (magnitude), because it is destroyed by repetition.

In the Islamic logical tradition, classifying a concept into the correct Aristotelian category is essential for accurate reasoning. This passage from the Mantiq defines 'angle' and examines whether it belongs to quantity, quality, relation, or position. Through a series of refutations, the author demonstrates that angle is fundamentally a quality, despite being measured. Let's walk through this categorical analysis step by step.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of angle
The passage defines angle as 'a magnitude that terminates at a point' (مقدار ينتهي عند نقطة). Note that this is a descriptive definition (rasm) rather than a strict genus-differentia definition.
2
Identify the candidate categories
The text considers four categories for angle: quantity (كم), quality (كيف), relation (مضاف), and position (وضع). Each is argued by some scholars.
3
Apply the criteria for quantity
Quantity is not destroyed by doubling (الكم لا يبطل بالتضعيف أصلا). An angle, however, is destroyed when repeated (e.g., adding angles to make a full circle eliminates the angle). Therefore, angle cannot be quantity.
4
Evaluate the arguments for quality and other categories
Angle is receptive to similarity/dissimilarity, which suggests quality. The author initially doubts this because the substrate is quantity, but ultimately accepts it. Arguments for relation (contact of two lines) and position (ratio of parts) are also examined and each has a counterargument.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
After weighing all perspectives, the author declares the truth (الحق) that angle is only quality (كيف), though it can be viewed under other categories depending on the aspect considered.

And it has been said that angle is a magnitude ending at a point, which is close to what we mentioned. And it has been said that it is of quantity, otherwise it would not be receptive of equality and inequality, but this is subject to denial because quantity is not nullified by doubling at all, whereas the angle is nullified by doubling if repeated, although this denial is also subject to denial, known by reflection. As for the statement of equality and inequality, that is because its substrate is quantity. And it has been said that it is of quality, otherwise it would not be receptive of similarity and dissimilarity; and that is not due to the substrate because the substrate is quantity, and it is not receptive by essence, but it is also subject to denial due to the possibility that it is by another even if not due to the substrate. And it has been said that it is of relation, otherwise it would not be correct to say that it is the contact of two lines, and it is subject to denial because the angle is described as being smaller (1) supposed. And its form: 'Allah'. And larger, and contact is not described by them at all. In short, it is not far to say that it is of quantity by consideration of magnitude, and of quality by consideration of form, and of relation by consideration of adjacency, and of position by consideration of the ratio between its parts, although the truth is that it is only quality.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines 'angle' (زاوية) and examines which Aristotelian category it belongs to. The author begins by clarifying that a plane angle terminating at a point does not make it an angle—rather the magnitude delimited is key. He rejects the view that angle is a separate genus of quantity between line and surface, explaining that the plane angle is itself a surface and the solid angle a body. When considering the form (الهيئة), angle is a quality (كيفية). Engineers use 'angle' to refer to the magnitude possessing the angle, hence it can be bisected, equal, etc. The author then presents and refutes several arguments: (1) Some say it is quantity because it admits equality/inequality; but the author counters that quantity is not destroyed by doubling, while an angle is destroyed when repeated (e.g., a full circle). (2) Others say it is quality because it admits similarity/dissimilarity; the author notes this may be due to its substrate (quantity) or another cause. (3) Another says it is relation because it is contact of two lines; but contact itself is not described as smaller or larger as angles are. Ultimately, the author concludes that the truth is that angle is only a quality, though it could be considered in multiple categories: quantity by magnitude, quality by form, relation by adjacency, and position by the ratio of its parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is angle not a quantity?
A) Because it cannot be measured.
B) Because quantity is not destroyed by doubling, but an angle is destroyed when repeated.
C) Because it is a substance.
D) Because it is a relation.

Key Takeaway
Essential categorical classification requires testing properties like receptivity to increase/decrease and destruction under repetition, not just surface features.

Composition of Accidents: Shape and Color
Claims that accidents can compose, using shape+color as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the composite nature of accidental qualities and analyze how shape and color combine to produce a new aesthetic quality.

Core Idea
Accidental qualities can combine to produce a new quality not present in their parts.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection to the composition of accidents. He argues that just as substances can be composed of genus and differentia parts, accidents can also form composites. He uses the example of a square and the combination of shape and color to demonstrate that a new quality emerges from their union.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about composition
Find where the author states that composition in accidents is possible. He says 'التركيب في الأعراض ممكن' (composition in accidents is possible).
2
Extract the example of the square
The author gives an example of a square composed of a boundary and four boundaries. Understand how this shows composition.
The square's parts are not substances but boundaries, yet they compose.
3
Analyze the combination of shape and color
When shape mixes with color appropriately, the result is called 'beautiful in form' (حسن الصورة). Note that this quality is not found in either part alone.
This demonstrates that accidents can compose to produce a new quality.
4
Compare with substances
The author contrasts substances, where parts have natures of genus and differentia, with accidents, which do not have such distinct parts.
This shows that accident composition is different but still possible.
5
Conclude the new quality
The special state of combination in shape+color demonstrates that composition in accidents yields a new quality, and this is evident ('وهذا ظاهر').

Source Text

And the fifth matter concerns the state of creation: it is a quality that is within one genus of the types of quality, and it is color and shape together. So someone may say: if composition is possible in the types of substances from substances, then it is not far that it is similarly possible in the types of accidents from accidents, and their definitions have composition from genus and differentia. But we say: composition in accidents is possible. And how not? The square is composed of a boundary and four boundaries. However, in substances there may be found what suits the nature of their genus and what suits the nature of their differentia as differing parts, even if one of them is not the nature of the genus and the other the nature; but in accidents such is not found. So shape, when it mixes with color appropriately, the thing is said to be beautiful in form and good in composition. And if one of them is devoid of the other, that is not said, nor its opposite. So shape, insofar as it is combined with color or with something else, has a special state of combination that is not present in either of its parts at all; and this is clear.

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth matter concerning composition in accidental qualities. It begins by classifying certain states of creation as a quality within a single genus of quality, specifically color and shape together. An imagined objector argues: if composition is possible among substances (e.g., animal from body and soul), why not among accidents? After all, definitions of accidents also have genus and differentia (e.g., 'line' is length without breadth). The author responds: composition in accidents is possible. The square, for instance, is composed of a boundary (the perimeter) and four boundaries (sides). But in substances, you find components that match the nature of the genus and differentia as distinct parts (e.g., body and soul in animal); in accidents, you don't find such distinct parts. Instead, when shape mixes with color appropriately, the result is called 'beautiful in form' (حسن الصورة). If one is absent, the term is not applied. Therefore, shape when combined with color has a special state of combination not found in either part alone. This demonstrates that composition of accidents can produce a new quality.

When Two Things Form a Single Category
Analyzes when two things form a single composite category or remain separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a true composite that belongs to a single category and an accidental aggregate that does not.

Core Idea
A composite of two things only belongs to a category if one is subject and the other attribute.

This passage examines the sixth matter concerning composites of two things. It explains that for a composite to be a unitary entity falling under one category, one component must be the subject (mawsuf) and the other an attribute. If neither dominates, the combination is merely accidental, forming an aggregate, not a new category. This distinction is crucial for understanding how categories apply to complex entities in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Locate the two things being combined in the composite. The passage labels them as 'موصوف' (subject) and 'صفة' (attribute) when they form a true composite.
The example given is 'المربع الذي يُعنى به سطح ذو هيئة' (the square meant as a surface possessing a form).
2
Determine subject-attribute relationship
Check if one component is naturally the subject and the other an attribute. In the square, the surface is the subject and the form is the attribute, so the whole belongs to the category of the subject (substance or quantity).
3
Assess whether a single essence results
Ask: does the combination produce 'جملة واحدة في الذات' (a single entity in essence)? If yes, it deserves a single category. If no, it falls under 'المجموع' (aggregate).
The passage contrasts this with accidental combinations like writing and length.
4
Apply the rule to other examples
Test other composites: e.g., 'الكتابة والطول' (writing and length) – neither is more deserving to be subject, so no single category arises.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the logic is clear: accidental combinations do not yield a new unitary category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسادس في حال المركب من شيئين أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون أحدهما أولى بأن يكون موصوفًا، والآخر صفة كالمربع الذي يُعنى به سطح ذو هيئة. فإن السطح هو الموصوف بالهيئة؛ والهيئة عارضة له. فالجملة من مقولة الموصوف باعتبار أنها سطح أو لا يكون كما إذا اختلفا؛ وليس أحدهما أولى بالموصوفية والآخر بالصفتية. فالاجتماع بينهما حينئذ يكون اجتماعًا عرضيًا كما في الكتابة والطول فلا يحدث من الاجتماع بينهما جملة واحدة في الذات فلا يستحق ذلك مقولة بل يدخل في المجموع والمجموع مركب فتكون مقولات هذه الأشياء أيضًا مركبة من المقولات فالكتابة إذا اجتمعت مع
And the sixth matter concerns the state of the composite of two things: it is inevitable that either one of them is more deserving to be described, and the other is a property, like the square which is meant as a surface possessing a form. For the surface is described by the form, and the form is accidental to it. So the whole belongs to the category of the described insofar as it is a surface, or not—as when they differ and neither is more deserving of being described and the other of being a property. Then the combination between them is an accidental combination, as in writing and length, so no single entity in essence results from their combination, so it does not deserve a category, but rather falls under the aggregate, and the aggregate is composite, so the categories of these things are also composed of categories. So writing, when it combines with...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth matter: a composite of two things and how it relates to the ten categories. It distinguishes two cases: (1) One component is naturally the subject and the other the attribute. For example, a square is a surface (subject) possessing a form (attribute). The whole ('a square') belongs to the category of the subject (substance or quantity) because the surface is the substrate. (2) When neither is more fit to be subject or attribute—they differ, like writing and length—their combination is merely accidental (عرضي). No single essence results, so no single category applies. Instead, it falls under 'aggregate' (المجموع), which itself is composite. Hence the categories of such composites are themselves composed of categories. The passage ends abruptly with the example of writing combining with something, but the argument is clear: accidental combinations do not yield a new unitary category.

Key Takeaway
A composite of two things yields a true category only when one component is naturally the subject and the other an attribute; otherwise, it is merely an aggregate.

Determining the Category of a Composite of Two Things
Author examines how a composite of two items fits into categories when one is primary substance and the other accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the category of a composite of two things by identifying which component is the subject (mawṣūf) and which is the accident (ṣifa).

Core Idea
A composite inherits the category of its subject if one part is a substance and the other an accident.

When two items combine, how do we classify them into the Aristotelian categories? The author clarifies that the key is to see whether one part naturally serves as the subject (substrate) and the other as an accidental description. If so, the whole takes the category of the subject. If not, the combination is accidental and belongs to multiple categories simultaneously. This lesson will walk you through the logic of such categorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the composite
Look for the two distinct things that are combined. For example, in 'square' we have 'surface' and 'shape'. In 'writing and length' we have writing (a quality) and length (a quantity).
2
Determine which component is the subject (mawṣūf) and which is the accident (ṣifa)
Ask: which one can exist independently and which one inheres in the other? The surface is the subject that receives the shape; shape is accidental to it. In 'writing and length', neither is more deserving to be subject; they are both accidents.
3
Assign the category based on the subject
If one component is clearly the subject (like surface), the composite takes the category of that subject. The square is in the category of substance because it is a surface.
4
Recognize accidental combination when no subject dominates
If neither component is naturally subject, the combination is merely aggregate (ijtimāʿ ʿaraḍī). It does not form a single essence, so it falls under multiple categories—e.g., writing (quality) and length (quantity) together yield both categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the sixth [aspect] is regarding the state of the composite of two things: that it is inevitable that either one of them is more deserving to be described and the other a description, like the square which is intended as a surface with a shape. For the surface is the described by the shape, and the shape is accidental to it. So the whole is from the category of the described insofar as it is a surface. Or it may be that they are not like that, when they differ and neither is more deserving of being described and the other of being a description. Then the combination between them is an accidental combination, as in the case of writing and length. So from their combination there does not arise a single whole in essence, so it does not deserve a category, but rather enters into the aggregate, and the aggregate is composite. So the categories of these things are also composed of categories. So when writing combines with length, the aggregate results from a quality, a relation, and a quantity, without real unity. So consider what you know: that what enters into intelligibles from matters and natures, that is what is constituted by a generic meaning and another specific meaning by which the genus is constituted, as was known.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage discusses how to categorize a composite of two items. If one item is the subject (e.g., surface) and the other an accident (e.g., shape), the composite takes the category of the subject (substance). If neither is naturally subject or description, the combination is accidental (like writing and length), and the aggregate does not form a single entity in essence; it belongs to multiple categories simultaneously. The author uses the example of writing (a quality) and length (a quantity) to illustrate that the combination yields a mere aggregate, not a new category.

Key Terms
موصوف
اجتماع عرضي

Key Takeaway
The category of a composite is determined by the subject component if one exists; otherwise, the composite is an accidental aggregate belonging to multiple categories.

Distinguishing Stable Affections from Transient Passions
Author defines the second type of quality as sensible qualities, distinguishing stable affections from transient passions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between stable affections (infi‘āliyya) and transient passions (infi‘ālāt) in the category of quality, and explain why transient qualities are still qualities.

Core Idea
Sensible qualities are either stable affections or transient passions; both are qualities because they are endpoints of change, not processes.

In Aristotle's Categories, the second type of quality comprises sensible qualities perceived by the five senses. The author subdivides these into stable affections (infi‘āliyya) and transient passions (infi‘ālāt). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying qualities in logic and for applying them in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where the permanence of a quality may affect legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes of sensible quality
Read the opening where the author says 'it is inevitable that it is either stable or not stable'. Note examples: stable = sweetness of honey, redness of rose; unstable = redness of shame, yellowness of fear.
Stable ones are called infi‘āliyya, unstable ones infi‘ālāt.
2
Analyze the naming reasons
The author gives two reasons for calling stable ones 'affections': (1) the senses are affected by them; (2) they arise from affections in their subjects. For transient ones, the name comes from the change itself.
The term 'infi‘āl' originally means 'being affected'; here applied to qualities resulting from being affected.
3
Understand the analogy to malaka and hal
Stable affections resemble 'possession' (malaka), transient passions resemble 'state' (hal). The author mentions these will be discussed later in the fourth type of quality.
This anticipates a further subdivision of quality into disposition vs. state.
4
Defend why transient qualities are still qualities
The author argues that yellowness (e.g., from fear) is not the process of becoming yellow (the category 'being affected'), but the endpoint of that process. Since it has reached a state, it is a quality, albeit quickly passing.
This prevents the exclusion of transient sensory qualities from the category.
5
Apply the distinction
Practice recognizing affections and passions in new examples: e.g., the bitterness of colocynth (stable) vs. the temporary sweetness of saliva after eating something sweet (transient).
Ensure you can justify why each falls into its respective subtype.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : فذلك ما يكون من المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس نحو الألوان والطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة، وإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون راسخًا أولاً أو لا يكون [1] والراسخ يسمى انفعالية كحلاوة العسل وحمرة الورد ورائحة المسك وحرارة النار مثلا، والانفعاليات إنما سميت بهذا الاسم لوجهين: أحدهما ما يعم جميعًا وهو أن الحواس منفعل عنها، والثاني ما يخص بعضها وهو أنها تحدث عن انفعاليات في موضوعاتها إما في أصل الخلقة كحلاوة العسل وصفرة المصفار، أو بعد الخلقة كملوحة ماء البحر وصفرة مرّية سوء مزاج / في الكبد. [ب] وما لا يكون راسخًا من الكيفيات، فذلك من الانفعالات كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل ونحو ذلك. والانفعالات إنما سميت بهذا الاسم لما فيها من التجدد والتغير وهو الانفعال فيكون هذا الاسم كالمستعار لها. فالانفعاليات مما يشبه المَلَكة، والانفعالات مما يشبه الحال؛ والملكة والحال من جملة ما يعرف في النوع الرابع من بعد. وأما الانفعالات فإنها ما يفهم أنها ليست من الكيفيات، وكيف هى منها والصفرة مثلا إذا لم تكن مستقرة مدة مديدة لم تكن من مقولة الكيف لكنها هي منها ؛ إذ الصفرة ليست بمعنى الاصفرار الذي هو من مقولة أن ينفعل بل هي التي ينتهي إليها الاصفرار، ولما انتهى إليها؛ فقد استقرت هي استقرار صفرة أخرى مما يدوم. وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون هذه الصفرة من جملة ما يكون مستقرا في الجملة لكنه لما كان سريع الزوال فقد سمي بهذا الاسم. فالانفعالات إذن ليست هي انفعالات في
The second type: that which is from the sensibles perceived by the five senses, such as colors, tastes, smells, heat, and cold. And it is inevitable that it is either stable or not stable. The stable is called 'affection' (infi‘āliyya), like the sweetness of honey, the redness of the rose, the scent of musk, and the heat of fire, for example. The affections are so named for two reasons: one general, namely that the senses are affected by them; the second specific to some, namely that they arise from affections in their subjects, either at the origin of creation, like the sweetness of honey and the yellowness of the yolk, or after creation, like the saltiness of seawater and the yellowness of bile due to a bad liver temperament. And what is not stable among the qualities, that is from the 'passions' (infi‘ālāt), like the redness of shame and the yellowness of fear and the like. The passions are so named because of their renewal and change, which is the passion, so this name is like a metaphor for them. So the affections resemble a possession (malaka), and the passions resemble a state (ḥāl). And possession and state are part of what is known in the fourth type later. As for the passions, it is understood that they are not from the qualities. How could they be? For yellowness, for example, if it is not stable for a considerable duration, it is not from the category of quality. But it is from it, because yellowness is not in the sense of 'becoming yellow' which is from the category of being affected, rather it is that which the 'becoming yellow' ends at. And when it ends at it, it has settled as a settling of another yellowness that endures. Then it follows that this yellowness is among that which is stable in general, but because it is quickly passing, it is called by this name. So the passions are not passions in
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of quality: sensible qualities perceived by the five senses. The author distinguishes between 'affections' (infi‘āliyya) which are stable qualities (e.g., sweetness of honey, redness of rose) and 'passions' (infi‘ālāt) which are transient (e.g., blushing, pallor from fear). Affections are so called because the senses are affected by them, and they arise from internal states. Passions are named metaphorically due to their instability. The author clarifies that even transient qualities like a blush of shame are still qualities, not processes (like becoming yellow), because they are the endpoint of a change. The passage defends the inclusion of transient qualities under the category of quality despite their impermanence.

Key Terms
انفعالية (infi‘āliyya): Stable sensible quality, such as sweetness or heat.
انفعالات (infi‘ālāt): Transient sensible qualities, like blushing.
ملكة (malaka): A stable disposition; analogous to affections.
حال (ḥāl): A transient state; analogous to passions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author classify a transient yellowness (e.g., from fear) as a quality rather than a process?
A) Because it is a stable state lasting for a long time.
B) Because it is the endpoint of the process of becoming yellow, not the process itself.
C) Because it is perceived by the senses as a color.
D) Because it is a disposition (malaka).

Key Takeaway
Transient sensible qualities are still qualities because they are the stable endpoints of change, even if brief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sensible Qualities: Stable and Transient
Author defines and subdivides sensible qualities into stable and transient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between stable sensible qualities (infi'āliyya) and transient sensible qualities (infi'ālāt) and explain why transient qualities are still genuine qualities according to the author.

Core Idea
Transient qualities are stable states in essence; only their speed of change earns them the name 'affections'.

After discussing the first type of quality (scientific qualities of the soul), we now turn to the second type: sensible qualities perceived by the five senses. The author subdivides them into stable and transient, but warns us not to be misled by the name 'transient'—these are still genuine qualities that share the nature of stable states. This lesson will help you navigate this subtle distinction and apply it in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus: sensible qualities
Find where the author defines the second type of quality as 'that which is from the sensibles by the five senses' (المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس). This is the broader category under which the subdivision occurs.
2
Extract the differentiae: stability vs. non-stability
The author says these qualities 'are not free from being either stable or not stable' (لا يخلو من أن يكون راسخًا أولاً). Stability (rusūkh) is the key differentia that separates the two sub-species.
Stable = rāsikh (راسخ); non-stable = ghayr rāsikh.
3
Define the stable sub-species: infi'āliyya
The stable qualities are called 'infi'āliyya' (passible qualities). Note the two reasons for the name: (1) senses are affected by them, (2) they arise from affections in the subject. Examples: honey's sweetness, rose's redness.
The term 'infi'āliyya' implies both the effect on the senses and the origin in a passion.
4
Define the transient sub-species: infi'ālāt
The non-stable qualities are called 'infi'ālāt' (transient affections). Examples: redness of shame, yellowness of fear. The author explains that despite the name, they are 'stable states' (hay'āt qārra) like other qualities, but because of their quick change, they are called affections.
Key clarification: infi'ālāt are not 'affections' in the sense of being non-qualities; they are stable in themselves.
5
Analyze the author's argument for genuine quality status
The author gives the example of yellowness: if yellowness is not stable for a long duration, one might think it is not a quality, but it is a quality because it is the end point of the process of becoming yellow (is-firār), and once it terminates, it has stability. Thus, all sensible qualities share the attribute 'stable state', and the distinction is only based on duration.
This resolves the potential misunderstanding that transient qualities are not genuine categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : فذلك ما يكون من المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس نحو الألوان والطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة، وإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون راسخًا أولاً أو لا يكون [1] والراسخ يسمى انفعالية كحلاوة العسل وحمرة الورد ورائحة المسك وحرارة النار مثلا، والانفعاليات إنما سميت بهذا الاسم لوجهين: أحدهما ما يعم جميعًا وهو أن الحواس منفعل عنها، والثاني ما يخص بعضها وهو أنها تحدث عن انفعاليات في موضوعاتها إما في أصل الخلقة كحلاوة العسل وصفرة المصفار، أو بعد الخلقة كملوحة ماء البحر وصفرة مرّية سوء مزاج / في الكبد. [ب] وما لا يكون راسخًا من الكيفيات، فذلك من الانفعالات كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل ونحو ذلك. والانفعالات إنما سميت بهذا الاسم لما فيها من التجدد والتغير وهو الانفعال فيكون هذا الاسم كالمستعار لها. فالانفعاليات مما يشبه المَلَكة، والانفعالات مما يشبه الحال؛ والملكة والحال من جملة ما يعرف في النوع الرابع من بعد. وأما الانفعالات فإنها ما يفهم أنها ليست من الكيفيات، وكيف هى منها والصفرة مثلا إذا لم تكن مستقرة مدة مديدة لم تكن من مقولة الكيف لكنها هي منها ؛ إذ الصفرة ليست بمعنى الاصفرار الذي هو من مقولة أن ينفعل بل هي التي ينتهي إليها الاصفرار، ولما انتهى إليها؛ فقد استقرت هي استقرار صفرة أخرى مما يدوم. وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون هذه الصفرة من جملة ما يكون مستقرا في الجملة لكنه لما كان سريع الزوال فقد سمي بهذا الاسم. فالانفعالات إذن ليست هي انفعالات في أنفسها بل هي هيئات قارة كغيرها من الكيفيات. فإن أنواع الكيفية بجميعها تشترك في أنها هيئات قارة لكن لكثرة الانفعالات العارضة لموضوعاتها سميت انفعالات تمييزاً لها عن غيرها؛ وهو الراسخ. وإنها مع الغير تشتركان في أن كل واحد منهما بحيث تنفعل عنها الحواس وإن كان في البعض مع الرسوخ وفي البعض لا مع الرسوخ على ما عرف ولما كان الانفعال من اللوازم في الكل فلا يتميز البعض منها عن البعض بالانفعال من حيث هو الانفعال مطلقا بل من اللوازم أن يعتبر غيره للتفرقة وهو الرسوخ وعدم الرسوخ. فيكون البعض منها انفعالية
The second type: that which is from the sensibles by the five senses, such as colors, tastes, smells, heat and cold. And it is not free from being either stable or not stable. The stable is called 'infi'aliyya' (passible quality) like the sweetness of honey, the redness of the rose, the scent of musk, the heat of fire, for example. The passible qualities are so named for two reasons: one general to all, that the senses are affected by them; the second specific to some, that they arise from affections in their subjects, either in the original constitution like the sweetness of honey and the yellowness of the yolk, or after constitution like the saltiness of seawater and the yellowness of a jaundice due to a bad temperament of the liver. And what is not stable among the qualities, that is from the 'infi'alaat' (transient affections) like the redness of shame and the yellowness of fear and the like. The transient affections are so named because of the renewal and change in them, which is being affected, so this name is like a loan for them. Thus the passible qualities resemble disposition (malaka), and the transient affections resemble state (haal). Disposition and state are among what will be known in the fourth type later. As for the transient affections, it might be understood that they are not from the qualities—but how could they be from them? For yellowness, for example, if it is not stable for a long duration, it is not from the category of quality; but it is from it, since yellowness is not in the sense of becoming yellow (is-firār) which is from the category of being affected, but rather it is that which the becoming yellow terminates into. And when it terminates into it, it has become stable as another stable yellowness of what endures. Then it necessarily follows that this yellowness is from among what is stable in some respect, but because it is quickly passing, it is given this name. Thus the transient affections are not in themselves affections, but are stable states like other qualities. For all types of quality share in being stable states, but due to the frequent affections (infi'alaat) that occur to their subjects, they are called 'infi'alaat' (transient affections) to distinguish them from others, i.e. the stable one. And they share with the other that each of them is such that the senses are affected by them, although in some with stability and in some without stability, as known. Since being affected (infi'āl) is a necessary concomitant in all, so some of them are not distinguished from others by being affected absolutely, but rather it is necessary to consider something else for differentiation, namely stability and non-stability. Thus some of them are passible qualities.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of quality (al-kayf), which are the sensible qualities perceived by the five senses (colors, tastes, smells, heat, cold). The author subdivides them into stable (rāsikh) and non-stable. The stable are called 'infi'āliyya' (passible qualities) because the senses are passive to them and they often arise from a passion in the subject. Examples include permanent qualities like honey's sweetness or the redness of a rose. The non-stable are called 'infi'ālāt' (transient affections) because they involve renewal and change, like blushing or paleness from fear. The author explains that despite their name, transcendent affections are actually stable states (hay'āt qārra) like other qualities, but because they change quickly, they are termed 'affections'. This is a clarification against a potential misunderstanding that transient qualities are not genuine qualities. The author then notes that both stable and transient qualities share the feature that the senses are affected by them, but they are distinguished by stability and non-stability. Therefore, some are called 'infi'āliyya' (passible qualities) and others 'infi'ālāt' (transient affections), but both are real qualities.

Key Terms

انفعالات
كيفية
راسخ

D) Because they are necessary concomitants of all qualities.

Key Takeaway
Transient sensible qualities are ontologically stable states; their name reflects rapid change, not a lack of genuine qualitative status.
