Roughness and Smoothness: Position, Not Quality
Author argues roughness and smoothness are positions, not qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify roughness (khushūna) and smoothness (mulābasa) into the category of position (waḍʿ) based on the author's definition and reasoning.

Core Idea
Roughness and smoothness are positions, not direct qualities.

This passage clarifies that roughness and smoothness belong to the category of position (waḍʿ), not quality. Roughness is defined as unevenness of parts, smoothness as evenness. They are sensed indirectly through other qualities like hardness or color. Understanding this prevents misclassification in the study of categories.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of roughness (khushūna).
Find where the author defines khushūna as اختلاف الأجزاء في ظاهر الجسم بحسب الارتفاع والانخفاض (difference of parts in the surface according to elevation and depression).
This is a positional definition.
2
Identify the category assigned to roughness.
The author states: وذلك من باب الوضع (that belongs to the category of position).
3
Understand why roughness is not a direct quality.
The author argues that roughness is sensed indirectly through other qualities like hardness or softness. If it were sensed directly, it would be a quality, but it is not.
The same applies to smoothness.
4
Apply the same analysis to smoothness (mulābasa).
Smoothness is defined as استواء الأجزاء في الوضع (evenness of parts in position), also from position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا إذا قلنا كيفيات انفعالية خرجت الخشونة والملابسة؛ إذ الخشونة هي اختلاف الأجزاء في ظاهر الجسم بحسب الارتفاع والانخفاض : وذلك من باب الوضع، والملابسة استواء الأجزاء في الوضع. ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون لا هذه ولا تلك من المحسوسات إلا بواسطة كما يحس المقادير والأبعاد والأشكال. فإن أحست بواسطة صلابة أو لين أو سواد أو بياض مثلا فلا يكون من جملة المحسوسات التي نحن في سبيلها؛ فإنه لا يؤثر في الحس تأثيرًا من جهة نفس الحال العارضة لأجزائها مطلقًا الذي هو الوضع بل لأمر آخر وهو صلابة أو لين مثلت فإن كان الخشونة والملابسة حال تحس بهما في الحقيقة لا بواسطة أمر آخر فتلك الحال هي الكيفية وهي غير الحال العارضة لها من أجزائها وهي الوضع. وأما الثّقل والخفة فقد يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية؛ وذلك باطل. ويظن [09] بهما أنهما / من باب القوة واللا قوة. وهو حق إذا كانت القوى الفعلية تدخل في ذلك الجنس فإنهما من هذا الباب ؛ وهما من جملة المحسوسات ومن جملة ما يحدث في الأجسام بالانفعالات. فإن الجسم يسخن فيخف، ويبرد فيثقل وهو واحد بعينه. وقد يوجد عند اجتماع الأجزاء وزن أقل أو أكثر مما لجملة
But if we say 'passible qualities', roughness and smoothness are excluded; for roughness is the difference of parts in the apparent body according to elevation and depression, and that belongs to the category of position (waḍʿ), and smoothness is the evenness of parts in position. If that is so, then neither these nor those are from the sensibles except indirectly, just as magnitudes, distances, and shapes are sensed. For if they are sensed through hardness or softness or blackness or whiteness, for example, then they are not among the sensibles we are discussing; for it [roughness/smoothness] does not affect the sense directly from the aspect of the very state ('āriḍa) occurring to its parts, which is position, but rather due to another matter, such as hardness or softness, as an example. But if roughness and smoothness are states that are sensed directly without another intermediary, then that state is the quality, and it is other than the state occurring to its parts, which is position. As for heaviness and lightness, it may be thought that they belong to the category of quantity; but that is false. And it is thought that they belong to the category of power and lack of power. That is true if active powers enter into that genus, for they are from this category; and they are among the sensibles and among what occurs in bodies through affections. For the body heats up and becomes light, and cools down and becomes heavy, being the same body. And it may be that when parts are combined, there is a weight less or more than that of the whole.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues regarding qualities. First, the author argues that roughness (khushūna) and smoothness (mulābasa) are not directly sensible qualities but belong to the category of position (waḍʿ). Roughness is defined as unevenness of parts in the surface of a body, and smoothness as evenness. They are sensed indirectly through other qualities like hardness or softness, or through colors. If they were sensed directly, they would be qualities, but that is not the usual case. Second, the author discusses heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa). Some mistakenly think they belong to quantity, but that is false. Rather, they belong to the category of power (quwwa) and lack of power (lā quwwa). They are sensible qualities that arise from affections (e.g., heating makes a body light, cooling makes it heavy). The author also notes that the weight of a composite body may differ from the sum of its parts. This passage clarifies the proper categorization of these phenomena within the Aristotelian framework.

ملابسة
وضع

Key Takeaway
Roughness and smoothness are classified as positions, not direct qualities.

Heaviness and Lightness: Powers, Not Quantities
Author argues heaviness and lightness are powers, not quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa) into the category of power (quwwa) based on the author's argument.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness are sensible qualities belonging to power, not quantity.

The author corrects a common misconception about heaviness and lightness. Some mistakenly place them in the category of quantity, but the author argues they belong to power and lack of power (quwwa wa lā quwwa). They are sensible qualities that arise from heating and cooling, which are affections. This categorization is important for understanding the nature of physical change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception addressed.
The author notes that some think heaviness and lightness belong to quantity. Find that statement in the passage: فقد يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية.
2
State the author's correct classification.
The author asserts they belong to the category of power and lack of power (القوة واللا قوة).
3
Understand the evidence: heating and cooling.
The author gives the example: الجسم يسخن فيخف، ويبرد فيثقل (the body heats up and becomes light, cools and becomes heavy). This shows that heaviness and lightness are powers that change with temperature.
4
Connect to the category of power.
Since active powers are included in that genus, heaviness and lightness are powers.

Passive Qualities as Stable States: The Role of Stability (Rusukh)
Author defines passive qualities and the differentiation criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish passive qualities from other qualities by identifying whether they are stable or non-stable as the differentiating criterion.

Core Idea
Affection alone does not differentiate; stability versus non-stability does.

In this passage, the author clarifies what passive qualities really are. They are stable states like other qualities, but called 'affections' due to the frequency of changes. To tell them apart, we use stability (rusukh). Let's walk through the logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common nature of all qualities
All qualities are stable states (hay'at qarra). The author emphasizes that passive qualities share this.
2
Understand why they are called 'affections'
Despite being stable, they are named after the frequent affections that occur in their subjects, to distinguish them from other qualities.
3
Find the criterion for differentiation within passive qualities
Affection is common to all, so we need another criterion: stability (rusukh) or its absence. Some passive qualities are stable, some are not.
This is the key analytical move.
4
Apply the criterion
Given a candidate passive quality, check if it is sensed with stability or not. That determines its subtype.
For example, heat can be stable (like in fire) or transient (like in water heated on fire).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بحسب الارتفاع والانخفاض : وذلك من باب الوضع، والملابسة استواء الأجزاء في الوضع. ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون لا هذه ولا تلك من المحسوسات إلا بواسطة كما يحس المقادير والأبعاد والأشكال. فإن أحست بواسطة صلابة أو لين أو سواد أو بياض مثلا فلا يكون من جملة المحسوسات التي نحن في سبيلها؛ فإنه لا يؤثر في الحس تأثيرًا من جهة نفس الحال العارضة لأجزائها مطلقًا الذي هو الوضع بل لأمر آخر وهو صلابة أو لين مثلت فإن كان الخشونة والملابسة حال تحس بهما في الحقيقة لا بواسطة أمر آخر فتلك الحال هي الكيفية وهي غير الحال العارضة لها من أجزائها وهي الوضع. وأما الثّقل والخفة فقد يظن بهما أنهما من باب الكمية؛ وذلك باطل. ويظن بهما أنهما من باب القوة واللا قوة. وهو حق إذا كانت القوى الفعلية تدخل في ذلك الجنس فإنهما من هذا الباب ؛ وهما من جملة المحسوسات ومن جملة ما يحدث في الأجسام بالانفعالات. فإن الجسم يسخن فيخف، ويبرد فيثقل وهو واحد بعينه. وقد يوجد عند اجتماع الأجزاء وزن أقل أو أكثر مما لجملة تلك الأجزاء إذا انفعل بعضها ببعض. وأما التخلخل والتكاثف؛ فالتخلخل يذكر ويراد به انفشاش الأجزاء كالصوف المنفوش، ويراد بالتكاثف ما يقابله ويراد به ما يكون أقل التقطيع والتشكل من الأجسام، وبالتكاثف ما يقابله ویراد به قبول المادة حجمًا أكبر، وبالتكاثف ما يقابله وهو قبول المادة حجمًا أصغر. وقد يظن بالثاني والثالث أنهما بمعنى واحد ؛ وذلك غلط فإن النار أشد تخلخلا من الهواء بمعنى زيادة الحجم وليس أقل منه للتشكل والتقطيع. ثم الأول من باب الوضع، والثاني من باب الكيف، والثالث من باب الكم المقارن للإضافة، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر من هذا النوع.
Rather, they are stable states like other qualities. For all types of quality share in being stable states, but due to the many affections befalling their subjects, they were called affections to distinguish them from others; and that is the stable. And they and the other share in that each one of them is such that the senses are affected by them, although in some with stability and in some without stability as known. And since affection is a concomitant in all, one part is not distinguished from another by affection as such absolutely, but it is necessary from the concomitants to consider something else for differentiation, namely stability and non-stability. Thus some of them are affective, but when we say 'affective qualities', roughness and smoothness are excluded; for roughness is the difference of parts on the surface of the body in terms of height and depth: that belongs to position, and smoothness is the evenness of parts in position. If so, then neither of these is sensible except by mediation, just as magnitudes, dimensions, and shapes are sensed. For if they are sensed by mediation of hardness or softness or blackness or whiteness, for example, then they are not among the sensibles we are treating; for it does not affect the senses by virtue of the very state pertaining to its parts absolutely, which is position, but by another matter, namely hardness or softness as supposed. So if roughness and smoothness are states sensed in reality without mediation of another matter, then that state is the quality, and it is other than the state pertaining to its parts, which is position. As for heaviness and lightness, it might be thought that they belong to quantity; that is false. And it might be thought that they belong to power and lack of power. That is true if actual powers enter into that genus; then they belong to this category. They are among the sensibles and among what occurs in bodies through affections. For a body becomes hot and becomes light, and becomes cold and becomes heavy, while it is one and the same. And sometimes there occurs, upon the aggregation of parts, a weight less or more than the sum of those parts when some act on others. As for rarefaction and condensation: rarefaction is mentioned and intended as the scattering of parts like fluffed cotton, and by condensation is meant its opposite, and it is intended as that which is less in division and shaping of bodies, and by condensation its opposite, and it is intended as the matter receiving a larger volume, and by condensation its opposite, which is the matter receiving a smaller volume. And it might be thought that the second and third are synonymous; that is a mistake, for fire is more rarefied than air in the sense of increased volume, not less than it in shaping and division. Then the first belongs to position, the second to quality, and the third to quantity relative to relation. And we restrict ourselves to this much of this type.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines and refines the classification of passive qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-inf'aliyya). The author begins by stating that passive qualities are in fact stable states (hay'at qarra) like other qualities, but due to the frequency of affections befalling their subjects, they are specifically called affections to distinguish them. He then explains that the distinction among passive qualities lies in stability (rusukh) or its absence: some affect the senses with stability, others without. However, since affection is common to all, one must use another criterion for differentiation, namely whether the quality is stable or not. The author then excludes roughness (khushuna) and smoothness (mulabasa) from the category of passive qualities altogether, arguing that they belong to the category of position (wad'), because roughness is a difference in height and depth of parts, and smoothness is evenness of parts—both are positional, not qualitative. He supports this by noting that if these were sensed without mediation, they would be qualities, but they are usually sensed through other qualities like hardness or softness. Next, the author addresses heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa). He rejects the view that they belong to quantity and affirms that they belong to the category of power (quwwa) and lack of power, since they are produced by affections (e.g., heating causes lightness, cooling causes heaviness). Finally, he discusses rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf), noting that the term 'rarefaction' can refer to three different meanings: (1) scattering of parts (positional), (2) less division and shaping (qualitative), (3) larger volume (relative quantity). He warns against confusing the second and third meanings, using the example that fire is more rarefied than air in volume but not in division. Thus he distinguishes these categories carefully.

Key Terms
كيفية
انفعال
رسوخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion to distinguish among passive qualities themselves?
A) The intensity of the affection
B) Stability (rusukh) or non-stability
C) The number of senses affected
D) The duration of the affection

Key Takeaway
Passive qualities are stable states that affect the senses; they are differentiated by whether the affection is stable or transient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following list of sensible affections: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, pain, pleasure. For each, determine whether it is typically a stable passive quality (rasikh) or a non-stable one (ghayr rasikh). Provide reasoning based on the text.
?
Classify each of the following as a quality (kayfiyya) or not, using the criterion of being a stable state: (a) the shape of a statue, (b) the whiteness of snow, (c) the roughness of sandpaper, (d) the weight of a stone.

Roughness and Smoothness: Positional, Not Qualitative
Author excludes roughness and smoothness from qualities, classifying them as positional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why roughness and smoothness belong to the category of position (wad') rather than quality, and evaluate the logical argument for this reclassification.

Core Idea
Roughness is a difference in height – that's position, not quality.

The author now excludes roughness (khushuna) and smoothness (mulabasa) from the category of passive qualities. They are not qualities at all – they belong to position (wad'). Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Define roughness and smoothness as given in the passage
Roughness is the difference of parts on a surface by height and depth; smoothness is evenness of parts in position.
These are definitions that explicitly involve arrangement.
2
Identify the category they belong to
The author says this is from the category of position (min bab al-wad').
Position is a distinct category from quality.
3
Understand the mediation argument
If roughness/smoothness were sensed directly, they would be qualities. But they are sensed through other qualities like hardness or softness. Therefore, they are not directly sensible qualities.
This is a logical argument: if sensed via mediation, then the perceived state is the mediating quality, not the positional arrangement.
4
Consider the counterexample
If roughness is sensed without mediation, then that state would be a quality, separate from the positional arrangement. But the author seems to imply that in reality, we always sense roughness via other qualities.
The passage leaves open the possibility that if it were directly sensed, it would be a quality.

Heaviness and Lightness: Power, Not Quantity
Author classifies heaviness and lightness as powers, not quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why heaviness and lightness belong to the category of power (quwwa) and reject the mistaken view that they are quantities.

Core Idea
Heaviness comes from cooling; it's a power, not a quantity.

The author addresses two common misconceptions about heaviness and lightness: that they are quantities, and that they are powers. He endorses the latter and provides evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views mentioned
Some think heaviness and lightness are quantities (bāb al-kammiyya); others think they are powers (bāb al-quwwa wa la quwwa). The author says the first is false, the second is true.
2
Understand the evidence for the second view
The author gives an example: a body becomes hot and light, cold and heavy. This shows that heaviness and lightness are produced by affections (heating/cooling), so they are in the category of power and lack of power.
Power here refers to the ability to affect or be affected.
3
Consider the weight change upon aggregation
Sometimes when parts act on each other, the total weight changes. This indicates that heaviness is not a fixed quantity but a result of powers.
This is a subtle empirical observation.

He then explains that the distinction among passive qualities lies in stability (rusukh) or its absence: some affect the senses with stability, others without. However, since affection is common to all, one must use another criterion for differentiation, namely whether the quality is stable or not. The author then excludes roughness (khushuna) and smoothness (mulabasa) from the category of passive qualities altogether, arguing that they belong to the category of position (wad'), because roughness is a difference in height and depth of parts, and smoothness is evenness of parts—both are positional, not qualitative. He supports this by noting that if these were sensed without mediation, they would be qualities, but they are usually sensed through other qualities like hardness or softness. Next, the author addresses heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa). He rejects the view that they belong to quantity and affirms that they belong to the category of power (quwwa) and lack of power, since they are produced by affections (e.g., heating causes lightness, cooling causes heaviness). Finally, he discusses rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf), noting that the term 'rarefaction' can refer to three different meanings: (1) scattering of parts (positional), (2) less division and shaping (qualitative), (3) larger volume (relative quantity). He warns against confusing the second and third meanings, using the example that fire is more rarefied than air in volume but not in division. Thus he distinguishes these categories carefully.

قوة
لا قوة

Key Takeaway
Heaviness and lightness are powers (quwwa) because they result from heating and cooling, not fixed quantities.

Rarefaction and Condensation: A Triple Category
Author distinguishes three meanings of rarefaction and condensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of rarefaction (takhalkhul) and assign each to its correct category (position, quality, quantity).

Core Idea
Rarefaction can be positional, qualitative, or quantitative – don't confuse them.

The author concludes by analyzing the terms rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf), which have multiple meanings. He warns against mixing them up and clarifies the category of each.

Guided Path
1
List the three meanings of rarefaction
1) Scattering of parts (like fluffed cotton). 2) Less division and shaping. 3) Larger volume.
Condensation is the opposite of each.
2
Assign each meaning to a category
Meaning 1 belongs to position (wad'), meaning 2 to quality (kayfiyya), meaning 3 to relative quantity (kamm al-muqaran li al-idafa).
This is a key classification.
3
Recognize the common mistake
Some think meaning 2 (less division) and meaning 3 (larger volume) are the same. The author shows they are different using fire and air: fire is more rarefied in volume (3) but not less in division (2).
This is an empirical test.

Rather, they are stable states like other qualities. For all types of quality share in being stable states, but due to the many affections befalling their subjects, they were called affections to distinguish them from others; and that is the stable. And they and the other share in that each one of them is such that the senses are affected by them, although in some with stability and in some without stability as known. And since affection is a concomitant in all, one part is not distinguished from another by affection as such absolutely, but it is necessary from the concomitants to consider something else for differentiation, namely stability and non-stability. Thus some of them are affective, but when we say 'affective qualities', roughness and smoothness are excluded; for roughness is the difference of parts on the surface of the body in terms of height and depth: that belongs to position, and smoothness is the evenness of parts in position. If so, then neither of these is sensible except by mediation, just as magnitudes, dimensions, and shapes are sensed. For if they are sensed by mediation of hardness or softness or blackness or whiteness, for example, then they are not among the sensibles we are treating; for it does not affect the senses by virtue of the very state pertaining to its parts absolutely, which is position, but by another matter, namely hardness or softness as supposed. So if roughness and smoothness are states sensed in reality without mediation of another matter, then that state is the quality, and it is other than the state pertaining to its parts, which is position. As for heaviness and lightness, it might be thought that they belong to quantity; that is false. And it might be thought that they belong to power and lack of power. That is true if actual powers enter into that genus; then they belong to this category. They are among the sensibles and among what occurs in bodies through affections. For a body becomes hot and becomes light, and becomes cold and becomes heavy, while it is one and the same. And sometimes there occurs, upon the aggregation of parts, a weight less or more than the sum of those parts when some act on others. As for rarefaction and condensation: rarefaction is mentioned and intended as the scattering of parts like fluffed cotton, and by condensation is meant its opposite, and it is intended as that which is less in division and shaping of bodies, and by condensation its opposite, and it is intended as the matter receiving a larger volume, and by condensation its opposite, which is the matter receiving a smaller volume. And it might be thought that the second and third are synonymous; that is a mistake, for fire is more rarefied than air in the sense of increased volume, not less than it in shaping and division. Then the first belongs to position, the second to quality, and the third to quantity relative to relation. And we restrict ourselves to this much of this type.

Commentary
This passage defines and refines the classification of passive qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-inf'aliyya). The author begins by stating that passive qualities are in fact stable states (hay'at qarra) like other qualities, but due to the frequency of affections befalling their subjects, they are specifically called affections to distinguish them. He then explains that the distinction among passive qualities lies in stability (rusukh) or its absence: some affect the senses with stability, others without. However, since affection is common to all, one must use another criterion for differentiation, namely whether the quality is stable or not. The author then excludes roughness (khushuna) and smoothness (mulabasa) from the category of passive qualities altogether, arguing that they belong to the category of position (wad'), because roughness is a difference in height and depth of parts, and smoothness is evenness of parts—both are positional, not qualitative. He supports this by noting that if these were sensed without mediation, they would be qualities, but they are usually sensed through other qualities like hardness or softness. Next, the author addresses heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa). He rejects the view that they belong to quantity and affirms that they belong to the category of power (quwwa) and lack of power, since they are produced by affections (e.g., heating causes lightness, cooling causes heaviness). Finally, he discusses rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf), noting that the term 'rarefaction' can refer to three different meanings: (1) scattering of parts (positional), (2) less division and shaping (qualitative), (3) larger volume (relative quantity). He warns against confusing the second and third meanings, using the example that fire is more rarefied than air in volume but not in division. Thus he distinguishes these categories carefully.

Dispositional Qualities: Natural Power vs Lack of Power
Author defines the third type of quality as dispositions for perfections, including natural power and lack of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural power (quwwa tabi'iyya) from natural lack of power (la quwwa tabi'iyya) in any given dispositional quality and identify whether it involves resistance or acceptance of affection.

Core Idea
Dispositional qualities are not mere potentiality but specific inclinations toward one opposite.

In this passage, the author introduces the third category of quality: dispositional qualities (al-isti'dadiyya). These are not directly sensible but prepare the body to receive perfections. He then divides them into natural power, which resists affection, and natural lack of power, which readily accepts it. This distinction is crucial for understanding how qualities can be specific tendencies rather than empty potentials, as seen in examples like healthiness versus proneness to illness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and species
The passage defines a type of quality. The broader genus is al-kayfiyya (quality). The species is al-isti'dadiyya (dispositional). Note that it is not sensible (laysa min al-mahsusat).
Dispositional qualities are not directly perceived; they are inferred from effects.
2
Distinguish the two subdivisions
Natural power (quwwa tabi'iyya) is defined as a disposition for resistance (al-muqawama) against affection (al-infi'al). Natural lack of power (la quwwa tabi'iyya) is a disposition for quick submission (al-idh'an) and affection. Find the Arabic terms: استعداد للمقاومة والإناء عن الانفعال and استعداد السرعة الإذعان والانفعال.
The Arabic text includes 'الإناء' which is likely a scribal error; the meaning is clear: resistance vs. acceptance.
3
Analyze the examples
For natural power, examples are healthiness (musahhahiya) and hardness (salaaba). These make the body not accept disease or being pressed. For natural lack, examples are proneness to illness (mumarrada) and softness (layn). These make the body quickly accept disease or being pressed. Understand each example in terms of resistance/acceptance.
Healthiness resists disease; hardness resists pressure. Proneness to illness readily accepts disease; softness readily accepts pressure.
4
Note what this power is not
The author explicitly excludes two things: the potentiality of prime matter (al-quwwa allati fi al-madda al-ula) and divine power (quwwat al-jawad). The power here is a specific inclination toward one opposite, not mere possibility.
Prime matter potentiality is neutral; dispositional power is weighted toward one side.
5
Test the definition
Does the definition of natural power as 'disposition for resistance' cover healthiness and hardness? Yes, because health resists disease, hardness resists pressing. Does it exclude what it should? Yes, because it excludes the mere absence of disease/pressing. Similarly for natural lack: proneness to illness and softness involve quick acceptance, which matches the definition.
The definition is complete for these examples; it distinguishes quality from privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث: فذلك من الكيفيات ما يكون الجسم بواسطته مستعدا لقبول الكمالات؛ وإنه لا يكون من المحسوسات البتة. وهذا الاستعداد إنما يتصور في النفس بالقياس إلى الكمالات؛ فإن كان استعدادًا للمقاومة والإناء عن الانفعال سمي قوة طبيعية كالمصحاحية والصلابة. وتلك هي الهيئة التي بها صار الجسم لا يقبل المرض ولا يقبل الانغماز لا نفس عدم المرض والانغماز. وإن كان استعداد السرعة الإذعان؛ والانفعال سمي لا قوة طبيعية مثل الممراضية واللين وهي أيضًا هيئة بها يسرع قبول الجسم للمرض. والانغماز لا نفس القبول ولا يعنى بهذه القوة القوة التي هي في المادة الأولى ولا قوة الجواد. فإن كان كل إنسان بتلك القوة مستعدا للصحة والمرض وإنه بالقوة صحيح ومريض كذلك لكن تتميز هذه القوة وهي ترجحها من جهة أحد طرفي النقيض فلا تكون في قوة الشيء أن يقبل المرض أو أن لا يقبل فقط بل أن يكون قد يرجح قبول المرض على لا قبوله أو لا قبوله على قبوله. وكما أن المصحاحية والممراضية من هذا الباب فكذلك الهيئة المصارعية
The third type: that among the qualities by which the body is disposed to receive perfections; and it is never among the sensibles. This disposition is only conceived in the soul in relation to perfections; if it is a disposition for resistance and [?] against affection, it is called natural power, like healthiness and hardness. And that is the state by which the body becomes not receptive to illness and not receptive to being pressed, not the very absence of illness and pressing. And if it is a disposition for quick submission and affection, it is called natural lack of power, like being prone to illness and softness, and it is also a state by which the body quickly receives illness and pressing, not the reception itself. And by this power is not meant the power that is in prime matter, nor the power of the generous. For every human is by that power disposed for health and illness, and he is potentially healthy and sick as such, but this power is distinguished by its inclination toward one of the two opposite sides, so that it is not merely the power of a thing to accept illness or not, but rather it may incline toward accepting illness over not accepting it, or not accepting it over accepting it. And just as healthiness and proneness to illness are of this category, so too the wrestling disposition [?].
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of quality, namely dispositional qualities (al-isti'dadiyya). These are states in a body that prepare it to receive perfections or their opposites, and they are not sensible at all. The author explains that disposition is conceived in relation to the perfections it leads to. He then subdivides them into two: (1) natural power (quwwa tabi'iyya), which is a disposition for resistance against affection, such as health (sihha) and hardness (salaaba). These are states that make the body not accept disease or being pressed, not simply the absence of disease or pressing. (2) Natural lack of power (la quwwa tabi'iyya), which is a disposition for quick submission and affection, such as being prone to illness (mumarrada) and softness (layn). These are states that make the body quickly accept disease or being pressed. The author clarifies that 'power' here does not refer to the potentiality of prime matter (al-quwwa al-ula) nor to the power of the generous (perhaps a reference to divine power?), but rather to a specific inclination toward one opposite over the other. For example, every human is potentially healthy and sick, but the dispositional quality (like being prone to illness) makes one more likely to become sick than healthy. This distinction is crucial for understanding how qualities can be specific tendencies rather than mere potentials. The passage ends with an example of a wrestling disposition (al-hay'a al-musari'a), though the text is cut off.

Three Meanings of Rarefaction and Condensation
Author clarifies three meanings of rarefaction and condensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of takhkhul and takathuf and identify which Aristotelian category each belongs to.

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation have three distinct meanings corresponding to different categories.

In this passage, the author clarifies that the terms 'rarefaction' (takhkhul) and 'condensation' (takathuf) are not univocal. They have three distinct meanings, each belonging to a different category: position (wad'), quality (kayf), and quantity (kamm). Failing to distinguish these leads to errors in reasoning about physical bodies, as illustrated by the example of fire and air.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning
Locate the first meaning of takhkhul: 'infishāsh al-ajzā'' (loosening of parts), like fluffed wool. Its opposite is the first meaning of takathuf: denseness in forming and cutting. Note that this meaning falls under the category of position (wad').
Wad' concerns how parts are arranged or situated.
2
Identify the second meaning
Find the second meaning: takhkhul as occupying a larger volume (accepting a larger size). Its opposite is takathuf as occupying a smaller volume. This belongs to the category of quality (kayf).
Kayf here refers to a qualitative state like size or shape.
3
Identify the third meaning
The third meaning is takhkhul as matter accepting a larger volume, but now considered as quantity (kamm) relative to relation (idāfa). The opposite is matter accepting a smaller volume.
Kamm here is quantity in the sense of capacity, not just size.
4
Apply the distinction with the fire and air example
The author says fire is more rarefied than air in the second sense (greater volume) but not in the first sense (forming/cutting). This shows that the second and third meanings are not synonymous: fire has more volume (second) but not necessarily less density (third? Actually the third is about accepting larger volume, which fire does, so maybe the example distinguishes second from first. Confirm: the author says one might think second and third are same, but they are not because fire is more rarefied in sense 2 but not in sense 1. Actually the example contrasts 2 and 1, but the warning is about confusing 2 and 3. So the example shows that fire can have a higher degree in sense 2 (volume) but not in sense 1 (looseness). The third sense is about capacity to accept volume, which is a different category.
The example illustrates that a single entity can have different rarefaction degrees in different senses.
5
Assign each meaning to its category
The author explicitly assigns: first meaning → wad' (position), second meaning → kayf (quality), third meaning → kamm (quantity) in relation to idāfa (relation). Memorize this mapping.
These categories come from Aristotle's ten categories, adapted in Islamic logic.

Those parts when some affect others. As for rarefaction and condensation: rarefaction is mentioned and meant by it the loosening of parts like fluffed wool, and by condensation is meant its opposite, and it is meant by it what is less in cutting and forming of bodies, and by condensation its opposite and it is meant by it the acceptance of a larger volume by matter, and by condensation its opposite and it is the acceptance of a smaller volume by matter. And one may think the second and third are synonymous; that is a mistake, for fire is more rarefied than air in the sense of increased volume and not less in forming and cutting. Then the first belongs to the category of position (wad'), the second to the category of quality (kayf), the third to the category of quantity (kamm) relative to relation (idafa). And we suffice with this amount of this type. (1) Supposed? And its form: 'and not what about'?
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a clarificatory note on the terms rarefaction (takhkhul) and condensation (takathuf). The author identifies three distinct meanings for each, warning that confusing the second and third meanings is a common error. The first meaning relates to the category of position (wad'), referring to the loosening or denseness of parts. The second meaning belongs to the category of quality (kayf), concerning the size of the body in terms of volume. The third meaning is in the category of quantity (kamm) but associated with relation (idafa), as it involves matter accepting a larger or smaller volume. The example of fire and air illustrates that fire can be more rarefied in the second sense (greater volume) but not necessarily less in forming (first sense). This precision is typical in logical discussions of categories to avoid equivocation.

Key Terms
التخلخل (takhakhkhul): Here it has three meanings: 1) loosening of parts (wad'), 2) occupying larger volume (kayf), 3) accepting larger volume (kamm). [Note: In later physics, it often means decrease in density specifically.]
التكاثف (takāthuf): Opposite of takhkhul: 1) denseness in forming, 2) smaller volume, 3) accepting smaller volume.

Key Takeaway
Rarefaction and condensation each have three distinct meanings tied to different Aristotelian categories: wad', kayf, and kamm (with idafa).

The Third Type of Quality: Disposition (Isti'dad)
Author defines the third type of quality as disposition and divides it into natural and non-natural power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural power (quwwa tabi'iyya) from non-power (la quwwa) based on their orientation toward resistance or submission, and identify the definitional components (genus and differentia) of each.

Core Idea
Disposition is a quality that preponderates toward one of two opposites—resistance or submission.

We have already encountered the first two types of quality (sensation and innate inclinations). Now the author turns to the third category: disposition (isti'dad). Unlike the other types, this quality is not directly perceptible; rather, it is a condition that makes the body strongly inclined toward accepting or resisting perfections. This lesson dissects the definition of disposition and its two subdivisions—natural power (quwwa tabi'iyya) and non-power (la quwwa)—using specific examples from medicine and the physical arts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author defines the third type of quality (al-naw' al-thalith) as 'that by which the body is disposed to accept perfections.' Pinpoint what is being defined: disposition (al-isti'dad).
The definiendum is not directly a term but the concept of disposition as a quality.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'quality' (kayfiyya). The passage states that this is among the qualities that provide a disposition. So the genus is the category of quality.
3
Extract the differentia for each subdivision
For natural power, the differentia is 'resistance and non-susceptibility to being affected' (e.g., healthiness, hardness). For non-power, it is 'speed of submission and being affected' (e.g., sickliness, softness). These differentiae distinguish the two types within the genus of dispositional quality.
The author explicitly pairs each type with examples.
4
Compare with potentiality
Note that the author distinguishes this power from the mere potentiality in prime matter. This quality is an actual condition that preponderates one side of a contradiction, not a neutral possibility.
This avoids confusion with the potentiality sense of 'power'.
5
Apply the definition to wrestling power
Consider the wrestling power (quwwa musara'iyya) as a composite example that involves bodily, motive, and perceptive aspects. Determine whether it fits as natural or non-power based on the criteria. The author suggests it may be homonymous, requiring further reflection.
This shows the definition's limits and the need for careful analysis.

النوع الثالث: فذلك من الكيفيات ما يكون الجسم بواسطته مستعدا لقبول الكمالات؛ وإنه لا يكون من المحسوسات البتة. وهذا الاستعداد إنما يتصور في النفس بالقياس إلى الكمالات؛ فإن كان استعدادًا للمقاومة والإناء عن (۱) الانفعال سمي قوة طبيعية كالمصحاحية والصلابة. وتلك هي الهيئة التي بها صار الجسم لا يقبل المرض ولا يقبل الانغماز لا نفس عدم المرض والانغماز. وإن كان استعداد السرعة الإذعان؛ والانفعال سمي لا قوة طبيعية مثل الممراضية واللين وهي أيضًا هيئة بها يسرع قبول الجسم للمرض. والانغماز لا نفس القبول ولا يعنى بهذه القوة القوة التي هي في المادة الأولى ولا قوة الجواد. فإن كان كل إنسان بتلك القوة مستعدا للصحة والمرض وإنه بالقوة صحيح ومريض كذلك لكن تتميز هذه القوة وهي ترجحها من جهة أحد طرفي النقيض فلا تكون في قوة الشيء أن يقبل المرض أو أن لا يقبل فقط بل أن يكون قد يرجح قبول المرض على لا قبوله أو لا قبوله على قبوله. وكما أن المصحاحية والممراضية من هذا الباب فكذلك الهيئة المصارعية  والهيئة الانصراعية لكن الاستعداد في هذا الباب من جملة ما فيه نظر لأنه إما أن يكون استعدادًا شديدًا على أن ينفعل الممراضية أو استعدادًا شديدا على أن ينفعل كالمصارعية أو استعدادًا شديدًا على أن ينفعل المصحاحية. والصلابة وقبول القوة على هذه الثلاثة قريب من أن يكون على سبيل اشتراك الاسم يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى. [ ٦٠ و] ثم المعنى من القوة المصارعية ليس القوة المحركة / النفسانية التي هي جوهر حتى لا تكون قابلة للأشد والأضعف بل هي كمال لتلك القوة من جهة مواتاة الأعضاء نسبتها إليها نسبة شدة الذكاء والفهم إلى النفس الناطقة. وأما المصارعية فإنها متعلقة بثلاثة أمور : أمر في البدن وأمر في القوة المحركة وأمر في القوة الدراكة. أما ما يتعلق بالقوة الدراكة فهو (۱) معرفة صناعية تحمل المصارعة. وبالجملة هو صنف من أصناف المعرفة بكيفية أفعال تتعلق بالحركة. وأما ما يتعلق بالقوة المحركة فهو ملكة يحسن بها تصريف العمل على إدراك الغرض في المصارعة. وأما الباقي وهو الأمر البدني فذلك كون الأعضاء في خلقتها الطبيعية بحيث يعسر عطفها ونقلها؛ فهذا هو من هذا الباب وهو جزء من أجزاء كمال صناعة المصارعة الطبيعية. وبالجملة فقد تقرر أن هذا الجنس هو استكمال استعداد أحد طرفي ما عليه القوة التي بمعنى الجواز حتى يكون شديد الاستعداد لوجود ما إذا وجد كان انفعالا بالفعل كالممراضية أو شديد الاستعداد لأن لا يوجد فيه كالمصحاحية. والأوجز منه أن يقال هذه القوة إما أن تستكمل نحو التغير عن الحالة الطبيعية وهو اللَّا قوة، وإما أن تستكمل نحو اللا تغير وهو القوة الطبيعية. (۱) النسخة فهي. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
The third type: among the qualities are those by which the body is disposed to accept perfections; and it is not among the perceptibles at all. This disposition is conceived in the soul relative to perfections. If it is a disposition for resistance and non-susceptibility to being affected, it is called natural power, like healthiness and hardness. That is the condition by which the body becomes not susceptible to illness and not susceptible to compression — not the mere absence of illness and compression. If it is a disposition for the speed of submission and being affected, it is called non-natural power, like sickliness and softness, which is also a condition by which the body quickly accepts illness and compression — not the acceptance itself. And this power does not mean the power that is in prime matter nor the power of the soul. For every human by that power is disposed to health and illness, and he is potentially healthy and ill likewise, but this power is distinguished by its being preponderant towards one of the two opposites, so it is not in the power of a thing to accept illness or not accept it only, but rather it may have a preponderance of accepting illness over not accepting it or not accepting it over accepting it. And just as healthiness and sickliness are of this category, so too the condition of being a wrestler (musara'iyya) and the condition of being non-wrestler (insara'iyya). But the disposition in this category is among that which requires reflection, because it is either a strong disposition to be affected like sickliness, or a strong disposition to affect like the wrestling condition? Or a strong disposition to be affected like healthiness. And hardness and the acceptance of power upon these three are close to being homonymous, as will be known by reflection, God willing. Then the meaning of wrestling power is not the moving psychic power which is a substance so that it is not susceptible to more and less, but rather it is a perfection of that power by way of the suitability of the organs, its relation to it being like the relation of sharpness of intelligence and understanding to the rational soul. As for non-wrestling, it is related to three things: a bodily matter, a matter in the motive power, and a matter in the perceptive power. As for what relates to the perceptive power, it is a technical knowledge that carries wrestling. In summary, it is a type of knowledge of how actions pertaining to movement. As for what relates to the motive power, it is a faculty by which the execution of the work is well managed according to the aim in wrestling. As for the remaining, which is the bodily matter, it is that the organs are in their natural creation such that their bending and movement are difficult. So this is of this category and is a part of the perfection of the natural art of wrestling. In summary, it has been established that this genus is the completion of the disposition of one of the two sides of that power which is in the sense of possibility, so that it becomes strongly disposed for the existence of something which, when it exists, would be an actual affect, like sickliness, or strongly disposed for it not to exist in it, like healthiness. A more concise formulation: this power either completes towards change from the natural state — that is non-power (la quwwa) — or it completes towards non-change — that is natural power. (1) The manuscript has 'فهي'. What we have established is correct, as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of quality (kayfiyya) as disposition (isti'dad) for perfections. It is not perceptible but conceptualized in the soul. The author divides it into natural power (quwwa ṭabīʿiyya), which is a disposition for resistance (e.g., healthiness making the body resistant to illness), and non-power (lā quwwa), a disposition for quick submission (e.g., sickliness making it prone to illness). Both are actual qualities that preponderate one side of a contradiction, not mere potentialities like prime matter or the soul's power. The discussion extends to wrestling power (quwwa muṣāraʿiyya) as a special case: it is not a substance but a perfection of the motive power through bodily suitability and technical knowledge. The author concludes by summarizing that this genus is the completion of a disposition towards one of two extremes (change or non-change relative to the natural state). Examples drawn from medicine (health/sickness) and physical arts (wrestling) illustrate the point.

Key Takeaway
The third type of quality, disposition, is an actual condition that preponderates toward either resistance (natural power) or submission (non-power), and must be distinguished from mere potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Power as a Dispositional Quality: The Threefold Division
Author defines quwwa and its three dispositional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus (quwwa) and the three species of dispositional preparedness (mumaridiyya, musari'iyya, musahhahiyya) and explain how they relate to the concept of intensity.

Core Idea
Power (quwwa) is a dispositional quality, not a substance; it admits intensity and has three types.

In this passage, the author examines the concept of power (quwwa) within the category of quality. He distinguishes three types of strong preparedness (isti'dad) and clarifies that the power of wrestling (musari'iyya) is a perfection of the psychic motive power, not a separate substance. By working through this passage, you will learn to parse a definition that involves genus, species, and differentiae.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
The broader category being discussed is quwwa, i.e., power in the sense of potentiality or capacity. Note that the author treats it as a quality, not a substance.
2
Extract the three species
The author mentions three types of strong preparedness: mumaridiyya (sickliness), musari'iyya (wrestling), and musahhahiyya (healthiness). Each is a specific direction of potential change or stability.
These are technical coinages in this treatise.
3
Understand the commonality
All three are 'strong preparedness' for a certain kind of change (sickliness) or stability (healthiness) or a specific action (wrestling). They share the genus of being dispositional qualities.
4
Analyze the wrestling power clarification
The author explicitly denies that wrestling power is a substance; rather, it is a perfection of the psychic motive power, analogous to how intensity of intelligence perfects the rational soul.
This prevents a common mistake of reifying dispositions.
5
Summarize the concise definition
The author concludes that power either perfects toward change (non-power) or toward non-change (natural power). This captures the essence of the genus and its two main directions.
This is a hadd-like formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والهيئة الانصراعية لكن الاستعداد في هذا الباب من جملة ما فيه نظر لأنه إما أن يكون استعدادًا شديدًا على أن ينفعل الممراضية أو استعدادًا شديدا على أن ينفعل كالمصارعية أو استعدادًا شديدًا على أن ينفعل المصحاحية. والصلابة وقبول القوة على هذه الثلاثة قريب من أن يكون على سبيل اشتراك الاسم يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى. ثم المعنى من القوة المصارعية ليس القوة المحركة النفسانية التي هي جوهر حتى لا تكون قابلة للأشد والأضعف بل هي كمال لتلك القوة من جهة مواتاة الأعضاء نسبتها إليها نسبة شدة الذكاء والفهم إلى النفس الناطقة. وأما المصارعية فإنها متعلقة بثلاثة أمور: أمر في البدن وأمر في القوة المحركة وأمر في القوة الدراكة. أما ما يتعلق بالقوة الدراكة فهو معرفة صناعية تحمل المصارعة. وبالجملة هو صنف من أصناف المعرفة بكيفية أفعال تتعلق بالحركة. وأما ما يتعلق بالقوة المحركة فهو ملكة يحسن بها تصريف العمل على إدراك الغرض في المصارعة. وأما الباقي وهو الأمر البدني فذلك كون الأعضاء في خلقتها الطبيعية بحيث يعسر عطفها ونقلها؛ فهذا هو من هذا الباب وهو جزء من أجزاء كمال صناعة المصارعة الطبيعية. وبالجملة فقد تقرر أن هذا الجنس هو استكمال استعداد أحد طرفي ما عليه القوة التي بمعنى الجواز حتى يكون شديد الاستعداد لوجود ما إذا وجد كان انفعالا بالفعل كالممراضية أو شديد الاستعداد لأن لا يوجد فيه كالمصحاحية. والأوجز منه أن يقال هذه القوة إما أن تستكمل نحو التغير عن الحالة الطبيعية وهو اللَّا قوة، وإما أن تستكمل نحو اللا تغير وهو القوة الطبيعية.
And the dispositive state, but the preparedness in this topic is among that which requires consideration, because it is either a strong preparedness for the sickliness to be acted upon, or a strong preparedness for it to be acted upon like the wrestling [ability], or a strong preparedness for the healthiness to be acted upon. And solidity and acceptance of power upon these three is close to being by way of homonymy, known by reflection, God willing. Then the meaning of the power of wrestling is not the psychic motive power which is a substance such that it is not susceptible to more and less, but rather it is a perfection of that power from the aspect of the compliance of the limbs, its relation to it being like the relation of the intensity of intelligence and understanding to the rational soul. And as for wrestling, it pertains to three things: a thing in the body, a thing in the motive power, and a thing in the perceptual power. As for what pertains to the perceptual power, it is an artful knowledge that carries wrestling. In summary, it is a kind of knowledge of how to perform actions related to movement. As for what pertains to the motive power, it is a disposition (malaka) by which the execution of the work is improved for achieving the purpose in wrestling. As for the remainder, which is the bodily matter, it is that the limbs are in their natural constitution such that it is difficult to bend and move them; so this is from this topic and it is a part of the perfection of the natural art of wrestling. In summary, it has been established that this genus is the perfecting of the preparedness of one of the two sides of what the power in the sense of possibility is upon, such that it is strongly prepared for the existence of that which, if it exists, would be an actual passion like sickliness, or strongly prepared for not existing in it like healthiness. The more concise than this is to say: This power either perfects itself toward change from the natural state, and that is the non-power, or it perfects itself toward non-change, and that is the natural power.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of power (quwwa) within the context of the category of quality, specifically focusing on dispositional states. The author begins by noting that preparedness (isti'dad) can be of three types: strong preparedness for being acted upon resulting in sickliness (mumaridiyya), strong preparedness for being acted upon in the context of wrestling (musari'iyya), and strong preparedness for being acted upon resulting in healthiness (musahhahiyya). The author remarks that the terms 'solidity' and 'acceptance of power' may be applied to these three only by homonymy, requiring careful reflection. Next, the author clarifies that the power of wrestling is not the psychic motive power (which is a substance), but rather a perfection of that power arising from the compliance of the limbs, analogous to the intensity of intelligence in the rational soul. Wrestling involves three aspects: bodily constitution, motive power, and perceptual power. The perceptual aspect is an artful knowledge of how to perform movements; the motive aspect is a disposition (malaka) that enables efficient execution; the bodily aspect is the natural constitution of limbs that allows such movements. The author then summarizes that the genus under discussion is the perfecting of the preparedness of one of the two sides of what power (in the sense of possibility) is upon: either strong preparedness for the existence of a passion (like sickliness) or strong preparedness for non-existence (like healthiness). The most concise formulation is that this power either perfects itself toward change from the natural state (which is 'non-power') or toward non-change (which is 'natural power'). This passage is definitional, aiming to clarify a key concept in the theory of qualities.

Key Takeaway
Power (quwwa) as a dispositional quality is defined by its preparedness for change or non-change, and it is not a substance but a perfection within the category of quality.

Distinction between Malaka and Hal
Author defines and distinguishes malaka and hal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between malaka (habitual disposition) and hal (transient state) based on stability, and identify examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Malaka and hal are not different species but the same nature differing only in stability.

In this passage, the author introduces the fourth type of psychic accidents: those that are perfections in themselves. These are divided into two: firmly rooted dispositions called malaka, and quickly passing states called hal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing character traits and transient emotions in Islamic ethics and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Notice that both malaka and hal fall under the genus of psychic accidents (a'rad nafsiyya) that are perfections in themselves. They are not sensibles per se.
2
Contrast stability
Malaka is described as 'firmly rooted' (rasikh) with difficulty to remove, while hal is 'quickly disappearing' (sari' al-zawal). This difference in stability is the key criterion.
Examples: knowledge and health are malaka; brief anger of a forbearing person is hal.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives pairs: musahhahiyya (disposition to health) vs. sihha (actual health), and mumaridiyya (disposition to illness) vs. marad (actual illness). These illustrate that a stable disposition (malaka) may not always manifest as an actual state (hal).
4
Understand the logical relation
The author explicitly states that hal and malaka are not two species under a genus (separated by differentiae), nor are they two individuals under a species. Rather, they are the same nature considered with or without firmness. A single person can have both at different times (like youth and old age).
5
Apply to virtues and vices
Virtues (fada'il) and vices (radha'il) are malakat, not the actions themselves. Virtues are psychic states from which praiseworthy actions flow spontaneously without reflection.
This distinction is important for moral responsibility: a person with a vice finds it difficult to act virtuously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: فذلك من الأعراض النفسانية التي هي كمالات بأنفسها وإنه لا يكون من المحسوسات أيضًا بذاتها، لكنه إما أن يكون راسخا في المتكيف رسوحًا لا يزول أو لا يسهل زواله ويسمى ملكة مثل العلم والصحة والخلق الحسن كالعفة والعدل أو غيره كالفجور والجور، أو لا يكون راسخا بل يكون سريع الزوال ويسمى حالا مثل غضب الحليم ومريض المصحاح. والفرق بين المصحاحية والصحة، والممراضية والمرض؛ فإن الممراض قد لا يكون مريضًا، والمصحاح قد لا يكون صحيحًا. والأظهر في تعاريف أهل هذه الصناعة أن الحال ليس مقولا على الملكة حتى يكون كل ملكة حالا؛ وليس كل حال ملكة بل الحال اسم لطبيعة هذا الجنس إذا لم يكن مستحكما . فإنه إذا كان مستحكمًا لم يسم حالا بل يسمى ملكة وليس الافتراق بينهما افتراق نوعين تحت جنس؛ إذ ذاك بالفصول؛ وهذا بالأعراض. ولا أيضًا افتراق شخصين تحت نوع حتى تكون الاثنينية بينهما الاثنينية ما بين الشخصين بل يجوز أن تكون بينهما الاثنينية شخص واحد كما هي بين الشاب والشيخ؛ إذ الشاب لا يكون غير الشيخ في ذاته وإن كان غيره بالاعتبار. ثم من الملكات العلوم والفضائل، وليست الفضائل هي الأفعال المحمودة بل الهيئات النفسانية التي تصدر عنها الأفعال المحمودة صدورًا من غير روية كالطبيعي فيكون بحيث إذا أريد إصدار أضدادها صعب على أصحابها. وهذا مثل الأخلاق الحميدة والرذائل التي هي أضدادها فإنها ملكات، والفاجر بالخلق يتعذر عليه التعفف عند التمكن ففي نفسه إذن هيئة مطاوعة
The fourth type: that is from the psychic accidents which are perfections in themselves, and it is not from the sensibles also in themselves, but it is either firmly rooted in the qualified with a firmness that does not disappear or whose disappearance is not easy, and it is called a habitual disposition (malaka), like knowledge, health, and good character like chastity and justice, or other than that like debauchery and injustice; or it is not firmly rooted but rather is quickly disappearing, and it is called a transient state (hal), like the anger of a forbearing person and the sickness of a healthy person. And the difference between healthiness (musahhahiyya) and health (sihha), and between sickliness (mumaridiyya) and illness (marad); for the sickly person may not be ill, and the healthy person may not be healthy. And the most apparent in the definitions of the people of this craft is that 'hal' is not predicated of 'malaka' such that every malaka is a hal; nor is every hal a malaka, but hal is the name for the nature of this genus when it is not firmly established. For if it is firmly established, it is not called hal but rather is called malaka. And the separation between them is not the separation of two species under a genus; for that is by differentiae, and this is by accidents. Nor is it the separation of two individuals under a species such that the duality between them is the duality between individuals; rather, it is possible that the duality between them is for one individual, as between a youth and an old man; for the youth is not other than the old man in essence, though he is other by consideration. Then among the malakat are sciences and virtues. And virtues are not the praiseworthy acts, but rather the psychic states from which the praiseworthy acts emanate without reflection, like a natural [trait], such that when [the person] wants to produce their opposites, it is difficult for him. And this is like the praiseworthy character traits and the vices that are their opposites, for they are malakat, and the immoral person by character finds it difficult to be chaste when able, so in his soul there is an obedient disposition.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of psychic accidents, namely malaka (habitual disposition) and hal (transient state). The author begins by characterizing this type as perfections in themselves (not sensible per se). They are divided into two: firmly rooted and not easily removed (malaka), and quickly passing (hal). Examples of malaka include knowledge, health, and virtues like chastity and justice, as well as vices like debauchery and injustice. Examples of hal include the anger of a forbearing person (who is not habitually angry) and the sickness of a healthy-prone person. The author then clarifies a subtle distinction: the terms musahhahiyya (healthiness, a disposition to health) and sihha (actual health) differ, as the healthy-prone person may not actually be healthy, and similarly for sickliness and illness. Next, a crucial logical point is made: hal is not a genus under which malaka falls; rather, they refer to the same nature but differ in degree of stability. If the state is firmly established (mustahkam), it is called malaka; if not, it is called hal. The difference between them is not a specific difference (by differentiae) but by accidents. Moreover, they are not even different individuals under a species; rather, the same individual can have both a malaka and a hal at different times (e.g., youth vs. old age). Finally, the author explains that virtues (and vices) are malakat, but virtues themselves are not the praiseworthy actions; rather, they are psychic states (hay'at nafsiyya) from which actions emanate spontaneously. For example, a chaste person has a malaka of chastity that makes it difficult to commit unchaste acts, and similarly a vicious person finds virtue difficult.

Key Terms
ملكة (malaka): A firmly rooted psychic disposition that is not easily removed, such as knowledge, health, virtues, and vices. [Note: In later Mantiq, malaka is a standard category; here it is distinguished from hal primarily by stability.]
حال (hal): A transient psychic state that passes quickly, such as a brief anger or temporary sickness. [Note: In Sufi terminology, hal often means a spiritual state; here it is a logical category.]
فضيلة (fadila): A virtue; a psychic disposition (malaka) from which praiseworthy actions arise spontaneously, not the actions themselves. [Note: In ethics, fadila can mean the action itself; here it is strictly a state.]
رذيلة (radhila): A vice; a psychic disposition (malaka) from which blameworthy actions arise. [Note: As above.]
مصحاحية (musahhahiyya): The disposition to health, as opposed to actual health (sihha). [Note: A technical term specific to this discussion.]
ممراضية (mumaridiyya): The disposition to illness, as opposed to actual illness (marad). [Note: As above.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is an example of a malaka (habitual disposition)?
A) The anger of a forbearing person
B) Health (sihha)
C) The sickness of a healthy-prone person
D) Virtue (fadila)

Key Takeaway
Malaka and hal differ only in stability: a firmly established psychic state is malaka, a transient one is hal; they are not separate species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a malaka or a hal according to the author: (a) knowledge of fiqh, (b) a sudden feeling of generosity, (c) the vice of envy that one struggles to resist, (d) the anger of a normally patient person.
?
Explain why the author says that hal and malaka are not two different species but the same nature differing in stability. Use the example of a young man becoming old.

Distinguishing Malaka from Hal in the Fourth Type of Quality
Author defines the fourth type of quality, malaka and hal, and distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between malaka and hal based on the criterion of stability in the soul.

Core Idea
Malaka is firmly rooted; hal is transient.

The fourth type of quality covers psychical accidents that are perfections in themselves. But not all such states are equal: some are deeply ingrained, others are fleeting. This lesson teaches you to spot the difference using the text's precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of the fourth type
The passage begins by stating the fourth type is from the psychical accidents that are perfections in themselves. This is the broader category (genus) under which malaka and hal fall.
The genus is 'al-a'rāḍ al-nafsāniyya'.
2
Extract the defining characteristic (differentia)
The passage divides this genus based on whether the quality is firmly rooted (rāsikh) or not. This is the distinguishing feature.
Firmness vs. transience is the differentia.
3
Apply the definitions to examples
The text gives examples: knowledge (malaka), anger of a clement person (hal). Check that each example fits the definition.
4
Recognize the relativistic nuance
The author notes that easiness is relative; the same quality (e.g., anger) can be hal in one context and malaka in another depending on how deeply it is ingrained.
This shows the distinction is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: فذلك من الأعراض النفسانية التي هي كمالات بأنفسها وإنه لا يكون من المحسوسات أيضًا بذاتها، لكنه إما أن يكون راسخا في المتكيف رسوحًا لا يزول أو لا يسهل زواله ويسمى ملكة مثل العلم والصحة والخلق الحسن كالعفة والعدل أو غيره كالفجور والجور ، أو لا يكون راسخا بل يكون سريع الزوال ويسمى حالا مثل غضب الحليم ومريض المصحاح. والفرق بين المصحاحية والصحة، والممراضية والمرض؛ فإن الممراض قد لا يكون مريضًا، والمصحاح قد لا يكون صحيحًا. والأظهر في تعاريف أهل هذه الصناعة أن الحال ليس مقولا على الملكة حتى يكون كل ملكة حالا؛ وليس كل حال ملكة بل الحال اسم لطبيعة هذا الجنس إذا لم يكن مستحكما . فإنه إذا كان مستحكمًا لم يسم حالا بل يسمى ملكة وليس الافتراق بينهما افتراق نوعين تحت جنس؛ إذ ذاك بالفصول؛ وهذا بالأعراض. ولا أيضًا افتراق شخصين تحت نوع حتى تكون الاثنينية بينهما الاثنينية ما بين الشخصين بل يجوز أن تكون بينهما الاثنينية شخص واحد كما هي بين الشاب والشيخ؛ إذ الشاب لا يكون غير الشيخ في ذاته وإن كان غيره بالاعتبار. ثم من الملكات العلوم والفضائل، وليست الفضائل هي الأفعال المحمودة بل الهيئات النفسانية التي تصدر عنها الأفعال المحمودة / صدورًا من غير روية كالطبيعي فيكون بحيث إذا أريد إصدار أضدادها صعب على أصحابها. وهذا مثل الأخلاق الحميدة والرذائل التي هي أضدادها فإنها ملكات، والفاجر بالخلق يتعذر عليه التعفف عند التمكن ففي نفسه إذن هيئة مطاوعة لذلك الفعل؛ وكذلك المتعفف. والعلوم أيضًا ملكات لا من حيث هي استيفاء أصول الصناعة والمهارة فيها فقط بل من حيث آراء يعسر زوالها الذهن بعد التيقن والتمكن فيه. وقد قيل في ملكة الصناعة: «إنها عبارة عن أن يصدر عنه الصناعة من غير روية على ما عرف». وأما الحال فتسمى به ما كان من هذا الجنس سهل الزوال سهولة زوال الحرارة العرضية والبرودة العرضية مثلا . والحرارة والبرودة ليستا من هذا الجنس لكنا أوردناهما لما أنهما من جملة ما يزول بسرعة. والصحة والمرض إذا كانا سهلي الزوال فهما من هذا الجنس؛ وكذلك الغم والهم والعقد والظن إذا لم يكونوا مستحكمين ، فأما إذا كانوا فذلك من جملة الملكات. وقول من قال إن الفرق بين الحال والملكة ، إن هذه سهلة معناه أن هذه فلا تكون سهلة. وبالجملة إن الحال كيفية سريع الزوال، والملكة كيفية راسخة لكن السهولة من أمر مُشكل. فإن الشيء قد يكون سهلا بالقياس إلى شيء آخر؛ فيشبه أن يكون كل غضب مثلا فله شيء تحته سهلة الزوال وشيء هو تحته صعبة الزوال، وعلى هذا في الغير من الكيفيات المحسوسة وغير المحسوسة.
The fourth type: that is from the psychical accidents which are perfections in themselves, and it is not from the sensibles by their essence either. But it is either firmly rooted in the qualified thing with a firmness that does not vanish or its removal is not easy, and it is called malaka, like knowledge, health, and good character such as chastity and justice, or their opposites such as debauchery and injustice; or it is not firmly rooted but rather quickly passing, and it is called hal, like the anger of a clement person and the frequently ill person. And the difference between being frequently healthy and health, and being frequently ill and illness; for the one frequently ill may not be ill, and the one frequently healthy may not be healthy. And the most apparent in the definitions of the people of this craft is that hal is not predicated on malaka such that every malaka is a hal; nor is every hal a malaka. Rather, hal is a name for the nature of this genus when it is not firmly established. For if it is firmly established it is not called hal but rather malaka. And the distinction between them is not the distinction of two species under a genus; for that is by differentiae, while this is by accidents. Nor is it the distinction of two individuals under a species such that the duality between them is the duality between individuals; rather it is possible that the duality is in one individual, as between a youth and an old man; for the youth is not other than the old man in his essence, even if he is other by consideration. Then among the malakat are the sciences and virtues. And virtues are not the praiseworthy actions but rather the psychical dispositions from which praiseworthy actions proceed, proceeding without deliberation, like the natural, such that if it is desired to produce their opposites, it is difficult for their possessors. This is like the praiseworthy character traits and the vices which are their opposites, for they are malakat. And the debauched by character finds it difficult to be chaste when able; so in his soul there is a compliant disposition for that action, and likewise the chaste. And sciences are also malakat, not only from the perspective of mastering the principles of the craft and skill in it, but from the perspective of opinions whose removal from the mind is difficult after certainty and firm establishment. And it has been said regarding the malaka of a craft: 'It is an expression that the craft issues from him without deliberation, as is known.' As for hal, it is called what is from this genus that is easily removed, like the easy removal of accidental heat and cold for example. Heat and cold are not from this genus, but we mention them because they are among what quickly vanishes. And health and disease, if they are easily removed, are from this genus; likewise grief, worry, perplexity, and opinion if they are not firmly established. But if they are, then that is from the malakat. And the saying of whoever said that the difference between hal and malaka is that this one is easy means that this one is not easy. In summary, hal is a quality that quickly passes, and malaka is a firmly rooted quality, but easiness is a problematic matter. For a thing may be easy relative to another thing; so it seems that every anger, for example, has something under it that is easily removed and something under it that is with difficulty removed, and similarly for other qualities, sensible and non-sensible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of quality according to Aristotle's Categories, which are psychical accidents (al-a‘rāḍ al-nafsāniyya) that are perfections in themselves. The author begins by stating that this type is not from the sensibles by essence—i.e., it is not a physical quality like hot or cold—but rather a quality of the soul. Within this type, there is a division: either it is firmly rooted (rāsikh) and not easily removed, called malaka (habit/disposition), or it is not firmly rooted and passes quickly, called ḥāl (state). The author gives examples: malaka includes knowledge, health, and good character traits like chastity and justice, or their opposites like debauchery and injustice. Ḥāl includes the anger of a clement person (who is not normally angry) and a person who is frequently ill but not actually ill at the moment. The author then clarifies that the distinction between being prone to health (muṣḥāḥiyya) and actual health (ṣiḥḥa) is similar to that between being prone to illness (mumāriḍiyya) and actual illness (maraḍ). The key point is that ḥāl is not a species of malaka; rather, they are different levels of stability within the same genus. The difference between them is not like the difference between two species (which have distinct differentiae) nor like that between two individuals (which have distinct identities); instead, it can be the same individual at different times or aspects, like youth and old age. Next, the author discusses that among malakāt are sciences and virtues. Virtues are not the praiseworthy actions themselves but the psychical dispositions from which those actions issue spontaneously, without deliberation, making it difficult to act oppositely. Similarly, vices are such malakāt. Sciences are also malakāt because they involve firmly held opinions that are hard to dislodge after certainty. The author then quotes a definition of malaka of a craft as the ability to perform the craft without deliberation. Regarding ḥāl, it is called so when the quality is easily removed, like temporary heat or cold, though heat and cold themselves are not from this genus (they are sensible qualities) but are used as examples of quick disappearance. Health and disease can be ḥāl if temporary. Similarly, grief, worry, perplexity, and opinion are ḥāl if not firmly fixed; if they become fixed, they become malaka. The author critiques a simplistic statement that the difference is simply that ḥāl is easy and malaka is not, because easiness is relative. For instance, anger can have both an easy-to-remove component and a hard-to-remove component depending on the person and situation. This relativity applies to all qualities, sensible and non-sensible. Overall, the passage provides a detailed analysis of the fourth category of quality, emphasizing the fluidity between malaka and ḥāl and grounding the discussion in psychological and ethical examples from the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
الملكة
الحال
الأعراض النفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best illustrates the difference between malaka and ḥāl?
A) Knowledge of fiqh is a ḥāl because it can be forgotten easily.
B) The anger of a normally patient person when provoked is an example of ḥāl.
C) Chastity as a fixed virtue is an example of ḥāl.
D) Temporary heat in a body is a malaka.

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of quality is subdivided into malaka (firmly rooted) and hal (transient), but the boundary is relative to the individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify whether each of the following is malaka or hal according to the passage: (a) a person's habitual truthfulness, (b) a sudden feeling of anger that passes, (c) deep knowledge of fiqh.

Malaka as the Basis of Virtues and Sciences
Author defines the fourth type of quality, malaka and hal, and distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why virtues and sciences are considered malakat and how they relate to spontaneous action.

Core Idea
Virtues are malakat that produce actions without deliberation.

The passage extends the concept of malaka beyond simple stability. Virtues and sciences are malakat because they are deeply ingrained psychical states that generate actions or knowledge effortlessly. This lesson explores that application.

Guided Path
1
Locate the discussion of virtues and sciences
After defining malaka and hal, the passage discusses that among malakat are sciences and virtues. Read that section carefully.
2
Understand the criterion for virtues as malakat
Virtues are not actions but psychical dispositions that produce actions spontaneously. The passage says it is difficult to act oppositely.
Key phrase: 'صدورًا من غير روية' (without deliberation).
3
Apply the same to sciences
Sciences are malakat because after certainty, opinions are hard to remove from the mind.
Similar to virtues in stability.
4
Distinguish from hal in this context
The passage notes that if these qualities are not firmly established, they are hal (e.g., temporary knowledge).
Relates back to the core distinction.

Regarding ḥāl, it is called so when the quality is easily removed, like temporary heat or cold, though heat and cold themselves are not from this genus (they are sensible qualities) but are used as examples of quick disappearance. Health and disease can be ḥāl if temporary. Similarly, grief, worry, perplexity, and opinion are ḥāl if not firmly fixed; if they become fixed, they become malaka. The author critiques a simplistic statement that the difference is simply that ḥāl is easy and malaka is not, because easiness is relative. For instance, anger can have both an easy-to-remove component and a hard-to-remove component depending on the person and situation. This relativity applies to all qualities, sensible and non-sensible. Overall, the passage provides a detailed analysis of the fourth category of quality, emphasizing the fluidity between malaka and ḥāl and grounding the discussion in psychological and ethical examples from the Islamic tradition.
