Distinguishing Quality from Thing Qualified
Author explains the distinction between quality and thing qualified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quality and the thing qualified in any given example from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Quality is the attribute; thing qualified is what possesses it.

When we say 'Zayd is knowledgeable,' we distinguish between the quality (knowledge) and the person who has it. This lesson clarifies that distinction, which is fundamental for understanding predication in logic and its application in fields like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality
Look for the term that names the attribute itself, e.g., 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm) or 'whiteness' (al-bayāḍ). These are the qualities (kayfiyyāt).
Qualities are one of Aristotle's categories, divided into four types.
2
Identify the thing qualified
Find the entity that possesses the quality. It may be a substance (jawhar) or a quantity (kammiyya), and it is often named by a derivative term, e.g., 'knowledgeable' (ʿālim) from 'knowledge'.
The thing qualified is called dhū al-kayfiyya or dhawāt al-kayfiyya.
3
Check derivation
Verify whether the name for the thing qualified is derived from the quality. In Arabic, derivation is common (e.g., 'white' from 'whiteness'), but the passage notes that in Greek some qualities lack derived names.
Derivation helps distinguish the two: if the name comes from the quality, it signals the thing qualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الفرق بين الكيفية وذي الكيفية ] وأما الفرق بين الكيفية وذي الكيفية فالكيفيات هي التي مر ذكرها في الأنواع الأربعة. وأما ذوات الكيفية فهي التي لها هذه الكيفيات إما أولاً وإما ثانيا جواهر كانت أو كميات فيشتق لها الاسم منها كما يشتق من الكمية وغيرها. وقد قيل إنه لا يشتق في اللغة اليونانية عن بعض الكيفيات بل يفرد
Section: On the difference between quality and thing qualified. As for the difference between quality and thing qualified: the qualities are those mentioned in the four types. As for the things having quality, they are those that have these qualities, either primarily or secondarily, whether they are substances or quantities, so a name is derived for them from it, just as it is derived from quantity and others. And it has been said that in the Greek language, derivation from some qualities does not occur, but rather it is isolated.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This is a short section (faṣl) dedicated to clarifying the distinction between the quality (al-kayfiyya) itself and the thing that possesses the quality (dhū al-kayfiyya). The author states that qualities are the ones already discussed in the four types (the four categories of quality from Aristotle: habits/states, capacities, affective qualities, and shapes). The things having quality (dhawāt al-kayfiyya) are the substances (jawāhir) or quantities (kammiyyāt) that possess these qualities, either primarily or secondarily. For example, a substance like a person can have the quality of knowledge (malaka), and the name 'knowledgeable' is derived from that quality. The derivation process is analogous to how names are derived from quantity (e.g., 'long' from length). The author also notes a linguistic point: in Greek, some qualities do not allow derivation of a name, so the names are used in isolation (e.g., the quality itself is named without an adjective). This section draws attention to the ontological distinction between the attribute and the subject, a key point in the Categories. Within the Islamic logical tradition, this distinction is crucial for understanding predication and the categories.

Distinguishing Malaka and Hal as Qualities
Author defines malaka and hal as types of quality with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between malaka (disposition) and hal (state) by analyzing their relative stability and identifying examples from the passage.

Core Idea
The same quality can be a state or a disposition depending on its stability.

In the science of logic, qualities are divided into two types: those that are firmly established (malaka) and those that are easily removed (hal). This lesson unpacks the author's careful distinction, showing that the difference is not absolute but relative to context. You will learn to apply this classification to real examples from the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Notice that both malaka and hal are introduced as types of kayfiyya (quality). The genus is the broader category of quality. Read the passage and confirm that both are described as 'kayfiyya'.
The term 'kayfiyya' is a category in Aristotelian logic.
2
Extract the differentia
The key difference is stability: malaka is 'rasikha' (firmly established) and difficult to remove; hal is 'sahl al-zawal' (easily departing). Find the Arabic phrases that express this contrast.
Look for 'راسخة' and 'سهل الزوال' in the text.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives examples: sciences (disposition), accidental heat/cold (not states but similar), health/illness (states if transient), grief/opinion (states if not firmly established, dispositions if rooted). List these and note why each fits its category.
The author notes that heat and cold are not true states but are used for illustration.
4
Handle the relativity
The author critiques a simplistic reading, noting that 'easiness' is relative: the same quality (e.g., anger) can be easy or difficult to remove depending on context. Understand that the classification is not fixed for all instances of a quality.
This is the author's expansion (expansion_probe) on the basic definition.

For that action; and likewise the chaste. And the sciences are also dispositions, not insofar as they are the fulfillment of the principles of the craft and skill in it only, but insofar as they are opinions whose removal from the mind is difficult after certainty and mastery in it. And it has been said concerning the disposition of craftsmanship: 'It is an expression for the fact that the craft issues from him without reflection, according to what is known.' As for the state, it is called that which is of this kind, easy to depart, like the ease of departure of accidental heat and accidental cold, for example. But heat and cold are not of this kind, but we mentioned them because they are among things that depart quickly. And health and illness, if they are easy to depart, are of this kind; likewise grief, worry, opinion, and conjecture if they are not firmly established. But if they are firmly established, then that is among the dispositions. And the statement of one who said that the difference between state and disposition is that this is easy — its meaning is that this is not easy. In summary, state is a quickly departing quality, and disposition is a firmly established quality, but easiness is a problematic matter. For a thing may be easy in relation to another thing; so it seems that every anger, for example, has something under it that is easy to depart and something under it that is difficult to depart, and likewise for other sensible and non-sensible qualities.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of quality: malaka (disposition) and hal (state). The author begins by noting that sciences are dispositions, not merely because they involve mastery of principles but because they produce opinions that are difficult to remove after certainty. This is similar to a craft skill that issues without deliberation. Then he defines hal as a quality that is easy to depart, giving examples of accidental heat and cold, though they are not actually states but are cited for illustration. Health and illness, if transient, are states; likewise fleeting emotions. However, if these become deeply rooted, they become dispositions. The author critiques a simplistic distinction based on ease, showing that easiness is relative: the same quality (e.g., anger) can be transient in one case and persistent in another. Thus, the distinction between state and disposition is not absolute but depends on stability.

Distinguishing Quality from Thing Having Quality
Author distinguishes the category of quality from the subject that possesses it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a quality and the subject that possesses it, and identify cases where the name of the quality is derived from the qualified thing rather than the reverse.

Core Idea
The name of the qualified thing can sometimes give rise to the quality name, not vice versa.

In logic and ontology, it is crucial to separate a category from its individuals. Here, the author clarifies that the category of quality (kayfiyya) is distinct from the things that have those qualities (dhu al-kayfiyya). While normally the quality lends its name to the qualified thing, sometimes—as with the term 'miṣḥāḥ'—the derivation is reversed, and the quality name is coined from the qualified thing. Understanding this nuance sharpens your ability to analyse definitions and avoid conceptual confusion in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key concepts
Locate the terms 'al-kayfiyya' (quality) and 'dhu al-kayfiyya' (thing having quality) in the Arabic passage. Note that qualities are attributes like colours, tastes, etc., while things having quality are the substances or quantities that bear those attributes.
2
Understand the normal derivation
The author states that normally a name for the qualified thing is derived from the quality (e.g., from 'bayād' (whiteness) you get 'abyad' (white thing)). This is the typical relation in language.
3
Analyse the exceptional case
Focus on the example of 'al-miṣḥāḥ' (one who recovers health quickly). The author argues that its name does not come from a quality; rather, the quality name 'miṣḥāḥiyya' is artificially derived from the person's name. This reverses the natural order.
The quality itself (the disposition to heal fast) has no standard name.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic legal terms
Consider a term like 'fāsiq' (sinner) in fiqh. Is 'fāsiq' the quality or the qualified person? Usually, 'fisq' is the quality, and 'fāsiq' is the qualified thing. Can you think of a case where the derivation might be reversed? Reflect on terms like 'ṣāliḥ' (righteous) and 'ṣalāḥ' (righteousness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الفرق بين الكيفية وذي الكيفية ] وأما الفرق بين الكيفية وذي الكيفية فالكيفيات هي التي مر ذكرها في الأنواع الأربعة. وأما ذوات الكيفية فهي التي لها هذه الكيفيات إما أولاً وإما ثانيا جواهر كانت أو كميات فيشتق لها الاسم منها كما يشتق من الكمية وغيرها. وقد قيل إنه لا يشتق في اللغة اليونانية عن بعض الكيفيات بل يفرد للمتكيف اسم ولا يكون للكيفية اسم موضوع أصلا. ولا يبعد أن يكون في لغة العرب كذلك. فإن المصحاح من جملة ما لا يكون اسمه مشتقا من الكيفية وهي الاستعداد في هذه الصورة بل هو مشتق من الكمال؛ وهو الصحة لا على طريق المعتاد في الاشتقاق فإن الذي لا صحة فيه إذا مرض فإنه يكون في حال المرض مصحاحًا وإن لم يكن صحيحًا إذا كان سريع القبول للصحة والهيئة المصحاحية فإنها لا اسم لها بل ربما يشتق بالتكلُّف عن اسم الشيء ذي الهيئة على عكس القانون الطبيعي. فيقال مصحاحية فيكون اسم ذي القوة غير مشتق من اسم القوة حينئذ بل بالعكس.
Section: On the difference between quality and the thing having quality. As for the difference between quality and the thing having quality: qualities are those mentioned in the four species. As for things having quality, they are those that have these qualities either primarily or secondarily, whether they are substances or quantities, so a name is derived for them from it, just as it is derived from quantity and other things. And it has been said that in the Greek language, derivation does not occur from some qualities; rather, a name is singled out for the thing qualified, and there is no name for the quality at all. And it is not unlikely that it is so in the Arabic language. For 'al-miṣḥāḥ' is among those whose name is not derived from the quality—which is the disposition in this form—but rather it is derived from perfection, i.e., health, not in the usual way of derivation. For one who has no health, if he falls ill, he is called 'miṣḥāḥ' during his illness even if he is not healthy, if he is quick to accept health. And the 'miṣḥāḥiyya' quality has no name; rather, a name is perhaps derived with effort from the name of the thing possessing the quality, contrary to the natural rule. So it is said 'miṣḥāḥiyya', and then the name of the thing having the quality is not derived from the name of the quality, but the reverse.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between a quality (kayfiyya) and the thing that has that quality (dhu al-kayfiyya). Qualities are the attributes previously listed in the four species (of category of quality). Things having quality are the subjects (substances or quantities) that possess these qualities, either primarily or secondarily. Names are derived from qualities for those subjects, similar to derivation from quantity. However, the author notes that in Greek (and likely Arabic), sometimes no name exists for the quality itself; rather, a name is given to the qualified thing, and then a quality name is derived from that. Example: 'miṣḥāḥ' (a person quick to recover health) is not derived from the quality but from perfection (health). The quality of being miṣḥāḥ is then artificially derived from the person's name. Thus, derivation can be reversed: normally the quality gives the name, but here the quality name comes from the thing. This illustrates that the relation between quality and qualified thing is not always straightforward.

Opposition Across the Four Species of Quality
Author discusses how opposition applies differently to each species of quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four species of quality admit opposites and provide examples from the Aristotelian categorization.

Core Idea
Opposition runs through every species of quality except the first.

This passage examines how the accidental property of opposition (tadadd) applies to the four species of quality. Understanding which species have opposites is essential for correctly classifying qualities and for using them in definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four species of quality
Recall from earlier lessons the four species: (1) qualities specific to quantities (shapes/figures), (2) affective qualities and their resulting states, (3) potentiality and non-potentiality, (4) disposition and state.
The first species is also called 'qualities of figures'.
2
Note the exception
The passage states that opposition occurs in every species except the first. Why? Because shapes and figures do not have contraries in the same way; a triangle is not the opposite of a square.
This exception is important for understanding the scope of opposition.
3
List examples from the passage
For each species that has opposites, extract the examples: (2) white/black, hot/cold; (3) health/sickness; (4) cowardice/recklessness, correct/incorrect opinion.
Note that the passage also mentions 'disposition and state' with examples of ethical qualities and intellectual qualities.
4
Check the condition for opposites
The passage defines opposites as two things that cannot coexist in the same subject. Ensure that each example pair meets this condition.
For instance, the same body cannot be both white and black simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يكون من اللواحق فذلك متعددة منها التضاد وإنه يجري في كل نوع من أنواع الكيف إلا في النوع الأول منها وهو الكيفيات المخصوصة بالكميات: أما في الانفعالية والانفعاليات فكالبياض والسواد والحرارة والبرودة، وفي القوة واللا قوة فمثل المصحاحية والممراضية، وفي الملكة والحال فمثل الجبن والتهور ومثل العقد الصواب والخطأ. ثم أمرين غير مجتمعين فهما مضادان؛ فالزوج مثلا إذا خالف الفرد فإنما يخالفه بأنها العدد الذي ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد ليس كذلك وإن كان للفردية معنى آخر أزيد من ذلك : فذلك في الظاهر أنه مباين لا يشاركه في الموضوع؛ وذلك بمعزل عن الضد فإذن الزوجية والفردية والوحدة والكثرة غير مضادة تعرف على التحقيق من بعد. ولما كان لأنواع من الكيف أضداد دون النوع الواحد: فتلك الأنواع قابلة للاشتداد والنقص مثل الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فإن كل واحدة منها تقبل الأشد والأضعف والأزيد والأنقص؛ وهذا ذات جميع الأضداد التي بينها وسائط كالعدالة التي هي متوسطة بين طرفي الإفراط والتفريط، والعفة كذلك غير أن هذه المتوسطات إذا قربت
And what is among the accidents is multiple, among them opposition. And it occurs in every species of quality except the first species, which is the qualities specific to quantities. As for the affective and affective-qualities, like whiteness and blackness, heat and cold. And in potentiality and non-potentiality, like healthiness and sickliness. And in disposition and state, like cowardice and recklessness, and like correct and incorrect opinion. Then two things that do not coexist are opposites. For example, the even, if it differs from the odd, it differs only by being a number that is divided into two equal halves, while the odd is not so, even if oddness has another meaning beyond that. That is apparently a disparate (mubāyin) not sharing the subject; and that is far from opposition. So evenness and oddness, unity and multiplicity, are not opposites, as will be known in reality later. Since some species of quality have opposites while others do not: those species are subject to intensification and diminution, like heat and cold, moisture and dryness, for each one of them admits being more intense or weaker, more or less. And this is the nature of all opposites that have intermediates, like justice which is intermediate between the two extremes of excess and deficiency, and likewise chastity, except that these intermediates when they approach...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage discusses the accidental property of opposition (tadadd) as it applies to the four species of quality. The author states that opposition runs through all species of quality except the first species (qualities specific to quantities, i.e., shape and figure). Examples are given: in the affective and affective-qualities (second species), white/black, hot/cold; in potentiality and non-potentiality (third species), health/sickness; in disposition and state (fourth species), cowardice/recklessness, correct/incorrect opinion. Then the author distinguishes true opposites from mere disparity (mubāyana): two things that cannot coexist in the same subject are opposites. However, evenness and oddness, despite being mutually exclusive in numbers, are not true opposites because they are not in the same genus of quality? The passage suggests they are rather disparate. Later, the author notes that some qualities admit degrees (intensification/diminution) like hot/cold, moist/dry. These are opposites that have intermediates, such as justice between excess and deficiency. The passage begins to discuss intermediates but is cut off.

Distinguishing True Opposition from Mere Disparity
Author distinguishes true opposites from disparity using even/odd example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true opposites (mutaḍaddān) from merely disparate (mubāyin) properties using the example of evenness and oddness.

Core Idea
Not all mutually exclusive properties are opposites; true opposites share the same subject genus.

The passage refines our understanding of opposition by examining the case of evenness and oddness. Although these are mutually exclusive in numbers, they are not true opposites because they do not inhere in the same subject in the same way. This lesson helps you apply this distinction to other pairs.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for opposites
The passage states: 'two things that do not coexist are opposites.' However, this condition alone is not sufficient; the pair must also be in the same category of quality and in the same subject.
The author uses the phrase 'in the same subject' (في الموضوع).
2
Analyze the even/odd example
Evenness and oddness are properties of numbers, but they are not qualities that inhere in a subject in the same way as whiteness inheres in a body. The passage says 'that is apparently a disparate (mubāyin) not sharing the subject.'
The subject here is the number itself, but the opposition is not in the category of quality? Actually, numbers are in the category of quantity. So they belong to a different category altogether.
3
Compare with a true opposite pair
Take heat and cold. Both are in the category of quality (affective qualities) and can inhere in the same substrate (e.g., water). They cannot coexist, so they are true opposites.
This contrast highlights the importance of categorial belonging.
4
Apply the distinction to other examples
Consider unity and multiplicity from the passage. They are also not opposites for similar reasons. The key is to check whether the pair belongs to the same species of quality and shares the same subject type.
If they are in different categories or do not share a subject, they are disparate, not opposites.

And what is among the accidents is multiple, among them opposition. And it occurs in every species of quality except the first species, which is the qualities specific to quantities. As for the affective and affective-qualities, like whiteness and blackness, heat and cold. And in potentiality and non-potentiality, like healthiness and sickliness. And in disposition and state, like cowardice and recklessness, and like correct and incorrect opinion. Then two things that do not coexist are opposites. For example, the even, if it differs from the odd, it differs only by being a number that is divided into two equal halves, while the odd is not so, even if oddness has another meaning beyond that. That is apparently a disparate (mubāyin) not sharing the subject; and that is far from opposition. So evenness and oddness, unity and multiplicity, are not opposites, as will be known in reality later. Since some species of quality have opposites while others do not: those species are subject to intensification and diminution, like heat and cold, moisture and dryness, for each one of them admits being more intense or weaker, more or less. And this is the nature of all opposites that have intermediates, like justice which is intermediate between the two extremes of excess and deficiency, and likewise chastity, except that these intermediates when they approach...

Commentary
This passage discusses the accidental property of opposition (tadadd) as it applies to the four species of quality. The author states that opposition runs through all species of quality except the first species (qualities specific to quantities, i.e., shape and figure). Examples are given: in the affective and affective-qualities (second species), white/black, hot/cold; in potentiality and non-potentiality (third species), health/sickness; in disposition and state (fourth species), cowardice/recklessness, correct/incorrect opinion. Then the author distinguishes true opposites from mere disparity (mubāyana): two things that cannot coexist in the same subject are opposites. However, evenness and oddness, despite being mutually exclusive in numbers, are not true opposites because they are not in the same genus of quality? The passage suggests they are rather disparate. Later, the author notes that some qualities admit degrees (intensification/diminution) like hot/cold, moist/dry. These are opposites that have intermediates, such as justice between excess and deficiency. The passage begins to discuss intermediates but is cut off.

How Quality Names Can Break Derivation Rules
Author explains that some qualities have no name derived from them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where a quality lacks a name and analyze reverse derivation patterns in Arabic.

Core Idea
Some qualities have no name; the possessor's name is derived from perfection, not the quality.

In Arabic, names are typically derived from qualities: the possessor is named after the quality (e.g., 'ālim from 'ilm). But this passage reveals an irregularity: some qualities themselves have no name, forcing the derivation to reverse direction. The example of 'muṣaḥḥaḥ' shows that the name comes from perfection (health) rather than the underlying quality of quick recovery. Understanding this helps you avoid misinterpreting derived terms in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the thing qualified from the quality
Note that al-mutakayyif (the thing qualified) has a name, but al-kayfiyya (the quality) may not have a name at all. The author states this as a general observation.
2
Examine the example of 'muṣaḥḥaḥ'
The term 'muṣaḥḥaḥ' means 'one who is quick to recover health.' Its derivation is not from the quality of quick recovery (which is a preparedness) but from perfection (health). Even an unhealthy person can be called muṣaḥḥaḥ if they recover quickly.
The quality of being quick to recover has no name in Arabic.
3
Identify the reverse derivation pattern
Normally, the possessor is named after the quality (e.g., 'ālim from 'ilm). Here, the possessor's name (muṣaḥḥaḥ) is derived from perfection, and then a name for the disposition (muṣaḥḥaḥiyya) is artificially derived from the possessor's name—the opposite of the natural rule.
This reversal is called 'derivation by effort' (ishtiqāq bi al-takalluf).
4
Apply to Islamic science contexts
Search for other Arabic terms in fiqh or hadith where a quality lacks a name, causing the possessor's name to be derived from something else. This sharpens your sensitivity to linguistic nuance in legal and theological texts.
For example, the term 'muṣaḥḥaḥ' appears in medical traditions; knowing its derivation prevents confusion about its meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمتكيف اسم ولا يكون للكيفية اسم موضوع أصلا. ولا يبعد أن يكون في لغة العرب كذلك. فإن المصحاح من جملة ما لا يكون اسمه مشتقا من الكيفية وهي الاستعداد في هذه الصورة بل هو مشتق من الكمال؛ وهو الصحة لا على طريق المعتاد في الاشتقاق فإن الذي لا صحة فيه إذا مرض فإنه يكون في حال المرض مصحاحًا وإن لم يكن صحيحًا إذا كان سريع القبول للصحة والهيئة المصحاحية فإنها لا اسم لها بل ربما يشتق بالتكلُّف عن اسم الشيء ذي الهيئة على عكس القانون الطبيعي. فيقال مصحاحية فيكون اسم ذي القوة غير مشتق من اسم القوة حينئذ بل بالعكس.
The thing qualified has a name, but the quality itself has no name at all. And it is not unlikely that this is the case in the Arabic language. For al-muṣaḥḥaḥ is among those whose name is not derived from the quality—which is the preparedness in this form—but rather derived from perfection, i.e., health, not in the usual way of derivation. For the one who has no health, if he becomes ill, he is called muṣaḥḥaḥ while ill, even if he is not healthy, provided he is quick to accept health. And the ṣaḥīḥ-type disposition (al-hay'a al-muṣaḥḥaḥiyya) has no name, but sometimes a name is laboriously derived from the name of the thing possessing the disposition, contrary to the natural rule. Thus one says muṣaḥḥaḥiyya, so the name of the possessor of the power is then not derived from the name of the power, but rather the opposite.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage explains a subtle point about the derivation of names for qualities. The author states that while the thing possessing a quality (al-mutakayyif) has a name, the quality itself (al-kayfiyya) often has no name at all. He gives the example of 'muṣaḥḥaḥ', meaning one who is quick to recover from illness. This term is not derived from the quality of being quick to recover (which is a preparedness for health) but rather from perfection or health itself. Even during illness, if someone is prone to quick recovery, he is called muṣaḥḥaḥ. The underlying disposition (al-hay'a al-muṣaḥḥaḥiyya) has no name. Sometimes, a name for the disposition is artificially derived from the name of the thing possessing it (e.g., 'muṣaḥḥaḥiyya'), reversing the normal pattern where the thing is named after the quality. This passage highlights the irregularity in Arabic linguistic usage regarding qualities.

Opposition in the Species of Quality
Author discusses opposition (tadādd) in each species of quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which species of quality admit genuine opposites and cite examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Opposition runs in three species of quality, but not in quantitative qualities.

This passage from a logic text examines where opposition (tadādd) occurs within the four species of quality. The author specifies that opposition applies to every species except the first, which comprises qualities specific to quantities. Let's break down the examples and understand the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of opposition
The passage says 'two things that are not together are opposites.' This is a preliminary rough definition; a refined version will include sharing a subject.
2
List the species of quality mentioned
The author mentions three species: affective qualities (infi'āliyya), power and lack of power (quwwa wa lā quwwa), and disposition/state (malaka wa ḥāl). The first species (quantitative qualities) is excluded from opposition.
3
Extract examples for each species
For affective: white/black, hot/cold. For power: quick to recover vs. prone to illness. For disposition: cowardice/rashness, correct/incorrect belief.
4
Confirm absence in the first species
The author explicitly states that opposition does not run in the first species (qualities specific to quantities, like squareness).
5
Summarize the rule
Opposition in quality is limited to three species; quantitative qualities are excluded because they are not subject to alteration in a shared subject in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يكون من اللواحق فذلك متعددة منها التضاد وإنه يجري في كل نوع من أنواع الكيف إلا في النوع الأول منها وهو الكيفيات المخصوصة بالكميات: أما في الانفعالية والانفعاليات فكالبياض والسواد والحرارة والبرودة، وفي القوة واللا قوة فمثل المصحاحية والممراضية، وفي الملكة والحال فمثل الجبن والتهور ومثل العقد الصواب والخطأ. ثم أمرين غير مجتمعين فهما مضادان؛ فالزوج مثلا إذا خالف الفرد فإنما يخالفه بأنها العدد الذي ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد ليس كذلك وإن كان للفردية معنى آخر أزيد من ذلك : فذلك في الظاهر أنه مباين لا يشاركه في الموضوع؛ وذلك بمعزل عن الضد فإذن الزوجية والفردية والوحدة والكثرة غير مضادة تعرف على التحقيق من بعد.
And among the accidents are many, including opposition, and it runs in every species of the categories of quality except the first species of it, which is the qualities specific to quantities. As for the affective and passions, like whiteness and blackness, heat and cold. As for power and lack of power, like the state of being quick to recover (muṣaḥḥaḥiyya) and being prone to illness (mamrāḍiyya). As for disposition and state, like cowardice and rashness, and like correct and incorrect belief. Then two things that are not together are opposites; for example, even number when it differs from odd number, it differs because it is the number that can be divided into two equal parts, and odd is not so, even if oddness has an additional meaning beyond that. So it is apparently separate (mubāyin) not sharing a subject; and that is far from being a true opposite. So evenness and oddness, oneness and plurality, are not opposites, as will be known upon investigation.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage classifies opposition (tadādd) within the category of quality. The author states that opposition applies to every species of quality except the first species (qualities specific to quantities, like squareness and triangularity). He gives examples: In the species of affective qualities (infi'āliyyāt), opposition exists between whiteness and blackness, heat and cold. In the species of power and lack of power (quwwa wa lā quwwa), opposition is between being quick to recover (muṣaḥḥaḥiyya) and being prone to illness (mamrāḍiyya). In the species of disposition and state (malaka wa ḥāl), opposition is between cowardice and rashness, or between correct and incorrect belief. Then the author refines the notion: two things that are never together (e.g., even and odd) are opposites in a sense. But even and odd are not true opposites because they do not share a common subject; one number cannot be both even and odd, but they are simply different categories. True opposites must coexist in the same subject. Therefore, evenness and oddness, oneness and plurality, are not genuine opposites, as will be clarified later.

الملكة والحال

The Condition for True Opposition: Even and Odd
Author discusses opposition (tadādd) in each species of quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why evenness and oddness are not considered genuine opposites in the Aristotelian classification of quality.

Core Idea
Even and odd are not opposites because they do not share a common subject that can receive both qualities alternately.

The passage introduces a nuance: while many pairs in quality are opposites, evenness and oddness, oneness and plurality, are not. This is because they do not coexist in the same subject in the way that, for example, whiteness and blackness do. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of opposition
The passage says 'two things that are not together are opposites.' But note the implicit condition of sharing a subject, which is clarified later.
2
Analyze the pair even/odd
Even is number divisible by two equal parts; odd is not. They are mutually exclusive but belong to different numbers.
3
Apply the shared subject test
Can a single number be both even and odd? No. But does a number have the quality of evenness in the same way a wall has whiteness? The passage says no, because evenness and oddness are not qualities that inhere in a subject that can change; they are fixed properties of numbers.
4
Contrast with true opposites
For true opposites like hot/cold, the same body can be hot at one time and cold at another. Evenness and oddness cannot alternate in the same number because number is what it is.
5
Conclusion
Even and odd are mubāyin (separate) not muḍādd (opposite).

Source Text

Qualities and Their Degrees: Intensity and Deficiency
Author explains which qualities admit intensity and which do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a quality as admitting or not admitting intensity/deficiency based on whether it has an opposite and/or an intermediate.

Core Idea
Qualities with opposites admit degrees; those without do not.

In everyday language, we say something is 'hotter' or 'more just', but can we say something is 'more square'? This passage from the logic text clarifies which qualities can have degrees and why. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyze definitions in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with ethical and physical qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key criterion
Read the first sentence: qualities that have opposites (aḍdād) are capable of intensity (ishtidād) and deficiency (naqṣ). This is the main rule: if a quality has an opposite (e.g., heat/cold), it admits degrees.
Opposites here mean contradictory or contrary pairs within the same species.
2
Distinguish two types of opposites
The author then separates opposites into two groups: those with intermediates (wasāʾiṭ) like justice (between excess and deficiency) and those without intermediates like heat and cold. Both groups admit intensity, but the intermediate ones have an additional property: when instantiated in individuals, their 'true intermediate' may only be grasped by definition, not by sensation.
Justice in the abstract does not vary, but Zayd's justice can be more or less intense than Amr's.
3
Identify qualities that do NOT admit intensity
The author gives squareness (tarbīʿ) and triangularity as examples. These have no opposites; a square cannot have an opposite like 'non-square' in a graded way. Therefore, they do not admit intensity or deficiency.
Unless the square is imperfect in sensation, but that is a matter of perception, not the real form.
4
Apply the criterion to new examples
Now test other qualities: Does 'health' admit intensity? Yes, because it has an opposite (sickness) and is an intermediate in bodily mixture. Does 'being a father' admit intensity? No, because it has no opposite and is a relation, not a quality in this sense.
The passage explicitly mentions health as an intermediate, so it confirms it admits intensity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان لأنواع من الكيف أضداد دون النوع الواحد: فتلك الأنواع قابلة للاشتداد والنقص مثل الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فإن كل واحدة منها تقبل الأشد والأضعف والأزيد والأنقص؛ وهذا ذات جميع الأضداد التي بينها وسائط كالعدالة التي هي متوسطة بين طرفي الإفراط والتفريط، والعفة كذلك غير أن هذه المتوسطات إذا قربت بالمادة واعتبرت في الأشخاص لم يعتبر توسطها الحقيقي فإن ذلك غير مدرك إلا بالحد فيما قارن ذلك الحقيقي ولم يخرج إلى الطرفين خروجًا ظاهرا؛ فإنه يعد في الواسطة. ولذلك قد يكون عدالة زيد أشد من عدالة عمرو وإن كانت العدالة المطلقة ليست أشد من عدالة أخرى فإنها واحدة لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص. وكذلك حال الصحة التي هي توسط ما في المزاج وفي هيئة التركيب بين أمور زائدة وناقصة. وأما الأمور التي لا أضداد لها فإنها لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص نحو المربع والمثلث وغير ذلك وإلا لكان التربيع مثلا يوجه في النقصان إلى ضد؛ وذلك محال فالتربيع بحقيقته لا يكون قابلا على خلاف السواد والبياض وغير ذلك. فإن ذلك بالحقيقة يكون قابلا ولا اعتبار للحسية في هذه الأمور. فإن المربع في الحس لا يلزم أن يكون مربعا في الحقيقة، وكذلك السواد والبياض ونحوهما من الأعراض.
And since for some species of quality there are opposites without a single type, those species are capable of intensity and deficiency, like heat and cold, wetness and dryness, for each of them accepts the more intense and the weaker, the more and the less. And this is the property of all opposites that have intermediates, like justice, which is intermediate between the two extremes of excess and deficiency, and likewise chastity. But these intermediates, when they are approximated to matter and considered in individuals, their true intermediateness is not considered, for that is not perceived except by definition insofar as it approximates that true intermediate and does not go out to the extremes in an apparent way. So it is counted as intermediate. Therefore, Zayd's justice may be more intense than 'Amr's justice, even though absolute justice is not more intense than another justice, for it is one and does not accept the more and the less. Similarly is the case of health, which is an intermediate in the mixture and in the composition form between excessive and deficient matters. As for things that have no opposites, they do not accept intensity and deficiency, like squareness, triangularity, and others. Otherwise, squareness would tend in deficiency toward a opposite, which is impossible. So squareness in its reality is not capable of having more or less unlike blackness and whiteness. Indeed, that (whiteness) is in reality capable, and sensibleness is not considered in these matters. For a square in sensation is not necessarily a square in reality, and similarly for blackness and whiteness and similar accidents.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage elaborates on which qualities admit intensity (ishtidād) and deficiency (naqṣ). The author states that the species of quality that have opposites (e.g., heat-cold, wet-dry) are capable of intensity: a thing can be hotter or colder. This applies to all opposites that have intermediates (wasā'iṭ), like justice which is a mean between excess and deficiency. However, when these intermediates are instantiated in matter (e.g., specific individuals), their true intermediate nature is not directly perceptible; we only grasp it by definition. Thus, Zayd's justice may be more intense than 'Amr's, though absolute justice (the theoretical mean) does not admit degrees. Similarly, health is an intermediate in bodily mixture. On the other hand, things without opposites—like squareness or triangularity—do not admit intensity. A square cannot be more square than another square; it simply is square or not. If squareness could increase or decrease, it would tend toward an opposite (non-squareness), which is absurd. The author distinguishes between the sensible and the real: a perceived square may not be perfectly square in reality, but the form of squareness itself is not subject to degrees. This preserves the logical distinction between qualities that are measurable and those that are not.

Key Terms
الاشتداد
الواسطة
الحسي

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a quality specific to quantities.
B) Because it has no opposite to tend towards.
C) Because it is not sensible.
D) Because it is a real form, not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Qualities that have opposites (with or without intermediates) admit degrees; qualities without any opposite do not.

Refuting that Qualities are Relations: Essence vs. Accident
Author refutes the claim that many qualities belong to the category of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a quality being essentially a relation versus accidentally involving a relation in the context of the Aristotelian categories as applied to Islamic attributes.

Core Idea
A quality's essence is not a relation, even if it implies one.

In this passage, the author engages with an objection: many qualities (like knowledge, power) seem to be relations because they are always 'of something'. He refines the criterion, showing that the specific nature of a quality remains in the category of quality, while relation is an accidental attachment. This distinction is crucial for understanding how Islamic theologians and philosophers categorize divine and human attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
What does the objector say about qualities like knowledge and power? Find the statement in the passage that lists these as belonging to the category of relation.
2
Find the author's distinction
How does the author differentiate between the essence of a quality and its accidental relation? Look for phrases like 'ليست بذواتها' (not in themselves) and 'عرضت له الإضافة' (relation accidentally occurs).
3
Analyze the example of 'head'
Why does the author use the example of a head being 'head of something'? What does it show about the nature of relation? The head itself is not a relation; being a head of something is accidental.
4
Apply to an Islamic science
Consider the attribute of 'knowledge' in the context of Allah's attributes. Is Allah's knowledge essentially a relation? Or is it a quality that accidentally relates to the known? Use the author's reasoning to decide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول إن أكثر الأشياء التي أوردتموها في مقولة الكيف فذلك من مقولة المضاف كالملكة والقوة والعلم وما أشبه ذلك لكنا نقول إن هذه الأشياء ليست بذواتها من مقولة المضاف بل ربما عرضت له الإضافة فإنه وإن كانت ماهياتها مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها فإنها لا يجب بذلك أن تكون من مقولة المضاف بل هي مفتقرة في كونها من المضاف إلى دعامة أخرى. فلا يقال مثلا إن هذا الرأس هو هذا الرأس لشيء بل يقال هذا الرأس رأس لشيء؛ فالإضافة إذن عارضة حتى إن التخصيص يتعرى عن موافقة تخصيص الإضافة بإزائه بل يلزمه من الإضافة ما يلزم الطبيعة العامة التي يلحقها الإضافة كالرأسية. وكان هذا التخصيص ليس من جهة ما لحقه من الإضافة بل من جهة الوجود الخاص كان كيفية ما تكون هيئة في النفس وصورة مجردة عن المواد ; وذلك الوجود غير مضاف . فالنوعيات إذن ليست من المضاف
Then one may say that most of the things you have listed in the category of quality belong to the category of relation, like disposition, power, knowledge, and the like. But we say that these things are not in themselves from the category of relation; rather, relation may accidentally occur to them. For even if their essences are said in relation to others, it is not necessary that they be from the category of relation. Rather, they need another support to be in the category of relation. For one does not say, for example, that this head is this head of something; rather, one says this head is a head of something. So relation is accidental, such that the specification (takhṣīṣ) is free from agreement with the specification of relation opposite to it. Rather, it entails from relation what the general nature that is attached to relation entails, like headness. And this specification is not by virtue of what attaches to it from relation, but rather by virtue of the particular existence, which is a quality—a form in the soul and a form abstracted from matters—and that existence is not relative. So the specific natures (al-naw'iyyāt) are not from the relative.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objector claims that many things listed under quality (such as disposition, power, knowledge) actually belong to the category of relation (al-muḍāf). The author responds by refining the criterion: these things are not essentially (bi-dhātihā) relations; rather, relation is accidental to them. Even though their essences are described with respect to others (e.g., knowledge is knowledge of something), that does not automatically place them in the category of relation. To be a relation, they require an additional support. The author gives the example of 'head': we say 'this head is a head of something', but the head itself is not a relation; being a head of something is an accidental relation. The specific nature (e.g., headness) is not relative by itself but becomes relative through a particular existence. The author emphasizes that the specific forms (naw'iyyāt) are not from the relational category. Thus, qualities retain their own category even if they often involve relations.

Key Terms
المضاف (al-muḍāf): The category of relation; things that are essentially said with reference to something else. [Note: Standard Aristotelian category.]
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa): Relation; an accidental attribute that makes a thing relative. [Note: Some later logicians like Ibn Taymiyya challenge this, but here it is standard.]
النوعيات (al-naw'iyyāt): Specific natures or essences; the form that makes a thing what it is. [Note: Used here to refer to the specific qualities that define a category.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge is a form in the soul.
B) Because the relation is accidental to its essence.
C) Because knowledge has no opposite.
D) Because knowledge is not sensible.

Key Takeaway
The specific natures of qualities are not from the category of relation; relation is accidental to them.

Qualities That Admit of More and Less
Author argues that some qualities admit intensity, others do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a quality admits intensity based on the presence of opposites and the possibility of intermediate states.

Core Idea
Only qualities with opposites can accept more and less.

In this passage, the author distinguishes between qualities that vary in intensity, like justice and health, and those that are fixed, like square and triangle. This distinction is crucial for understanding the category of quality (al-kayf). Let's examine the criterion he uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for accepting more and less
The author says that qualities which have opposites can accept intensity, while those without opposites cannot. Look for the phrase 'things that have no opposites, they do not accept more and less'.
The Arabic: 'وأما الأمور التي لا أضداد لها فإنها لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص'.
2
Contrast examples: justice vs. square
Justice admits more/less because it has an opposite (injustice); square does not have an opposite, so a square cannot be more or less square in reality.
The author uses 'عدالة' and 'مربع'.
3
Note the role of sense perception
The author warns not to rely on sensory appearances: a square that appears smaller in sensation is still a square in reality. The criterion is ontological, not perceptual.
He says 'لا اعتبار للحسية في هذه الأمور'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمادة واعتبرت في الأشخاص لم يعتبر توسطها الحقيقي فإن ذلك غير مدرك إلا بالحد فيما قارن ذلك الحقيقي ولم يخرج إلى الطرفين خروجًا ظاهرا؛ فإنه يعد في الواسطة. ولذلك قد يكون عدالة زيد أشد من عدالة عمرو وإن كانت العدالة المطلقة ليست أشد من عدالة أخرى فإنها واحدة لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص. وكذلك حال الصحة التي هي توسط ما في المزاج وفي هيئة التركيب بين أمور زائدة وناقصة. وأما الأمور التي لا أضداد لها فإنها لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص نحو المربع والمثلث وغير ذلك وإلا لكان التربيع مثلا يوجه في النقصان إلى ضد؛ وذلك محال فالتربيع بحقيقته لا يكون قابلا على خلاف السواد والبياض وغير ذلك. فإن ذلك بالحقيقة يكون قابلا ولا اعتبار للحسية في هذه الأمور. فإن المربع في الحس لا يلزم أن يكون مربعا في الحقيقة، وكذلك السواد والبياض ونحوهما من الأعراض.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إن أكثر الأشياء التي أوردتموها في مقولة الكيف فذلك من مقولة المضاف كالملكة والقوة والعلم وما أشبه ذلك لكنا نقول إن هذه الأشياء ليست بذواتها من مقولة المضاف بل ربما عرضت له الإضافة فإنه وإن كانت ماهياتها مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها فإنها لا يجب بذلك أن تكون من مقولة المضاف بل هي مفتقرة في كونها من المضاف إلى دعامة أخرى. فلا يقال مثلا إن هذا الرأس هو هذا الرأس لشيء بل يقال هذا الرأس رأس لشيء؛ فالإضافة إذن عارضة حتى إن التخصيص يتعرى عن موافقة تخصيص الإضافة بإزائه بل يلزمه من الإضافة ما يلزم الطبيعة العامة التي يلحقها الإضافة كالرأسية. وكان هذا التخصيص ليس من جهة ما لحقه من الإضافة بل من جهة الوجود الخاص كان كيفية ما تكون هيئة في النفس وصورة مجردة عن المواد؛ وذلك الوجود غير مضاف. فالنوعيات إذن ليست من المضاف في ذواتها وإنما المقول ماهية بالقياس هو الجنس الذي لأجله يقال كذلك للنوع، وكما أن للنوعيات وجود ليست هي به مضافًا فكذلك الجنس. وإن عرض له الإضافة فليس من المضاف بذاته فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون الجنس داخلا بذاته في مقولة والأنواع غير داخلة فيها؛ فتبين أنه لما لم يكن النوع من المضاف لم يكن الجنس من المضاف. فالشيء الواحد نحو العلم مثلا إذا كان مضافًا كانت الإضافة عارضة له لا أنه بذاته نوع من المضاف؛ فهو يدخل إذن في المضاف بالعرض. وإن كان ذلك العروض من اللوازم والشيء الواحد لا يمتنع أن يكون داخلا في المقولين على وجهين في أحدهما بالذات وفي الأخرى بالعرض. وإن كان يمتنع دخوله في المقولين بالذات لامتناع تقومه بهما من حيث هو هو. فلو كان العلم من المضاف الحقيقي لكان نوعه وهو النحو مثلا كذلك؛ والنحو ليس نحو الشيء إلا أن يوجد من حيث هو علم فيقال النحو علم بشيء. وعلى هذا في القوة وغيرها. ولا يقال إن حكم النحو حكم العلم فإن النحو نحو بالقياس إلى شيء هو إعراب اللغة؛ إذ الإعراب من حيث هو هو لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو فكيف يكون النحو مضافًا إليه لكن الإعراب مقول بالقياس إلى النحو من حيث هو معلوم حتى لو كان إعراب اللغة موجودًا ألف سنة من غير أن يكون معلوما لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو. فلما لحق به المعلوم صار معلوما فصار بإزائه هيئة نفسانية هي علم جملتها مقولة بالقياس إلى هذه الجملة؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن الرأس مثلا من حيث هو رأس مضاف إلى البدن من حيث هو ذو رأس.
By matter, and considered in individuals, their real intermediate is not considered, for that is not known except by definition in what accompanies that real [intermediate] and does not go out to the extremes in an apparent manner; for it is counted among the intermediate. Therefore, Zayd's justice may be more intense than Amr's justice, even though absolute justice is not more intense than another justice, for it is one and does not accept more and less. And likewise the case of health, which is an intermediate in the mixture and in the state of composition between excessive and deficient matters. As for things that have no opposites, they do not accept more and less, like square, triangle, and others; otherwise the square, for example, would be directed in deficiency toward an opposite, and that is impossible. So the square in its reality is not receptive [of more and less], unlike blackness, whiteness, and others. For those are in reality receptive, and sense perception is not considered in these matters. For the square in sensation is not necessarily a square in reality, and likewise blackness, whiteness, and similar accidents.
Then someone may say that most of the things you have adduced in the category of quality are from the category of relation, like disposition, power, knowledge, and the like. But we say that these things are not in themselves from the category of relation; rather, relation is accidental to them. For even if their quiddities are predicated in relation to others, it does not necessarily follow from that that they are from the category of relation; rather, they are in need, for their being relative, of another basis. For example, it is not said, 'This head is this head for something,' but, 'This head is a head for something.' Therefore, relation is accidental, so that the particularization is bare of agreement with the particularization of relation corresponding to it; rather, what follows from relation follows from the general nature that relation attaches to, such as head-ness. And this particularization is not by virtue of the relation that attaches to it, but by virtue of the particular existence, which was a quality that is a state in the soul and a form stripped of matter, and that existence is not relative. So the specific natures are not from the relation in themselves, but that which is predicated as a quiddity with respect to another is the genus for which the species is so called. And just as specific natures have an existence by which they are not relative, so too the genus. And if relation accidentally attaches to it, it is not from relation in itself, for it is impossible that the genus be inherently included in a category while the species are not included in it. Thus it is clear that since the species is not from relation, the genus is not from relation. So one and the same thing, like knowledge, if it is relative, relation is accidental to it, not that it is in itself a species of relation; so it enters into relation accidentally. And even if that accidentalness is from necessary concomitants, one thing is not prevented from being included in two categories in two ways: in one essentially, and in the other accidentally. But it is impossible for it to be included in two categories essentially, because it is impossible for it to be constituted by both in its very identity. For if knowledge were from real relation, then its species, like grammar, would be likewise. But grammar is not 'grammar of something' unless it exists insofar as it is knowledge, so grammar is said to be 'knowledge of something.' And similarly for power and others. And it is not said that the ruling of grammar is the ruling of knowledge, for grammar is 'grammar' with respect to something that is the parsing of language; since parsing in itself is not predicated with respect to grammar, so how can grammar be relative to it? But parsing is predicated with respect to grammar insofar as it is known, so even if the parsing of language existed for a thousand years without being known, it would not be predicated with respect to grammar. When knowledge attached to it, it became known, and thus there came to correspond to it a psychic state which is knowledge, the whole of which is predicated with respect to this whole. So consider what you have known: for example, the head insofar as it is head is relative to the body insofar as it is possessor of a head.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a critical expansion responding to an objection about the categories of quality and relation. The author begins by discussing intermediate states like justice and health, which admit of more and less, unlike geometric shapes such as squares and triangles that do not. This serves to distinguish qualities that vary in intensity from those that do not. Then an objector claims that many things the author placed in the category of quality, such as disposition, power, and knowledge, actually belong to the category of relation (al-mudaf). The author refutes this by arguing that these things are not essentially relative; relation is accidental to them. For example, 'head' is a head of something, but the 'head-ness' is a general nature; the particular existence of knowledge is a psychic state stripped of matter, not inherently relative. Thus specific natures (al-naw'iyyat) are not relative in themselves; only the genus is predicated with respect to another. The author emphasizes that one thing can be in two categories accidentally but not essentially. Using the example of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its species grammar (al-nahw), he shows that grammar is not 'grammar of something' unless it is considered as knowledge; the object of grammar (parsing, al-i'rab) is only predicated with respect to grammar when it becomes known. Therefore, the relation is accidental. The passage concludes by urging the reader to reflect on examples like the head relative to the body. All examples adhere to the Islamic sciences tradition, using justice, health, knowledge, and grammar from the Islamic intellectual heritage.

العدالة
الصحة
المربع
المثلث

C) Because it is a quality

Key Takeaway

5 min
Relation is Accidental to Qualities Like Knowledge
Author refutes that knowledge is essentially relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue that knowledge and similar qualities are not essentially relative, using the author's distinction between essence and accident.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a quality by essence; relation is accidental.

An objector claims that knowledge belongs to the category of relation because it always relates to an object. The author refutes this by showing that relation is accidental to knowledge's essence. Let's analyze his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Someone says that things like disposition, power, and knowledge, which the author placed in quality, should be in relation because they always involve reference to another.
Look for: 'ثم لقائل أن يقول إن أكثر الأشياء التي أوردتموها في مقولة الكيف فذلك من مقولة المضاف'.
2
Find the author's rebuttal
The author says these things are not essentially relative; relation is accidental. Their quiddities are predicated in relation, but that does not make them from the category of relation.
He says: 'ليست بذواتها من مقولة المضاف بل ربما عرضت له الإضافة'.
3
Analyze the example of head
Head is head of something, but its head-ness is a general nature; the particular existence of a head is a quality. Relation attaches to the general nature, not the particular existence.
The example: 'الرأس من حيث هو رأس مضاف إلى البدن'.
4
Understand the distinction between essence and accident
One thing can be in two categories: one essentially, one accidentally. Knowledge is essentially a quality; relation is accidental to it.
The author says: 'الشيء الواحد لا يمتنع أن يكون داخلا في المقولين على وجهين في أحدهما بالذات وفي الأخرى بالعرض'.

بالمادة واعتبرت في الأشخاص لم يعتبر توسطها الحقيقي فإن ذلك غير مدرك إلا بالحد فيما قارن ذلك الحقيقي ولم يخرج إلى الطرفين خروجًا ظاهرا؛ فإنه يعد في الواسطة. ولذلك قد يكون عدالة زيد أشد من عدالة عمرو وإن كانت العدالة المطلقة ليست أشد من عدالة أخرى فإنها واحدة لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص. وكذلك حال الصحة التي هي توسط ما في المزاج وفي هيئة التركيب بين أمور زائدة وناقصة. وأما الأمور التي لا أضداد لها فإنها لا تقبل الأزيد والأنقص نحو المربع والمثلث وغير ذلك وإلا لكان التربيع مثلا يوجه في النقصان إلى ضد؛ وذلك محال فالتربيع بحقيقته لا يكون قابلا على خلاف السواد والبياض وغير ذلك. فإن ذلك بالحقيقة يكون قابلا ولا اعتبار للحسية في هذه الأمور. فإن المربع في الحس لا يلزم أن يكون مربعا في الحقيقة، وكذلك السواد والبياض ونحوهما من الأعراض.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إن أكثر الأشياء التي أوردتموها في مقولة الكيف فذلك من مقولة المضاف كالملكة والقوة والعلم وما أشبه ذلك لكنا نقول إن هذه الأشياء ليست بذواتها من مقولة المضاف بل ربما عرضت له الإضافة فإنه وإن كانت ماهياتها مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها فإنها لا يجب بذلك أن تكون من مقولة المضاف بل هي مفتقرة في كونها من المضاف إلى دعامة أخرى. فلا يقال مثلا إن هذا الرأس هو هذا الرأس لشيء بل يقال هذا الرأس رأس لشيء؛ فالإضافة إذن عارضة حتى إن التخصيص يتعرى عن موافقة تخصيص الإضافة بإزائه بل يلزمه من الإضافة ما يلزم الطبيعة العامة التي يلحقها الإضافة كالرأسية. وكان هذا التخصيص ليس من جهة ما لحقه من الإضافة بل من جهة الوجود الخاص كان كيفية ما تكون هيئة في النفس وصورة مجردة عن المواد؛ وذلك الوجود غير مضاف. فالنوعيات إذن ليست من المضاف في ذواتها وإنما المقول ماهية بالقياس هو الجنس الذي لأجله يقال كذلك للنوع، وكما أن للنوعيات وجود ليست هي به مضافًا فكذلك الجنس. وإن عرض له الإضافة فليس من المضاف بذاته فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون الجنس داخلا بذاته في مقولة والأنواع غير داخلة فيها؛ فتبين أنه لما لم يكن النوع من المضاف لم يكن الجنس من المضاف. فالشيء الواحد نحو العلم مثلا إذا كان مضافًا كانت الإضافة عارضة له لا أنه بذاته نوع من المضاف؛ فهو يدخل إذن في المضاف بالعرض. وإن كان ذلك العروض من اللوازم والشيء الواحد لا يمتنع أن يكون داخلا في المقولين على وجهين في أحدهما بالذات وفي الأخرى بالعرض. وإن كان يمتنع دخوله في المقولين بالذات لامتناع تقومه بهما من حيث هو هو. فلو كان العلم من المضاف الحقيقي لكان نوعه وهو النحو مثلا كذلك؛ والنحو ليس نحو الشيء إلا أن يوجد من حيث هو علم فيقال النحو علم بشيء. وعلى هذا في القوة وغيرها. ولا يقال إن حكم النحو حكم العلم فإن النحو نحو بالقياس إلى شيء هو إعراب اللغة؛ إذ الإعراب من حيث هو هو لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو فكيف يكون النحو مضافًا إليه لكن الإعراب مقول بالقياس إلى النحو من حيث هو معلوم حتى لو كان إعراب اللغة موجودًا ألف سنة من غير أن يكون معلوما لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو. فلما لحق به المعلوم صار معلوما فصار بإزائه هيئة نفسانية هي علم جملتها مقولة بالقياس إلى هذه الجملة؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن الرأس مثلا من حيث هو رأس مضاف إلى البدن من حيث هو ذو رأس.
By matter, and considered in individuals, their real intermediate is not considered, for that is not known except by definition in what accompanies that real [intermediate] and does not go out to the extremes in an apparent manner; for it is counted among the intermediate. Therefore, Zayd's justice may be more intense than Amr's justice, even though absolute justice is not more intense than another justice, for it is one and does not accept more and less. And likewise the case of health, which is an intermediate in the mixture and in the state of composition between excessive and deficient matters. As for things that have no opposites, they do not accept more and less, like square, triangle, and others; otherwise the square, for example, would be directed in deficiency toward an opposite, and that is impossible. So the square in its reality is not receptive [of more and less], unlike blackness, whiteness, and others. For those are in reality receptive, and sense perception is not considered in these matters. For the square in sensation is not necessarily a square in reality, and likewise blackness, whiteness, and similar accidents.
Then someone may say that most of the things you have adduced in the category of quality are from the category of relation, like disposition, power, knowledge, and the like. But we say that these things are not in themselves from the category of relation; rather, relation is accidental to them. For even if their quiddities are predicated in relation to others, it does not necessarily follow from that that they are from the category of relation; rather, they are in need, for their being relative, of another basis. For example, it is not said, 'This head is this head for something,' but, 'This head is a head for something.' Therefore, relation is accidental, so that the particularization is bare of agreement with the particularization of relation corresponding to it; rather, what follows from relation follows from the general nature that relation attaches to, such as head-ness. And this particularization is not by virtue of the relation that attaches to it, but by virtue of the particular existence, which was a quality that is a state in the soul and a form stripped of matter, and that existence is not relative. So the specific natures are not from the relation in themselves, but that which is predicated as a quiddity with respect to another is the genus for which the species is so called. And just as specific natures have an existence by which they are not relative, so too the genus. And if relation accidentally attaches to it, it is not from relation in itself, for it is impossible that the genus be inherently included in a category while the species are not included in it. Thus it is clear that since the species is not from relation, the genus is not from relation. So one and the same thing, like knowledge, if it is relative, relation is accidental to it, not that it is in itself a species of relation; so it enters into relation accidentally. And even if that accidentalness is from necessary concomitants, one thing is not prevented from being included in two categories in two ways: in one essentially, and in the other accidentally. But it is impossible for it to be included in two categories essentially, because it is impossible for it to be constituted by both in its very identity. For if knowledge were from real relation, then its species, like grammar, would be likewise. But grammar is not 'grammar of something' unless it exists insofar as it is knowledge, so grammar is said to be 'knowledge of something.' And similarly for power and others. And it is not said that the ruling of grammar is the ruling of knowledge, for grammar is 'grammar' with respect to something that is the parsing of language; since parsing in itself is not predicated with respect to grammar, so how can grammar be relative to it? But parsing is predicated with respect to grammar insofar as it is known, so even if the parsing of language existed for a thousand years without being known, it would not be predicated with respect to grammar. When knowledge attached to it, it became known, and thus there came to correspond to it a psychic state which is knowledge, the whole of which is predicated with respect to this whole. So consider what you have known: for example, the head insofar as it is head is relative to the body insofar as it is possessor of a head.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a critical expansion responding to an objection about the categories of quality and relation. The author begins by discussing intermediate states like justice and health, which admit of more and less, unlike geometric shapes such as squares and triangles that do not. This serves to distinguish qualities that vary in intensity from those that do not. Then an objector claims that many things the author placed in the category of quality, such as disposition, power, and knowledge, actually belong to the category of relation (al-mudaf). The author refutes this by arguing that these things are not essentially relative; relation is accidental to them. For example, 'head' is a head of something, but the 'head-ness' is a general nature; the particular existence of knowledge is a psychic state stripped of matter, not inherently relative. Thus specific natures (al-naw'iyyat) are not relative in themselves; only the genus is predicated with respect to another. The author emphasizes that one thing can be in two categories accidentally but not essentially. Using the example of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its species grammar (al-nahw), he shows that grammar is not 'grammar of something' unless it is considered as knowledge; the object of grammar (parsing, al-i'rab) is only predicated with respect to grammar when it becomes known. Therefore, the relation is accidental. The passage concludes by urging the reader to reflect on examples like the head relative to the body. All examples adhere to the Islamic sciences tradition, using justice, health, knowledge, and grammar from the Islamic intellectual heritage.

العلم
النحو
الإعراب
العرض

The Definition of Relation (al-Mudaf)
Author defines al-mudaf as that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of al-mudaf (the relative) and explain why the first formulation is preferred over the second.

Core Idea
The relative is defined by its quiddity being conceived in relation to another.

Among the ten categories, al-mudaf (relation) is unique because its very essence involves another thing. This passage presents two nearly identical definitions for the relative and explains why one is better. Understanding this subtle distinction will sharpen your ability to classify entities precisely—a skill essential for Islamic logic and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two formulations
Read the Arabic carefully and identify the two definitions given: (1) 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another' and (2) 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to the other'. Notice that the key difference is 'another' (ghayri) versus 'the other' (al-ghayr).
This is a common pattern in logical matn: variants are presented and then evaluated.
2
Analyze the difference
Consider what changes when you say 'another' (indefinite) versus 'the other' (definite with al-). The indefinite 'another' points to something else in general, while 'the other' may imply a specific other. The relative's quiddity is general relation, not relation to a particular entity.
Arabic grammar: al-ghayr = the other (specific), ghayr = another (non-specific).
3
Understand the author's preference
The author says the first is 'more appropriate' (awlā) 'according to meaning'. The reason is that the essence of a relative (like fatherhood) is to have a relation to something else in general, not to a specific person. The second formulation could be misinterpreted as requiring a definite other, which would be too narrow.
This preference reflects a common logical principle: definitions should capture the essential nature, not accidental specifics.

Section: On the Relative. As for the relative, it is that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another. And it is said that it is that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to the other. And the first is more appropriate because according to meaning...

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of the category of relation (al-mudaf). The author presents two formulations: (1) 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another' and (2) 'that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to the other.' He prefers the first because it captures the essential idea that the relative's very nature involves a reference to something else, not merely a particular other. The definition is foundational for understanding how things like fatherhood, knowledge, and equality are categorized. In the Islamic logical tradition, this definition is memorized as a standard matn passage.

Proof that Genus is not a True Relative
Author argues that genus cannot be a true relative because species is not relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that genus is not a true relative and identify the conditional inference (modus tollens) used.

Core Idea
Genus is not a true relative because species is not relative.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument about the status of genus. The author argues that genus cannot be a true relative (mudaf) because if it were, its species would also be true relatives. Since species are not true relatives, genus is not. This argument relies on a conditional inference and careful analysis of accidental versus essential relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find where the author asserts that genus is not a true relative. The conclusion is that genus is not from the category of true relatives.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The author implies: if genus were a true relative, then its species would also be true relatives. This is the core conditional.
This is not explicitly stated as a single sentence but is the logical backbone.
3
Find the negation of the consequent
The author argues that species (like grammar) are not true relatives; they are only accidentally relative. So the consequent 'species is a true relative' is false.
The example of grammar and parsing shows the accidental nature.
4
Apply modus tollens
Given 'if genus is a true relative then species is a true relative' and 'species is not a true relative', we conclude 'genus is not a true relative'. This is a valid modus tollens.
Modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
5
Understand the nuance
The author clarifies that relation can be accidental without making something a true relative. Genus may have accidental relation but is not essentially relative.
This prevents misinterpretation that all relation implies true relativity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في ذواتها وإنما المقول ماهية بالقياس هو الجنس الذي لأجله يقال كذلك للنوع، وكما أن للنوعيات وجود ليست هي به مضافًا فكذلك الجنس. وإن عرض له الإضافة فليس من المضاف بذاته فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون الجنس داخلا بذاته في مقولة والأنواع غير داخلة فيها؛ فتبين أنه لما لم يكن النوع من المضاف لم يكن الجنس من المضاف. فالشيء الواحد نحو العلم مثلا إذا كان مضافًا كانت الإضافة عارضة له لا أنه بذاته نوع من المضاف؛ فهو يدخل إذن في المضاف بالعرض. وإن كان ذلك العروض من اللوازم والشيء الواحد لا يمتنع أن يكون داخلا في المقولين على وجهين في أحدهما بالذات وفي الأخرى بالعرض. وإن كان يمتنع دخوله في المقولين بالذات لامتناع تقومه بهما من حيث هو هو. فلو كان العلم من المضاف الحقيقي لكان نوعه وهو النحو مثلا كذلك ؛ والنحو ليس نحو الشيء إلا أن يوجد من حيث هو علم فيقال النحو علم بشيء. وعلى هذا في القوة وغيرها. ولا يقال إن حكم النحو حكم العلم فإن النحو نحو بالقياس إلى شيء هو إعراب اللغة؛ إذ الإعراب من حيث هو هو لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو فكيف يكون النحو مضافًا إليه لكن الإعراب مقول بالقياس إلى النحو من حيث هو معلوم حتى لو كان إعراب اللغة موجودًا ألف سنة من غير أن يكون معلوما لم يكن مقولا بالقياس إلى النحو. فلما لحق به المعلوم صار معلوما فصار بإزائه هيئة نفسانية هي علم جملتها مقولة بالقياس إلى هذه الجملة؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن الرأس مثلا من حيث هو رأس مضاف إلى البدن من حيث هو ذو رأس.
In their essences, that which is said to be a quiddity by relation is the genus on account of which it is said of the species. And just as species have an existence not as relative, so too does genus. And if relation occurs to it, it is not a relative in itself, because it is impossible for genus to be in a category in itself while the species are not in it. So it is made clear that since species is not relative, genus is not relative. So one thing, such as knowledge for example, if it is relative, the relation is accidental to it, not that it is in itself a type of relative. So it enters into the relative accidentally. And if that accidentality is among necessary concomitants, one thing is not prevented from being in two categories in two ways: in one by essence and in the other by accident. Although it is prevented from being in two categories by essence because of the impossibility of its being constituted by them as such. So if knowledge were a true relative, its species—such as grammar—would be so. But grammar is not relative to something except when it exists as knowledge, so it is said 'grammar is knowledge of something.' And similarly for power and others. And it is not said that the judgment of grammar is the judgment of knowledge, because grammar is relative to something which is the parsing of language. Since parsing as such is not said in relation to grammar, how can grammar be relative to it? But parsing is said in relation to grammar insofar as it is known, such that even if parsing of language existed for a thousand years without being known, it would not be said in relation to grammar. When the known attaches to it, it becomes known, and there becomes in front of it a psychic disposition which is knowledge, the sum of which is said in relation to this sum. Consider what you have known, for the head for example, insofar as it is a head, is relative to the body insofar as it has a head.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that genus is not a true relative category. The author begins by restating a principle: what is said to be a quiddity by relation is the genus said of the species. Just as species have an existence not relative, so does genus. If relation occurs to genus, it is not a relative in itself, because it is impossible for genus to be in a category essentially while the species are not in that category. Thus, since species is not a true relative, genus is not either. The author then explains that a single thing, like knowledge, can have relation accidentally without being essentially a relative. Such a thing can be in two categories: one essentially and one accidentally. If knowledge were a true relative, its species (grammar) would also be a true relative. But grammar is not a relative except when it is considered as knowledge. The author uses the example of grammar and parsing (i'rab al-lugha). Parsing as such is not relative to grammar, but when it is known, it becomes relative. Thus, the relation is accidental. The conclusion is illustrated with the head-body relation: the head is a head by essence, but its relation to the body is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Genus is not a true relative; its relation to species is accidental, not essential, as shown by a modus tollens argument.

The Category of Relation: Essential vs Accidental
Author defines mudaf and distinguishes essential from accidental relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (true) and accidental relative using the criteria of quiddity.

Core Idea
The true relative has no quiddity other than relation; the accidental relative has an independent quiddity.

This passage from the logic text defines the category of relation (al-mudaf) and splits it into two types: those whose quiddity is essentially relative and those whose quiddity is independent but acquires relation accidentally. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper definition and classification in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-mudaf
Read the first lines: al-mudaf is that whose quiddity is conceived by relation to another. The author prefers the definition based on meaning (ma'na) rather than wording.
The first definition focuses on the meaning of relation, not just the term.
2
Extract the two divisions
Find where the author divides things common to the definition into two types: one with an independent quiddity (like head) and one with only a relational quiddity (like fatherhood).
The second type is called al-mudaf al-haqiqi (true relative).
3
Test the criterion: quiddity alone
For each example, ask: does this entity have any meaning or essence apart from its relation? For 'head', yes it is a specific body part. For 'fatherhood', no meaning except relation to sonship.
The true relative disappears if the relation is removed.
4
Check the author's refinement
Notice that the author also notes that even the first type (like head) can be considered relative when viewed from the attached relation, but in reality only the second type is truly relative.
The 'true relative' in reality has no subsistence in itself.
